The RED PAGE
To a Caricaturist
To force the private soul grow public by a face
Known ev’ry where, but ne’er before with all the
springs,
Bolts, keys, and catches, drop-locks, winding
wheels, and cogs
Revealed to vulgar gaze; that each man for himself
May see that quaint machine whereby false smiles
are made,
Rolled eyes and clockwork tears or countenances
screwed
To just abhorrence of an ostentatious vice.
How curious to mark for one fleet breathing space
That oil-soakt hinge which throws the fringed lid
of shame
(So well assumed) down to the earth when devils
talk—
All this dost thou, and more. . . . The world thy
bitter brain
Fears with an anxious hatred, for chaos travels
Eddying in thy wake. . . . Friends see in friends
their foes,
And open foes grow friends by contrast to their
friends.
O, whither shall they run? . . . Death cannot
rescue them
If thou, who e’en can penetrate the grave, were by.
The whited stone would blush to tell its common
lie.
HUGH McCRAE.
The American Novel
American novel, with its
1 fashion-plate heroine beaming at us
from the cover, its illustrations in
lordly color, its beautiful type, and its
immediate appeal, is nowadays the stand-
ing dish at all our libraries. Let us
adventure among the strident best-sellers
in quest of a work of art.
\t>
Out of the unromantic night, out of the
sombre blurring January fog, came a voice
lifted in song, a soprano, rich, full and
round, young, yet matured, sweet and mys-
terious as a night-bird’s, haunting and elusive
as the murmur of the sea in a shell. . . .
Hillard, genuinely astonished, lowered his
pipe and listened.
And the tired novel-reader, taking up
The Lure of the Mash, the latest best-
seller of Harold Macgrath (Bobbs, Mer-
rill, Hew York, per G. Robertson) knows
that Hillard will chase and capture and
marry that multi-adjectived voice. Books
of this kind are evidently written for the
jaded mind of the American business-
man, or the lush sentimentalism of the
American girl. You do not have to
think; the novelist does not venture on
character development or indeed on any-
thing except a mildly exciting plot. The
inveterate novel-reader can guess the end
of every chapter—which is subtly flatter-
ing to Harold Macgrath’s cloudy-minded
public. And Harold does pile it on !
Ah ! but her eyes were blue; blue as
spring-water in the morning, blue as the
summer sky seen through a cleft in the
mountains, blue as . . .
And, lest you should get puzzled and
drop the book, Harold lets you partially
into the secret behind the mask ; but he
is careful to keep the tricks of plot
pleasantly conventional and the denoue-
ments happily familiar. And, for the rest,
the reader who is about to visit Italy can
find in this much-padded story, written
by a cunning man for adult-children,
enough descriptive passages about that
Dago peninsula to save him the purchase
of a Baedecker.
The inevitable (American) Winston
Churchill is too wise to stray from the
broad track that leads to the best
seller, and his latest, Mr. C'reiue’s Career,
(Macmillan) keeps steadily in the middle
of the macadamised road. He conforms
to the well-worn formula of the popular
novel. He takes a man and a maid,
whom everybody but their stupid selves
knows will wed in the last page. He
places between them a villain named Cir-
cumstances, and not till the click has
gone for the dropping of the curtain does
he allow the hero to run the villain
through and clasp the maid to his
bosom in the limelight, to the
chime of wedding-bells heard “off.”
The Circumstances vary—but the most
useful recipe is to take a popular question
of the day, and sandwich the love-scenes
in between. So you hurry through the
Circumstances in order to reach the
kisses. Thus you are spared any anguish,
for you know that the hero will get the
heroine anyhow. Thus you know that
you are buying a happy ending—in
this book you are even buying an account
of the wedding, which is generous of
Winston. The Circumstances in this
case are the shady politics of one of
America’s United States, where the
machine runs the legislators, and the
railroad boss works the machine. It is
a pleasant tale, cynically told, in all
essentials the Average Popular Novel,
rather well done.
\»>
We handle sterner stuff in Holman
Day’s King Spruce (Harper, per Dymock’s
Library), a stirring tale of lumbering in
the Maine woods. Exciting things hap-
pen, and they happen all the time, and
most of the characters speak in staccato.
Here, for instance, is the gentle heroine
in casual chat with her millionaire father:
“You want me to marry that bland and
blessed son and heir. But I’ll not do any
such thing. . . . He isn’t the man I want for
a husband ; and when John Barrett’s only
daughter tells him that with all her heart and
soul, I don’t believe John Barrett is going
to argue the question or ask for further
reasons or give any orders.”
John Barrett isn’t. But it is only fair
to state that the things that happen in
the Maine forest demand the staccato.
The novelist has the excuse of abounding
incident if at times his tone is too forced,
if, as a whole, the book is written at too
shrill a pitch. The yarn is cleverly con-
structed, without loose ends, and robustly
handled, with psychological insight and
a varied assortment of clear-etched
characters. It gives a picture of Ameri-
can lumbering methods that is graphic,
and, with due allowances for the necessi-
ties of romance, possible and true. Most
interesting of his pictures is that of a
group of inter-bred and but half-human
wastrels, degenerates of Cretinism, who
dwell like outlaws in the forest depths.
He had thought there could be no worse
in human guise than those he had seen. But
this huge, hairy, shaggy, almost naked giant,
cowering against the side of a shack with all
the timidity of a child, marked a climax even
to such degeneracy as he had quailed before.
“Mind in him about five years old,
and will always stay five years old,” said
the guide, pointing to the wistful, suffering
face. “Body speaks for itself. Look at
them muscles ! I’ve seen him ploughin’,
hitched with their cow. ...”
“An animal ! ” Wade gasped.
“ Not much else. Afraid of the dark, of
shadows, and women, mostly. Once a
woman scared him in Castonia, and he ran
away like a hoss, draggin’ the jumper. Old
Jule hitched him to a post after that.”
\f/
And Holman Day writes with a
psychological truth rare in the popular
novel. Here is his situation of the man
who at last gets his enemy into his power
—and doesn’t know what to do with him.
“ It all goes to show that in this world most
men don’t do what they think they’ll do,
when it comes to a big matter. I don’t
want to kill you, now that I have you where
I want you. It would be like batting a
sheep to death. ... It seems funny to think
that way, after all the thinking I’ve done
about what I would do to you.”
And here is his matter-of-fact summa-
tion of a dramatic climax :
An imaginative man might have seen the
new spirit of the woods facing the old.
But there were no imaginative men there
—there were only men who chewed tobacco
and wondered what it all meant.
The main defect of the yarn is the
apposite manner in which the characters
turn up at the right dramatic time ; but
this, in a popular novel of incident, is
bound to happen. King Spruce, from this,
very fault, would make a fine drama.'
And the quaint “ It’ll-Get-Ye Club,”
composed of the men who have been
maimed and rendered useless by lumber-
ing accidents, with their humorously
pessimistic comment, would act as the
chorus of that drama. It is a virile yarn,
and will be widely read.
vl/
But in The Coast of Chance, by Esther and
Lucia Chamberlain (Bobbs, Merrill, per
N.S.W. Bookstall Co.), we come to the
end of our quest of the real American
novel. This is a delicate, subtle thing, a
strange gem exquisitely wrought, the
paradox of a detective story concerned
rather with character analysis than with
incident. Yet it is supremely exciting.
But in the method of this story there is
real cleverness, and in the character
studies we meet real people, subtly and
intimately drawn. The style is lucid and
exact, the manner delicately influenced
by the literary craft of the inevitable
Henry James. The book is so admirably
written, without an ounce of padding, that
it cannot well be judged by quotation.
The plot has to do with the theft of a cele-
brated ring ; but the people fit the plot,
and the novel is a piece of even texture
throughout. The characters are alive and
real, hinted at with apt phrase rather
than described. It is a popular novel
done with cunning craft; and the secret
of the plot is admirably kept. If this is
a first novel of the Chamberlain ladies,
they have caught the manner with a
rattier surprising aptitude. We expect,
after S. E. White and Jack London, to
find in America virile novels like King
Spruce; but this feminine and subtle
thing, The Coast of Chance, is an unex-
pected discovery among the raucous
horde of best sellers.
The Poet Elucidative
Somewhat pained, J. le Gay Brereton
furnishes some helpful clues :
With the appearance of J. le Gay
Brereton’s Sea and Sky comes a Red Page
review, kindly, feminine and graceful. The
critic has an eye for form, and, when she
praises, her praise is worth having. Beyond
the form she does not penetrate far. For
her Brereton’s “ message ” —is the term not
yet out of fashion ?—is “elusive ”; and since
it eludes her, she suspects it is non-existent—
“Brereton, having nothing to say, says it
charmingly.” In a sense, none of us has
anything new to say ; but if to the expres-
sion of old thoughts we give a personal tone,
the artistic result is new ; and this writer’s
note is his own, and not, as the critic
hints, Tennysonian. He says some things
which, though as ancient as truth, are yet
new in Australian poetry. Even in
theme he appears to have chipped
at some new ground. An English critic
has said of the section named Oithona, “I
know nothing in literature that deals with
the same theme,” namely, the antenatal ex-
istence of a child in a dream-world brought
into view by the desires of her parents, and
the influence of that child upon their emo-
tions. Throughout, Brereton transmutes
fact into dream, and he does it deliberately.
How else should he arrive at truth ?
\l )
At first glance, his book may appear a
fortuitous drift-pile of casual lyrics. On the
contrary, there is continuity and cohesion.
After a dedication in which he entrusts his
meaning to the elucidation of love, the
author, in a “Foreword,” gives the key to
his symbolism by explaining the title, Sea
and Sky. Then comes the body of the
book, divided into sections under significant
headings. In “Ideals” we have a warning
prelude and improvisations upon the twofold
theme—human love and the perception of
the divine. “ Shadow ” hints at a means of
escape from the coils of a cold materialism.
The shadow drifts by ; there are clouds that
melt, and there is sufficient thunder to clear
the air. Then, in orderly progress, we have
poems which proclaim, primarily, the delights
of courtship, nuptial love, and parentage.
But though there is no inclination to under-
value sensuous beauty and the “fierce unde-
niable attraction ” of sex, Brereton does not
“ wallow in a sensual sty.” Sex is a mystery
which he reveres, finding therein meanifig
beyond meaning. Phallic worship is no
obscene frenzy of the races which have
passed into flying dust, but a living creed.
There are still worshippers who hang garlands
about the honest garden god.
“ Through your eyes
I see an universe by love made whole ”
is no idle compliment.
if/
Surely, then, we may expect some reve-
lation—or at least some further sug-
gestion—of so stupendous and beatific a
vision. In its broad features it is
perhaps given least incompletely in
“The Silver Gull,” a celebration of a cos-
mic emotion and a vision of divine harmony
which it is impossible to describe in the
language of ordinary experience, though it
may be suggested with simplicity and clear-
ness by the use of poetic symbols. Here he
can only indicate very crudely the drift of
the poem. In his sympathy with the life of
earth-born creatures the poet identifies
himself with the bird of the sea,
whereby he sheds the wealth of the
world and wins the treasure of poverty,
which is freedom. In exaltation of spirit he
is borne from one plane to another on the
spreading wings of universal sympathy. He
is the world-enclosing air and all within it.
All vital energies are his, and, despite variety
of expression, all alike are good. Yet the
imagination can transcend all bounds, and at
last he attains identity with the spirit of joy,
“unbodied, supreme,” from which all else
emanates. Still he flies with the sea-bird.
The higher does not destroy, but transfigures
and intensifies the lower sympathy. It is
merely the dream of a drunken man ; but
then he is intoxicated with the wine of
Dionysus, of which perhaps you have heard.
Other lyrics treat of separate aspects of
the vision and its relation to our daily
business. [And here, regretfully, space-con-
siderations compel us to delete an equally ela-
borate explanation of “Twain.” However —]
The household fire of earthly affection
burns with a clearer flame because we have
caught a visionary gleam of the tire which is
divine.
Enough—here are clues. If they are not
worth following, so be it. The question was
not whether Brereton had anything worth
saying, but whether he was retailing the
stock ideas of the daily versifier. And can
it be true that he is £ ‘ too domestic for the
taste of the general reader ? ” Does the
general reader really prefer the desire of the
streets to the love of a Beatrice who not only
may bless his eyesight upon earth, but will
lead him in an ecstasy from sphere to shin-
ing sphere ?
J. LE GAY BRERETON.
So the reader can take his choice. He
can read either the book or its explana-
tion.
—♦
“ Hell ” is the most exciting item in Fire
o’ the Flame, the daintily produced fourth
number of The Heart of the Bose (Lothian,
Melb.). It is Bernard O’Dowd who gives us
“Hell.” From his guide-book of the
locality we learn a number of interesting
facts, evidently gained at first hand.
“From the visitors’ point of view,”
he blithely begins, “the most awful
thing about Hell is the inability of its
denizens to recognise anybody who speaks to
them. .. . Indeed, one gets to think
that the rescue of those in Hell (which
will have to be done, if the universe
itself is to finally endure) will be most
easily effected by the injection of some
great Name into the brain-cells of the
Lost, for if they can utter It, they are
thereby brought into such direct connection
with the uplifting forces of the universe
that it is only a matter of time for
them to think Hell away, and be again.”
Well .. . Bernard ought to know. The
rest of Flame o’ the Fire is pretty little
poems by Australian bards. Despite the
literary slightness of this flaming little
magazine, we are glad to note the possi-
bility of a publication so eesthetic in a con-
tinent so prosaic.
Australian Architecture (G. Robertson), by
Robt. J. Haddon, a Melb. architect, is a
technical manual not for the general reader.
But the book is valuable to the student
because of its special application to Aus-
tralian conditions. Haddon’s sane ideas may
be indicated by this panegyric upon a brick :
Take a brick : an ordinary brick is turned out
reasonably square, uniform in color, with a hard
resisting surface. How should such an article be
treated ? It is obviously unreasonable to seek to
make a cabinet-work finish of it. To smear it over
with added color, to tuckpoint it with more or less
mock joints is, therefore, to destroy its natural
quality. It is a brick and .... must look what it
should look, strong and sufficient in itself.
There is no doubt that architecture in
Australia has been too long a senseless copy
of a style of building suitable to a damper
and colder environment ; and Haddon’s book
is but one indication (Art and Architecture
is another) that this continent is beginning
to evolve an architecture to suit itself. Mean-
time, every brick is advised be a brick.
Australia To-day is offering a prize of £25 for
“ the best poem on ‘ The Defence of Australia,’ ”
and another prize of the same amount for “the
best popular prose essay on ‘ Social Life in Aus-
tralia, its Advantages and Outlook.’ ’’ The poem
must not exceed 1000 words, and the essay 7000
words, MS. to be typewritten, and signed by a
pen-name only, and accompanied by the usual
sealed envelope, containing name and address.
Date of closing 2nd November, at 190 Flinders-
street, Melb.

A WONDERFUL CURE
FOR INDIGESTION
REDFERN TESTIMONY.
“Each morning I woke with the consciousness
that something was not right,” remarked Mr.
William Johnston, of No. 127 Eveleigh Street,
Redfern, to a newspaper representative, who was
making some enquiries. Continuing he said : “I
felt as if I was in for a spell of sickness, and, sure
enough, those misgivings foretold what afterwards
happened. You see, the heat was pretty consider-
able where I was, up at Mole, seventy miles from
Warren, 115 deg. in the shade for a week at a
stretch being regarded as only a fleabite to what
could happen when the sun was in good form, and
too much of that sort of business gets on a man’s
nerves, and runs him down a bit, I tell you, and
I think I ought to know.”
“ Of course, because you are speaking from ex-
perience,” interrupted the scribe.
‘ < Exactly. There were a good few of us working
at an artesian bore, which ejected soda water —
without. I need scarcely remark, any whisky in it
—and too much soda straight is not good. In-
deed, it proved very bad for us, and as we never
got within reach of anything in the way of vege-
tables, it is only to be expected that the conse-
quences were very bad for the blood. Mine, like
the rest, was in a terrible state, sores breaking out
in all directions, and the flies were so troublesome
that we had to go away and sit in the dark to get
a little peace. The remedies I tried to put myself
in order with were of no avail, and my sufferings
became unbearable. When I sat down to a meal
I would get half way through when an attack of
retching always occurred, so no wonder my strength
fell off like it did. I became as weak as a kitten,
and, the work being laborious, I could not keep
going. Matters were about at their worst, when
the overseer went over to Bourke, and returned
with some Clements Tonic for the men, and I give
you my word as an honorable man, that after I had
taken but one bottle I felt a different being en-
tirely. The headaches that I had beforehand
were enough to destroy one’s happiness, without
anything else 5 but, besides, I had most of those
wretched symptoms which usually accompany in-
digestion. To begin with, my chest felt quite
clogged up, as though I had swallowed a lump of
dough that was clogging the breathing passages
and about my loins. I felt as if I had been beaten
with a stick—sore and aching. Again, under the
blades of my shoulders there were shap, pricking
sensations as if a needle were at work, and m
addition to all else, there was a perpetual feeling
of weariness as if I had run myself to death.
When getting out of bed of a morning, I would
have to hold on to it to steady myself, the dizzi-
ness being so great that I would have overbalanced,
and all the time I was going down-hill fast. My
weight was disappearing in a most alarming man-
ner, and I dread to think of what would have
happened had not Clements Tonic made its timely
appearance, through the kindness of our overseer.”
‘ ‘ He had probably used it himself on some pre-
vious occasion ? ” hazarded the writer.
“He said so, and swore by it. There is no med-
icine in the world to equal Clements Tonic, he
used to say, and, my word, he was right, for it
fixed us up splendidly. Its purifying effects on
mv blood cannot be too highly spoken of. Enough
to say that the sores soon disappeared, after which
I rested better. I began to. eat more freely and
regularly under Clements Tonic’s influence, and
the retching after meals became a thing of the
past. And if Clements Tonic cannot cure indi-
gestion quicker than anything else, I’m a Dutch-
man. It put me right out of touch with each
symptom belonging to that complaint in no time;
then it added to its wonderful work by steadying
my nerves, and giving me as much strength as a
man could wish for. Whenever I meet a person,
either man or woman, suffering in any particular
like I did myself, I just tell them what I know
Clements Tonic can do, as in my case, and if they
don’t go and get a bottle or two of that remedy,
then all I can say is, they deserve to suffer. Pub-
lish this any way you please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, WILLIAM JOHNSTON, of 127 Eveleigh Street,
Redfern, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth
of Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that
I have carefully read the annexed document, and that it
contains and is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements—which I
give voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I
make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the
Oaths Act, moo
Subscribed and declared uc Redfern, this twenty-
eighth day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and eight, before me,

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Erosion.
Ever to the high hill’s crest
Swept the winds from east and west,
Roared the gales from north and southward up
the mountain’s rugged sides ;
Winds from distant tracts and dunes ;
Winds from downs and hot lagoons ;
Winds from dark and desolation, where eternal
cold abides.
As of old they whined and wailed
As their restless blasts assailed
Beetling crag and riven boulder of the worn and
aged hill
That, in immemorial time,
Loomed a mighty mass sublime,
And, with rugged brows of granite, frowned on
its assailants still.
Never giant shall withstand
Beat of rain and sleet of sand,
Though his majesty be lifted, like a dome, against
the skies;
All the fretful winds that blow
Wreck and seek to lay him low,
Wear him through the endless ages, till supine at
last he lies.
He that loomed across the plains,
Lording over mountain-chains,
Crowned with snows against the sunrise, with his
head reared to the stars,
Shall in after ages shrink
Into nothingness, and sink
Down to levels of the waters and the silled river-
bars.
For against his dizzy crest
Sweep the winds from east and west;
Jealous of his exaltation chafe the old tormenting
throng;
Never giant shall withstand
Beat of rain and sleet of sand
At his stubborn bulk for ever, though he triumph
ages long.
vio.
C. G. A. COLLES.
Ye Citie Sabboth.
A chaunt of prayse, upsoaring to ye skies,
Proclaymes ye earlie birde hath gayned hys
queste;
And that within hys little bellie lies
Ye thankless squirmer, slowelie to dygeste.
Now Mistresse Sparrowe, fussing round her
neste,
Prepareth her for her matutinal laie.
Whereat ye gentles with mute praier proteste,
And eke ye Thomas-catte doth slynk awaye
From an illicit tryste, or some wylde furrie fraie.
Now winged couriers, shod with hobnayles flete,
Lyke birde-lymed Mercuries ; and as they tear
Ye pavement’s laollowe sylences repeat,
Lyke drums ye tic-tac messages they beare,
Eerilie lanthornes swyng, and sykly stayre,
As necke and neclce, brasse-throated herauldes
runne
Toward still glaydes of farre Suburbia, where
Ye drowsie mayde hath (’spyte ye gong) begun
Fresh dreames. Eftzoones will shouttes an-
nounce ye rysen Sunne
Coarse snatches bring ye worthy cit a qualme,
Ere with “Adieus !” yeroysterer homeward reels
Then over all ther sinkes a holie calme,
As amidst ye dingie cliffs a softe lighte steals,
And—Gramercy ! ye thousand tongue-powered
peales
Ry ve ye grey welkyn ;—from their sonaunce
cleare
Ye weird chromatique moans and grumbling
wheels
Take ye glad theme, as lyke a hunted sphere
Ye all-nyght carre spedes headlong on its lone
career.
Whylome ye cabbie scannes ye house, and growles,
As dreames hys moke of corne in poze devoute.
With sloggy stryde ye moderne Charlie prowles
Lyke a lost shayde deserted laynes about;
Or warnes ye antient scolde in shabbie cloute.
A charyot clatters to a doore, whence peepe
A slatterne head, and jugg without a spoute
Steak blisters now, whyle dames ye thresholde
sweepe.
Forsooth ! in spite of thilke ye laggard lodgers
sleepe.
R. W. TAYLOR.
The Trees in Collins-street.
The trees—the trees in Gollins-street
Are showing spots of tender green;
Shyly the New Year trappings sweet
Cling to the limbs of each wood-queen,
Telling the news that Spring is come
To us who pass on restless feet,
With eyes downcast and senses numb,
Beneath the trees of Collins-street.
The trees —the trees in Collins-street
Make colonnades of spreading shade,
Where She and I may sometimes meet,
Whilst branches by the wind are swayed.
The dappled shadows that they fling
Play on her gown, whilst I repeat
The joyous message of the Spring
Brought by the trees in Collins-street.
The trees —the trees in Collins-street
Are bowls of gold when April’s nigh,
And Nature with a gay conceit
Displays her wealth. The misers sigh
And wander thoughtfully to town.
At night the blue electrics beat
On many a radiant golden crown
High on the trees of Collins-street.
The trees —the trees of Collins-street,
Clad in their Springtime dress of green !
Oh ! slowly go our wearied feet
And a dear silence falls between ;
When the grey city lies behind
And we but faintly hear the beat
Of its great heart, sweet peace we find
Beneath the trees of Collins-street.
VIC.
G.B.D.

COLD CHARITY.
In order to mitigate the serious unemployed trouble in Great Britain the Admiralty ordered five new cruisers to be constructed by private shipbuilding firms. The
authorities also invited tenders for 14 large ocean-going destroyers at an earlier period, than they otherwise would have done, in order to give work to the men who were idle.
John Bull gives his famishing unemployed a cup of water, while his protected neighbor helps himself by the bucketful.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 29, 1908.
WHAT IS A SEAT OF GOVERNMENT?
The Seat of Government of the Commonwealth shall be
determined by The Parliament, and shall be within terri-
tory which shall have been granted to or acquired by the
Commonwealth, and shall be vested in and belong to the
Commonwealth, and if New South Wales bean Original
State shall be in that State, and bf, distant not dess than
ONF. HUNDRED SULKS FROM SYDNEY.— CICLUSe 125 Of the
Constitution.
The ballot of the House of Representatives re the
Federal capital site has created a difficult problem,
which was almost overlooked at first; it only rose
like a troubled ghost within the last few days. It
involves the settlement of the legal question,
“What is a Seat of Government”? and that
appears to be only a new and worse version of
the dreadful old conundrum, “What is a Place?
—a conundrum which has wrecked more legal front
teeth than any other of the day. In this instance
the craving to drag everything right up against
Sydney’s back door has been carried as far as can
be done with safety, and possibly a little farther.
Some ten years ago, by a disreputable trick, all
Queensland, Westralia, South Australia, the
Northern Territory, Tasmania and Victoria were
disqualified as possible Seats of Federal Govern-
ment. The N.S.W. State Legislature, at the in-
stigation of the Reid Ministry, demanded
that these places should all be set down as unfit
or unclean or accursed, or words to that effect.
But by way of some slight concession the said
State Legislature consented that Sydney and an
area 100 miles wide around Sydney should also be
disqualified. The Legislature at Sydney de-
manded the complete disqualification of Mel-
bourne and 87,884 square miles attached thereto ;
of Brisbane and the 608,497 square miles of ad-
jacent country ; of Adelaide and the 903,690
square miles ruled from that city ; of Perth with
975,920 square miles of land adjoining; and of
Hobart and the 26,215 square miles of terri-
tory of which it is the political centre.
This looked so like an attempt to dis-
qualify every place except Sydney, and to
regard Federation solely as a means of bringing
wealth to Sydney, that by way of a self-denying
ordinance the N.S.W. capital and 100 miles of
adjacent land were likewise barred out. It was a
most unsatisfactory compromise, but when the
N.S.W. Legislature annulled the popular accept-
ance;of the original Constitution, something had
to be done in the way of compromise.
* * * *
Now, in the intense desire to drag everything
as close as possible to Sydney, the N.S.W. section
of the House of Representatives has secured a
vote in favor of a Federal capital territory which
is to straddle right across the 100-mile line. The
votes which made a majority for the Yass-Can-
berra site were obtained by large general assur-
ances about a Federal port at Jervis Bay and
other things. The Sydney daily papers were
eloquent about the great superiority of Jervis Bay
as compared with Twofold Bay, the sea-outlet for
a capital on Monaro. They and the State Govern-
ment were both large and vague in their assur-
ances about the grant by the State of a liberal
area of land if the Australian capital were planted
at Yass-Canberra, and about an allowance of land
whereon to build a railway to Jervis Bay, and also
concerning a seaport there. If these promises are
carried out, the arrangement will necessitate the
Federal territory being a long, attenuated strip,
of which one end will be some miles outside the
100-miles limit from Sydney and the other end 13
miles inside that limit. And whether this arrange-
ment is legal or not will depend partly on the
settlement of the question : “What is a Seat of
Government?” and partly on the settlement of
another question.
If a Seat of Government is simply the building
in which Federal laws are made, then it is obvious
that the Yassberra-Jervis Bay selection would
be legal enough. In that case, in fact, it
probably wouldn’t matter if the Parliamentary
refreshment room was over the line, so long as
Parliament House itself wasn’t. If the Seat
means Parliament House and the Government
buildings in which is done the official Work
without which there would be no Govern-
ment at all, and without which Parliament
would be a mere abstract expression of opin-
ion, then also the Yassberra-Jervis Bay site
would be legal, provided care were taken
that no outlying official buildings slopped over
the line, and provided the Governor-General, who
is a necessary item of the Government and of the
Seat thereof,' didn’t want to live where he could
have a sea-view, and sea-baths on summer morn-
ings, and where he could run naked on the beach.
If the whole Federal city is the Seat of Govern-
ment, then all would still, apparently, be well, if
sufficient care were taken to put the city at the
legal end of the Federal territory. But if the Seat
of Government is the territory which is granted or
acquired for purposes of Federal Government then
the ’Berra-Jervis Bay site is wholly illegal, and in
the unscrupulous struggle to drag everything to
Sydney things have been dragged too far. It is
not merely a question of a capital; the term
“ capital’’"might be legally definable, or again it
might not. But a Seat of Government is a large,
vague problem and a new expression. It suggests
the whole area that the Government sits on, and
the Government may be held to sit officially
on the whole territory which is allotted to
it for governing purposes; else why was
the territory allotted ? The Seat can’t be
the mere area that the Government sits
on literally or in the plain, fleshy sense, for
then whenever the Government stood up there
would be no area at all, and a Government that
was standing up would have no Seat so long as it
stood.
The statement that the Seat of Government
is to be “within territory which shall have been
granted to or acquired by the Commonwealth ”
doesn’t help the matter. If the clause stated
that the Seat of Government should be in the
middle of the territory, and that round it there
should be a wide fringe which wasn’t Seat, the
matter would be fairly clear, but nobody antici-
pated, or provided for, the frantic greedy struggle
that has been made to balance the Federal City
right on the thin edge of Sydney in order to grab
all the trade, so there is no such definition. The
word “within” throws no light on the subject.
A snail is within its shell, but the contents of the
shell are presumably all snail. A ballet-girl is
within her tights, but unless she is padded the
contents of the tights are all girl. A man may be
within his rights, but it doesn’t follow that there
is room for another man within the same rights.
Also, seven devils may be within a man, but that
doesn’t show that there is room for another devil.
In fact, the man may be packed so tightly that if
he tried to hold another devil he would burst. And
in like manner, though the Seat of Government
is within the Federal territory, that doesn’t demon-
strate that the contents of the Federal Territory
aren’t all Seat, and if they are all Seat then the
promise of a Federal port at Jervis Bay was
almost certainly a reckless lie and an obtaining of
votes by false pretences. The State Rights press,
which desires above all things a mean little Aus-
tralian metropolis that will be completely over-
shadowed by the State capitals, of course brushes
this question aside in an off-hand manner. It is
quite natural that it should do so, for if a Federal
city came to be planted at Yassberra because of
the promise of a sea-outlet at Jervis Bay, and
because of the consequent chance of commercial
and industrial expansion, and that sea-outlet was
afterwards cut off by legal decision, no one would
be gladder than the Tory and State Rights
press. If any false promise could fix
the capital where it would ultimately become
a nlere appanage or mean suburb of a big State
city, the State Rights press would probably regard
the false promise as a good investment. It has
bitterly opposed the idea of a federal port, and it
it could, by a wholly unreliable assurance that
there would be a Federal port, bring the capital
where, in the end, there would be no Federal
port, it would have achieved its purpose. On the
other hand, to put the capital where it would have
a Federal port, about its title to which there was
no doubt whatever, would be contrary to the whole
State Rights policy. The confidence man who
sells a gold brick always wants to be sure it is a
brick, and if he sells real gold by mistake he grows
wild. So the State Rights press declares airily that
the Australian Government can just as well own
a port inside the Sydney 100-miles limit as
it can own a Custom-house at Sydney or a
bit of ground for an office at Parramatta.
The unfortunate difficulty is that, if the Aus-
tralian Government wanted a Customs house at
Svdnev or a bit of land at Parramatta for Seat of
Government purposes, it couldn't own any such
thing, because, just like Jervis Bay, these places
are within the forbidden 100-miles radius.
* * * *
Furthermore, it would require very deep thought
to decide whether Clause 125 means that the Seat
of Government (which may be only a part of the
Federal territory, or which, again, may be the
whole Federal territory) is to be not less than 100
miles from Sydney, or that the entire Federal
territory, apart from any question of the Seat of
Government, is to be not less than 100 miles from
Sydney. Let the clause be considered again in
all its uncertainty :
The Seat of Government of the Commonwealth shall
be determined by the Parliament, and shall be within
territory . , ,
WHICH shall have been granted to or acquired by the
Commonwealth, and shall he vested in and belong to the
Commonwealth, and if New South Wales be an Original
State shall be in that State and be distant not less than
100 miles from Sydney.
The ‘‘which” plainly refers, not to the Seat of
Government, whatever that may mean, but to the
whole Federal territory. And if the “which”
continues to refer to the whole territory—as it
seems to do, seeing that no other “which” turns
up, then the whole Federal territory wherein the
Seat of Government is located must be at least
100 miles from Sydney. And in that case the
acquisition of votes for a Yassberra site on the
ground that it was to include a Federal port at
Jervis Bay, 13 miles inside the 100-mile limit, was
pure confidence trick, and was own brother to the
rich uncle in Fiji.
By wav of a side issue, the people who want to
strain the spirit and letter of the desperately hard
bargain which their Shylocic Government drove
with Australia rely on Clause 111 of the Consti-
tution, which says :
The Parliament of a State may surrender any part of
the State to the Commonwealth, and upon such surrender
and the acceptance thereof by the Commonwealth, such
part shall become subject to the exclusive jurisdiction of
the Commonwealth.
On these grounds apparently N.S.W. can cede
to the Commonwealth Jervis Bay, or part of it, or
the bit of land leading to it, or any other place
inside the 100-miles radius of Sydney ; and so long
as the bit of ceded territory isn’t used for Seat of
Government purposes, and isn’t part of the
Federal territory ceded for Seat of Government
purposes, all will be well. But if it is used for
Seat of Government purposes, or is part of the
territory ceded for Seat of Government purposes,
all will be very far from well. In fact, all will be
sick and darned bad. Now the whole proposal of
the Jervis Bay cession is bound up with the
question of the Seat of Government. The scrap
of Bay which the grudging Wade proposes to
transfer to the Commonwealth is to be joined on
to the Seat of Government territory, and to be
part of it; and its cession has been made almost a
condition of the selection of the Yassberra capital
site. And even if, after much consideration of
the “ which ” in Clause 125, it is decided that it is
the Seat of Government (whatever that may be),
and not the whole Federal territory, that is to be
at least 100 miles from Sydney, the fact remains
that unless that Seat can be defined, and nailed
down at the legal end of the Federal territory,
and rigidly prevented from slopping over into the
doubtful end, the promised port at Jervis Bay is
liable to be snatched away from the Common-
wealth just as soon as the expenditure of much
good Australian money has made it worth snatch-
ing, and as soon as some enthusiastic anti-
Federalist feels like hiring a barristerial snatcher
to raise the point.
It is all a mean, miserable, sordid, grudging,
ungenerous process of grabbing at the last copper
whether it is an ill-gotten and dubious copper or
not. It was the N.S.W. Government which
asked the other States to join in a Federation.
When the N.S.W. people accepted the original
Constitution, which left the location of the capital
an open question, and which might ultimately have
resulted in Sydney being the chosen metropolis
of Australia," the N.S.W. Legislature nullified
their decision, and demanded that all the States
which it had invited to join it should be dis-
qualified as possible sites for the capital. The
other States assented, and in return it was pro-
mised that the Seat of Government, or the
Federal territory containing the Seat of Govern-
ment, though ” in N.S.W., was not to be
within 100 miles of Sydney. On top of
that came the new demand that the N.S.W.
Parliament should have a right of veto
as regards the site for the capital of all Australia,
even though the place selected might fully con-
form to the spirit and letter of the Constitution.
Also there rose the further demand that it should
decide the area of the Federal territory, that it
should control the Federal city’s water-supply,
and even that the Federal city should be so situ-
ated that all its trade should go to Sydney.
Finally, having secured the selection, so far as the
House of Representatives is concerned, of a site
at Sydney’s back-door, by vague and loose assur-
ances about a liberal grant of land and the grant
of a sea-outlet, the liberal grant of land proves, if
it is possible to judge by Premier Wade’s hints,
to be an area smaller than that occupied by
Brisbane and its suburbs, and the port shows
signs of shrinking to a mere bit of space for a
wharf ; and the title to even that scrap of beach
seems distinctly uncertain, because the port will lie
13 miles nearer to Sydney than the Constitution
allows. In fact, a whole lot of the promised ter-
ritory will be inside the forbidden limit and will
be held by a shaky title. It is a poor, shabby,
police-court-lawyer affair from first to last—a
business worthy of what the lucid Yankee slang
calls a “shyster,” the same being a term which
describes the down-at-heel practitioner who car-
ries his documents in his hat, and rises to address
his Worship on a profound technical point bear-
ing on a half-crown case about a dog. And all
this row and all this straining of the Constitution
are done in order that more population may be
congested in what is already the most congested
part of Australia. The county of Bourke, in which
Melbourne stands, had, at last census, 304 people
to every square mile, and considering that the
Victorian average was less than 14 people to the
square mile this was a shameful affair. But the
County of Cumberland, in which Sydney stands,
had at the same date 3(32 people to the square
mile, and considering that the average in iN.S. W.
was less than persons to the square mile that
was at least a three-times worse case of congestion
than even the Victorian instance. The clamorous
desire to place the capital of Australia where its
trade, by going entirely to Sydney, will swell the
most aggravated human congestion in this con-
tinent to something worse even than it is, is a
sorrowful business.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Private Landlord and the Small Federal
Territory.
There is a profound method in the apparent mad-
ness of those people who advocate tne restriction
of the Federal capital territory to a mere scrap of
100 or 200 square miles. It is a cash method, and
the madness has a solid financial basis. The
Federal city, even if it is planted in such an un-
favorable spot that it can’t become a very large
city, will necessarily, because of its official popula-
tion alone, be a place of some importance. Its
establishment will create a great increase in land
values—an increase almost certainly far in excess
of the monev spent V)}' the Commonwealth on the
city itself. " That increase will spread for a
considerable distance around the city. If the
Federal capital stands somewhere towards the
centre of a Federal area of, say, 2000 or
5000 square miles, the increase in land values
will probably spread over most of the
Federal territory; if the city stands on a
Federal area of, say, 150 or 200 square miles, the
profit created will spread far beyond its own terri-
tory, and much of it will be mopped up by the
adjacent State and the landlords thereof. That
is to say, the wealth created by the expenditure
of the money of all Australia will largely go to the
landlords of one State of Australia. N.S.W.
stands, in the matter of population, for about two-
fifths of Australia. If it transfers 1000 square
miles of country to the Commonwealth it will
still liave a two-fifths interest—equal to 400
square miles—in the transferred property,
so its actual sacrifice will be 600 square
miles. If it transfers only 200 square miles its
interest in the transferred property will be equal
to 80 square miles, so its sacrifice will be only 120
square miles. In no case is the sacrifice anything
like so large as it looks. In the construction of
the public works of the new capital (streets,
pavements, drains, buildings, etc.) let it be
assumed that the Commonwealth will spend
£s,ooo,ooo—one sum is as good for purposes of cal-
culation as another—in which case N.S.W.’s share
of the outlay will be £2,000,000 ; also, its interest
in the Federal property will be worth £2,000,000.
If Private Enterprise” spends an equal sum on
houses, warehouses, gardens, shops, fences, etc.,
and if the value of improvements bears the same
proportion to the land values which the improve-
ments create as they do in Sydney, the expendi-
ture of that £10,000,000 (of which N.S.W. will
have contributed in a taxpaying sense £2,000,000)
will create £8,000,000 of land values, of which
N.S.W.’s undivided share as a partner in the
Commonwealth, if the whole of these new land
values are within Federal territory, will be
£3,200,000. That is to say, on this basis N.S.W.
will pay £2,000,000 towards the construction of an
Australian capital, and the value of its interest
in the capital and in the land values
created bv the building of the capital
will be £5.200,000. It will share on a
population basis, exactly the same as the
other States, and it will have a special advantage,
inasmuch as the establishment of the capital in
N.S.W. will bring much profitable business to the
N.S.W. railways, while to those of Queensland,
Tasmania, S. A.’, and Westralia it will bring none.
But if the Federal territory can be cut so small
that a great part of the land to which its estab-
ment must give a new value will lie outside the
Federal territory, then it is quite possible that
out of the £8,000,000 of land values created by the
expenditure of the money of all Australia
£3,000,000 will lie over the border in N.S.W. In
that case Premier Wade S State taxpayer will
have contributed £2,00(1,000 towards the construc-
tion of a capital, and will have as a sec-off : (1) An
interest worth £2,000,000 in the Federal capital;
(2) an interest worth £2,000,000 in the land values
(total value £5,000,000) which the expenditure of
public and private money will have created inside
<he Federal territory; (3) £3,000,000 worth of
new land values in the adjacent parts of N.S.W.
created by the expenditure of the money of all
Australia on the Federal capital and territory ;
and (4) most of the railway profits (value un-
known) of the business created by the new city.
That shows a fairly large gain on the transaction.
As a contra , N.S.W. will have given up its State
sovereignty over a bit of land which, under
present circumstances, is a dead loss, seeing that
it doesn’t yield enough revenue to pay for roads,
bridges, police and sundries. The only tangible
asset N.S.W. is to give away is the State land
within the ceded territory, but then there is none
that is of any real account in a revenue-producing
sense. For all private lands in the ceded area the
Commonwealth is to pay. The demand for a
miserable, cramped little Federal territory looks
like a good move in the interests of the State’s
private landlord, but Australia isn’t bound to
make sacrifices for the benefit of any private
landlord.
♦
The Farmer and the Freehold.
The debate on the N.S.W. Lands Bill gave the
remnant of the old Tory party in the Assembly,
and the members who are not openly Tory but
would be so if they dared, a chance to trot out the
old threadbare assertion that the farmer with only
a State lease, even if it be a 999 years’ lease, will
fail, for sentimental reasons, to take the same in-
terest in his work as the man with a freehold.
This is supposed to be one of the high patriotic
reasons why the N.S.W. Government is going to
give the State leaseholder the right, inalarge, whole-
sale fashion, to buy his land right out. Another
and much less perishable-looking reason is that the
Wade Government has got into such a stupendous
spending habit that, even with a revenue that is
miles ahead of anything any previous State
Government ever enjoyed, it sees scarcity ahead ,
so it proposes to adopt the easy, thriftless method of
wholesale, yet surreptitious, land-selling. If a State
leaseholder is paying £SO a year rent, and he is
allowed to buy his land right out for £2ooo—well,
the present Government will enjoy the ini a
lump, and some future one will feel the loss of the
£SO a year, and it will be all right, for the present
Government, anyhow. That looks a simple and
obvious explanation, while the tale about how the
State’s tenant farmer is discouraged for Jack of
the freehold, and so fails to put his whole soul and
shovel into?fhe business, looks a simple and ob-
vious lie. If that discouraged farmer was the real
trouble, the first case to be dealt with would not


be that of the State tenant, who, as a rule, has a
low rent and an easy landlord, but that of the
often rack-rented tenant of the private land-
holder ; and the first anxiety would be, not to
give the State tenant a right of com-
pulsory purchase on easy terms, but to
give the right of compulsory purchase on easy
terms to the miserable private tenants around
Picton, Campbelltown, Bathurst, Berry, Tam-
worth—in fact, to every man who rents his bit of
land from the private owner. There is quite a lot
of that kind of man. A very few years ago the
area occupied by tenant farmers in the district
around Sydney was set down at 200,683 acres ; in
the Lower Hunter, 642,474 acres; South Coast,
460,300 acres ; North Coast, 381,594 acres : Cen-
tral Tableland, 640,652 acres; Southern Table-
land, 628,184 acres. Therefore, in the mere scrap
of N.S.W. that is convenient to Sydney there
were, six or seven years ago, nearly 20,000 tenant
farmers pining for the freehold on nearly 3,000,000
acres of privately-owned land. Surely the beauti-
fully sympathetic frauds who weep so much about
the sufferings of the lightly-rented State tenant,
and about his craving for the freeehold, and about
the necessity of giving him the right to purchase
his holding from the owner at a low fixed price,
might think of these heavily rented 20,000 farmers,
and the need to give them the right of compulsory
purchase at a low, fixed price from the Antills
and Hays and Macarthur Onslows and their
tribe. The spectacle of certain big landlords,
whose servile and hat-touching tenantry turn
themselves into beasts of burden and drag the
squire’s carriage when he comes home after spend-
ing the rents in Europe, and whose serfs train one
swift boy in every family to run like the Devil and
open gates when the great man is seen coming—
the spectacle of landlords like these urging that the
farmer should have the freehold of his own farm
is calculated to create a heaving sensation in the
stomach region.
That “Generous Area.”
The large and generous-sounding announcement
made by N.S.W. State Premier Wade that he
would act liberally in the way of a grant of land
for a Federal capital if a site in the Canberra
neighborhood was adopted seems to have now
boiled down into a definite offer of “ between 100
and 200 square miles.” Even if this means the
whole 200 square miles, the area will only com-
pare with some existing cities as follows :
Acres.
Brisbane and suburbs 195,200
Melbourne ~ ~ 163,480
Federal City,, ~ 128,000
Adelaide ~ ~ .. .. .. 99,358
Sydney ~ ~ 93,094
That doesn’t look like any very gorgeous liber-
ality, and in point of fact the liberality isn’t even
a tenth so gorgeous as it looks. The State cities
find it necessary to have huge areas outside
their municipal boundaries for water catch-
ments, water channels, reservoirs, outlets for
drainage, sewage farms and the like. Sydney,
and Melbourne, for instance, would never dream of
allowing their water supply to be controlled even
by adjacent municipalities in whose territory the
water is collected, much less would they dream of
allowing control by adjacent States. Certainly
Sydney and Melbourne have enough influence in
their respective State Parliaments to make it
reasonably certain that they could make adjacent
municipalities keep the city water supply clean, but
even that isn’t regarded as sufficient. The Sydney
Board of Water Supply and Sewerage and the
Melbourne Board of Works were created in order
that Australia’s two big cities should have abso-
lute control over their water and sewerage sys-
tems. The same rule applies to the Brisbane
Board of Water Supply. But Premier Wade
holds that a system of divided or outside control,
which would be regarded as an absurdity in a pro-
vincial city, is quite good enough for the Austra-
lian capital. Sydney wouldn’t trust Parramatta,
or Liverpool, or Campbelltown to do the
fair thing by it in the matter of water
supply, though Sydney’s vote in the State
Assembly is quite large enough to suppress any ob-
jectionable proceedings on the part of Parramatta
or Liverpool or Campbelltown ; yet certain State
Fright politicians profess themselves quite unable
to understand why the capital of all Australia
shouldn’t be willing to depend on the State Legis-
lature for its water, the same to be clean or dirty,
scant or abundant, as the State authority feels in-
clined, though the said capital won’t have a single
vote in that State Legislature, and will have no
more direct legal control over its deeds or mis-
deeds in this line than if it were a foreign institu-
tion. It is a curious example of inability to put
oneself in the other fellow’s place, or of a violent
resolve not to consider the other fellow’s point of
view, or even to allow that he is entitled to possess
a point of view.

The Angels and the Decayed Meat.
The failure of George Reid’s motion of censure
in the House of Reps, was not a mere defeat —it
was a rout. Reid fled in wild disorder, leaving
behind him his baggage and his disorganised
forces, and saving only his Dry Dog and his eye-
glass out of the wreck. Since then the one or two
papers which still cling to Reid’s fallen fortunes
have tried to find satisfaction in pointing out how
iniquitous it was of the Forrest-Knox-Irvine
party not to stand' by Reid, and how degraded it
was for the Labor party to stand by Deakin, and
how degraded it was of Deakin to be thus stood
by, and how, in short, the House was divided into
two sections, the angels and the decayed meat, and
Reid’s slim minority was the angels, and the de-
cayed meat was everybody else. But the plain and
obvious reason for Reid’s failure was the utter
fatuousness of his case. In a general way, the
malcontents complained :
(1) That the Deakin Government raises far too much
revenue, and thus grievously oppresses the people.
(2) That it ought to return even more money than it
does to the State Governments (to do this it would have
to raise more revenue).
(3) That it returns too much to the State Governments,
and thus leads them into extravagant habits.
(4) That it proposes to return less to the State Govern-
ments, in order to find money for Defence, Old Age Pen-
sions, the taking over of the Northern Territory, etc.
(5) That all these things are urgent and necessary, but
that they shouldn’t be done with money “ taken from the
States.” A strange, mad idea rages in certain quarters
that the Australian Government should raise the neces-
sary funds by taxing Egypt, or Bolivia, or some such
place.
(6) That the Deakin Government is extravagant.
(7) That in its shameful and cheese-paring economy it
has starved the postal and other services into a condition
of utter inefficiency.
(7) That the fact that the Deakin Government has been
able, through this excessive economy, to pay the State
Governments, temporarily, very large sums over and
above the three-fourths secured to them by the Braddon
clause, gives the States a claim to equally large, if not
larger, sums in the future. At the same time, it is the
duty of the Deakin Government to find a huge sum of
money wherewith to put the Post-office in order, and at
the same time it ought to reduce taxation.
(8) That the proposed beginnings of the Defence
scheme are so small as to be ridiculous. They show an
absurd craze for economy. Also, they cost too much
money. Universal military training should be accom-
panied by universal payment. Also, the compulsory ser-
vice should be voluntary. At the same time, the cost
should be reduced—also increased. Further, the naval
subsidy to Britain should be greatly enlarged, and there
should be reductions in expenditure. What is wanted is
less outlay and more liberality, also not so much money
and more of it.
(7) That the Northern Territory should be taken over
at once without costing anybody anything. A good
transcontinental railway should be built instantly—
gratis. This railway should take the most direct track
through Victoria, N.S.W., Queensland, South Australia,
and the Northern Territory, so as to provide bee-line
communication between Melbourne, Adelaide, Port
Augusta, Sydney, Brisbane, Townsville, Port Darwin and
other places. It should go round by Kalgoorlie in a
straight line, and pass through the electorates of Bunt
and Wade and Fuller and Johnson on the way, and
traverse the property of “ Wingbn ” Abbott.
(8) That the Deakin Government should at once take
over the State debts on lines that will satisfy all the State
Premiers, and that it should get on faster with public
business, and not attempt to stifle freedom of debate.
There were other items also, and the whole affair
made a very large indictment, only the indictment
wouldn’t hang together. The men who said the
Deakin Ministry was too long wouldn’t vote with
those who said it was too short, and those who
said it was too broad wouldn’t vote with those
who said it was too narrow, and the men who de-
clared it was too thick preferred to support it
rather than join with those who said it was too
thin. So the attack went to pieces almost before
it was started, and covered the originators of the
movement with confusion.

The Country Toiler’s Wage.
It is a cold day indeed when the Pastoralists’
Review cannot raise a lament about the shortage
of country labor. In its September issue, the
poor old Review is more than usually puzzled
over the circumstance
It is an unaccountable fact that so many unemployed
exist in Australia while there is so much work to be done.
The cry pervading the rural industries is the scarcity of
labor, while in the cities one of the great problems of the
hour is in connection with the unemployed.
And apparently the questions of wages and hours,
and living conditions, never enter the head of the
Review ; or its alleged fact would not seem nearly
so unaccountable. The rural industries which,
according to the Review, are crying out for labor,
do a fair portion of their crying in the adver-
tising columns of the daily papers, and it invariably
sounds like this :
■VVyANTED, for the country, good man, understands
* ’ horses and double-furrow plough. Wages 20s.
per week and found.
ANTED, dairy-hand. 15s. and found.
TY7ANTED, man for station, must understand sheep.
’ ’ 20s. and found.
The hours aren’t specified, and “ found ” generally
means a, coarse and monotonous diet with an
unduly large proportion of treacle in it, and a
“doss” almost anywhere. In the city, on the
other hand, an ordinarily smart man, if he picks
up a trade, can get from Bs. to 10s. a day; and,
if he is only a pick-and-shovel wielder, he has a fair
chance of 7s. per day. He has no Sunday work,
does only eight hours toil per diem, and usually has
Saturday afternoon off. So when the Review
moans that—
One of the heaviest brakes on the wheels of Australian
development is the scarcity of labor of the right kind,
the Australian working man replies, in effect, that
quite the heaviest brake on the wheels of Australian
country development is the scarcity of wages and hours
and accommodation of the right kind.
If the squatter-friends of the Review, likewise its
farming acquaintances, would agree to pay decent
wages, and to generally improve the conditions of
country labor, there would soon be an end of the
scarcity of “ the right kind of labor;” and some
day very soon, let us hope, Wages Boards will see
that they do these things whether they agree or
not. Shearing, for example, is fairly well paid
for, and there is never any trouble in getting
plenty of good men. They come from far and
near. If anything like the same treatment were
meted out to the average bush worker there would
be such a rush for billets that a considerable
number of squatters and farmers would be trodden
underfoot in the scrimmage unless they got up
trees. When the Commonwealth gets control of
industrial legislation it will certainly pass a law
enforcing fair wages and fair hours and fair
accommodation. Then the rush will set in, and
the land magnate who has cut down all the trees
in his neighborhood will be sorry he was so reck-
less with the timber.
Bogus Subdivision of Big Estates.
Next month, so it is announced, Rupert Clarke,
Victoria’s affluent baronet, will sell 10,000 acres of
land within 16 or 20 miles of Melbourne—land
which has been locked up against settlement ever
since the first beginnings of the hamlet of Bear-
grass, which became Melbourne in course of
time. The land is to be divided into farms of
from 100 to 640 acres, so the proceeding really
looks like a little bit of close settlement. Unfor-
tunately, there is no guarantee at all that it will
be anything of the sort. The Victorian State
Legislature—which, in such matters as this, is
mostly the House of Rich Landlords—is so much
afraid of offending the immense land-shark that it
wouldn’t on any account whatever compel him to
cut up and sell his property in order to make room
for settlers. That is a villainously selfish, yet fairly
understandable attitude. But the State Legislature
goes further than that, and in its desire not to offend
the person (possibly unborn) who may be a stu-
pendous land shark by-and-bye it refuses to do
anything to prevent the aggregation of new big
estates. Therefore when a great landlord sells
10,000 or 20,000 acres in desirable allot-
ments, there is nothing to prevent one person
—who possibly may have 20,000 acres already—
buying all the desirable allotments, in which case,
to all intents and purposes, the land isn’t so much
cut up after it is cut up as it was before it was cut
up. So much of this is done that, though a certain
languid and voluntary process of cutting up has
gone on for 30 or 40 years, the small country land-
holder of Victoria has decreased all the time, and
the big one has increased. If, away back in the
’sixties, the lands of Victoria had been graded
according to their fertility, and it had been decided
what constituted a good living area in each district,
and it had been enacted that no m*n who already
possessed that area should acquire any further
land, also that the man who possessed less than
the specified living area or who possessed no area
at all should not acquire more than the fixed living
area—if that had been done 40 years ago Vic-
toria would now possess a lot of close settlement
that it has missed. If the State even passed
such a law now it would, in a year or
two, have a good deal of close settlement which,
as things are, it doesn’t seem likely to possess till
the Federal progressive land-tax comes along.
The scheme suggested above would certainly be
half-hearted and apologetic, yet even in its half-
hearted way it would do a great deal, and do it
without any new land-tax, or any compulsory
resumption, or any violence done to that sacred
and tremendous joss, the existing landlord. The
result would be that every time a big landlord
wanted money, and sold a few thousand acres of
land to get it, that area of land would
be cut up and sold to small holders, and
it would remain cut up for ever. In many places
even the sale of a 1200-acre farm would be impos-
sible under such a law —the farm would have to be
cut up into four 300-acre farms, and each of them
could be sold only to a person who has, at present,
no farm at all. Perhaps the great but flabby
Bent might be induced to consider this matter.
He is afraid to propose a progressive land-tax lest
he lose his job. He is afraid to propose a system
of compulsory resumption of big estates lest
he lose his job. He is afraid to let things
slide lest he lose his job. He is afraid of the
Country Party and the big landlords on one side,
aijd he is scared to death of the democratic Age
on the other. As a compromise (and he loves
compromises)—also as something to go on with
and as a means of postponing the loss of his
political skin, which will otherwise assuredly be
drying ere long on the Age’s fence — Bent might
try his hand at a measure providing for the limit-
ation of future estates. He couldn't make a
worse mess of it than he did of his Valuation
Bill.
The Case of Flynn.
Paris Nesbit, K.C., of Adelaide, as fair a lawyer
as ever put horsehair on his head, has taken up
the case of Myles Flynn, a prisoner under
sentence of 20 years in S. Australia. Having
petitioned the Governor to re-open the case,
Nesbit has now printed a pamphlet so that the
public may know the facts of the business. The
K.C. never acted for Flynn and doesn’t know
him, but he happened to be in Court while the
case was being tried ; in fact, there were two men
accused, and Nesbit appeared for the other one.
Chief Justice Way tried the case, and when
the jury came out and said that it had
doubts about Flynn, a constable (besides
18 others) having sworn to an alibi, the
Chief Justice remarked that the constable
and the accused were fellow-countrymen. There
wasn’t any evidence on that point; but as the
constable’s front name was Joseph Francis and
the accused’s was Myles, the Chief Justice evi-
dently reckoned that it was safe enough to put
them both down as Irishmen. If, however, an Irish
policeman isn’t to be trusted where another Irish-
man is concerned —and the Chief Justice’s remark
must either have meant that or it was meaning-
less —then what is the Chief Justice doing that he
doesn’t start a crusade for the purifying of the
police force by clearing out every Irishman from it ?
The case, however, does not rest wholly on that
point. There was a cloud of witnesses who spoke
to the same effect as the constable —namely, that
Flynn was miles away from where the highway
robbery was committed when it was committed.
And there are other circumstances in his favor.
Lawson, the other man, swears positively that
Flynn was not his mate. “ I would not
be in the shoes of anyone responsible for
Flynn’s conviction, or, with a knowledge of
the case, for his retention in confinement, for all
the money in the universe,” Paris Nesbit, K.C.,
wrote a year ago; and he is evidently of the same
opinion still. That opinion alone is worth a good
deal; the facts upon which he bases it are worth
more. Thousands of people in the State have a
most uneasy feeling with regard to the case ; and
nothing short of a review of the circumstances
will remove that feeling. Even the possibility of
wounding the keen sensibilities of Chief Justice
Way ought not to be enough to burke that
inquiry. It is better to find out that even the
Chief Justice has erred, and to repair the wrong,
than to feel that he has probably erred and to
refuse to repair the wrong. It is better to be
reminded that even the Chief Justice is human
than to have an uneasy feeling that, rather than
admit it, the State is prepared to shut? up an
innocent man for 20 years.
More Coalition.
William Kidston, Premier of Queensland, went
out and politically hanged himself one day last
week. He may last a long time after being
hanged, but he did the deed none the less. In
other words, he coalesced with the Philp party
the worst affliction and disgrace that ever fell
upon the great northern State —and now presum-
ably its gods will be his and its misdeeds will be
his also. The prospect ahead of the State
looks gloomy. Queensland has got back
to the two-party system, which is the fetich
of the daily press, and Australia’s experi-
ence in the past has been that the two-party
system, when it is arrived at by compromise and
coalition, is a curse which has no redeeming
feature. The Service-Berry and Gillies-
Deakin and Bent-Peacock coalitions in
Victoria produced three Ministries whose
records call aloud for burial. The Parkes-
Robertson and Carruthers-Twaddell coali-
tions in N.S.W. were better, but not much
better. The Stout-Vogel alliance in Maoriland
is still an unpleasant memory. The Griffiiavraith
combination in Queensland buried two political
reputations that had once been great, and the
Mcllwraith-Nelson combine which followed
was almost as bad. When the coalition consists
of two parties with widely-divergent policies, the
result is generally easy to foresee. The Liberal
half of the party prevents the Tory half going in
for actual retrogression—which is a pity, for the
more rapidly it filled up the measure of its iniquity
the sooner would the community be rid of it. The
Tory half prevents the Liberal half making any
progress, and a period of utter legislative stagnation
sets in. Yet the coalition must do something to fill
in time, and the easiest thing is to devise “a
vigorous Public Works policy ” and borrow lots of
money. By sheer bribery and corruption the
coalition keeps in power while the loans last.
Then there is a great smash and a perfume like that
of a long-drowned elephant that has burst; and
at the best there follows a long period of bitter
economy while some patient, indefatigable plodder
of the Turner variety is cleaning up the ruins.
All these wretched shifts and expedients are a
sacrifice to the silly English joss of Party Govern-
ment. If the Ministry were elected by the House
at the beginning of a new Parliament, and held
office for good or evil for the duration of that
Parliament, it really wouldn’t matter whether
there were two parties in the House or twenty.
Coalitions are the fruit of a corrupt tradition
that a House of Parliament should always
be divided into two factions—the one which has
office and doesn’t care how it keeps it, and the
one which hasn’t office and doesn’t care how it
gets it. The new Queensland combine will almost
certainly bury the political reputation of William
Kidston six feet deep ; and it can only be hoped
that it won’t dig up the political reputation of
Robert Philp, for to have that thing above
ground would increase the death-rate. How long
the new arrangement will last is a mere matter of
conjecture. In Victoria, the incapacity of Labor-
leader Prendergast kept the Bent-Peacock
combine in office for quite a while. In N.S.W.,
the incapacity of McGowen keeps the Wade-
Twaddell combine in office. In Queensland, the
impracticability of David Bowman is relied upon
to keep the Kidston-Philp or Philp-Kidston
Ministry, or the Phidston Government or the
Kilp Cabinet —its proper name won’t be known
for certain till we see its first policy statement-
in office. This is the period of unwholesome
coalitions in Australia; three-fourths of the popu-
lation is governed, in a State sense, by them.
Labor leaders of the calibre of Thomas Price
could certainly upset two of them at short notice,
but leaders like Price are rare. Labor, in addi-
tion to having a policy which appeals to its own
direct following, badly wants a policy which will
appeal to a great mass of people outside its own
following. If it declared itself in State politics as
the absolute anti-borrowing party and the con-
sistently pro-Federal and anti-State Frights party
it might scoop in enough new votes to lift it into
office. The public grows scared of loans and tired
of State Frights.

The Vision From Tsushimo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last night to me
Came a ghost from out the sea—
Came a spectre from the sand,
And it took me by the hand,
Ere it led
My uneasy spirit forth
To the dead.
Years had gone by
Since I’d laid me down. The sky
Seemed a sky all smeared with blood;
The black, sluggish Harbor flood
Mirrored fire,
And the wounded called to God
From the mire.
Straight to the tide
Led my silent, awful guide,
And he beckoned me to come,
Whilst the tapping of a drum
In the sea
Sounded in my stricken ears
Horribly.
Still played the drum,
As if bidding me to come,
And it called unto my soul
With its stern, insistent roll
Till it said :
“ Haste, O Soul, upon thy way
To the Dead ! ”
Numb —cold and numb—
At the calling of the drum,
Straight I stepped into the sea,
Where there came a dream to me
Of the Lost.
Said the drum : “O Spirit, see !
’Tis the Cost! ”
Deep in the sea
Marched the Men of History,
Steadily as seamen should,
Where each drowned captain stood
By his ship,
With his Russian sword again
At his hip.
Ranged rank on rank,
Where each shattered vessel sank,
Did the sailors take their stand,
Stern and bloodless on the sand
In review ;
And each captain looked upon
His dead crew.
Guns, sunken guns,
Where the Asian current runs,
Dreaming ever ’mid the sand
Of a bright, unguarded land
To the south.
Would that you could boom the word
Mouth by mouth —
That the iron lips of Hell,
With their flame and screaming shell,
Will pronounce a sentence dread
On a careless Nation’s head
Lest we arm.
Would to God that you could crash
That alarm !
Guns, vanquished guns,
As the tide of Mem’ry runs,
Do you dream of sunlit seas
And the smoke that with the breeze
Weaved a pall,
When in insolence you frowned
Down on all ?
With the waning moons and suns
Do you ever dream, O Guns,
Sunk in sleep,
Of the pride that once was yours
Ere you found upon the floors—
On the dark eternal floors
Of the deep—
The grey wisdom of the grave,
Learnt by ghostly king and slave,
Serf and lord,
That the Sword alone can sate
From the Sword.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

trates over or comes within the neighborhood of
the N.S.W. borders. Its sternly anti-Socialistic
gorge rises at the reflection that the Socialistic
Railways of N.S.W. don’t provide what it terms
“a readjustment of trains” (which, to employ
simple terms once more, signifies a gratis special
service) in order that it may arrive (free of charge)
at Newcastle, Bathurst, Orange, and other widely-
separated towns in time to greet the folks there
with- their matutinal rasher, and thus cut into the
profits of the N.S.W. papers published in those
towns. Also it wants the State railways to
carry it, and the State employees to handle it
(all free of charge), so that it may strike the dis-
tricts on the Victorian and Queensland borders
before any other newspapers get there. If these
requests are granted, the Telegraph will rest and
be satisfied for a while. And in the intervals of
leaping upon the “ pernicious and insane doctrine
of State Aid,” and “the foolish wickedness of
puffing-up.private industries by legislative inter-
ference,” it will have a kindly word or two for
the administration which is responsible for giving
it Socialism and Protection. It takes an uncom-
promising Freetrade and Anti-Socialist journal of
the Telegraph’s stamp to display such brute
illogicalness and live.
