Anti-Sosh and the Fake Thereof.
The Women’s Anti-Sosh Congress, just con-
cluded in Sydney, provided, perhaps, the most
striking instance of the devious methods of the
party that has been put forward for some years.
Fully one-half of the papers read and the
addresses delivered were obviously not composed
by the persons who purported to be the authors,
and had cross-examination been permitted, most
of those who dealt airily with intricate subjects
would have been hopelessly fogged. In one or
two instances, at least, the matter supplied by
one person was delivered by a second, on behalf
of a third, the second being chosen for histrionic
purpqses only. Since its last congress, Anti-Sosh
has learned something, and this time it put its
best ankle forward, in making a glorious dis-
play of feminine loveliness, and the average
session looked like the last lap of a
Beauty Competition, or a Commemoration Day
at Helena Rubinstein’s skin-food college.
Formerly Anti-Sosh women’s congresses were
dreary functions, in which gaunt, elderly females
in ill-fitting clothes droned over confused matters
for hours, mixing up State and Commonwealth
powers, and demanding reforms which were
granted by Edwy about A.D. 435. But all that
has ended. Anti-Sosh to-day is a magnificent
affair in which the elderly, gaunt female is thrust
as far in the background as possible, and the pub-
lic is regaled by an assortment of beauteous dam-
sels as glorious as tight-fitting, skirts and open-
work stockings can make them. Goo-goo eyes of
the most expert type are used for all they are
worth, and the value of casting these occa-
sionally towards the reporters’ table is not
overlooked. Taking it all round, there is
a strong suspicion that the whole affair was
staged-managed by J. C. Williamson. To be a
show girl in the Anti-Sosh theatrical combination
will in a year or two be ranked among the learned
professions, and the illusion will run little chance
of being blown out, so long as the stars of the
Brisbane Congress are drawn from Hobart
and Perth, and so on. Likewise when some
damsel paralyses the Melbourne Congress (run
under expert theatrical management) with her
40in. bosom and her tan stockings and her paper
on “The Effects of Corsets on the Political
Economy of Thibet,” nobody there will guess that
she had stepped right out of a George-street
vaudeville show or a Pitt-street bar. There are
illimitable possibilities in Anti-Sosh if it continues
to follow on these lines, and the Labor party
seems doomed unless it hires Maud Allan and
Pansy Montague to step across and pose in the
interests of Trades Unionism.
The Cow and the Child,
Lately the N.S.W. Public School Teachers’ Asso-
ciation sent out this inquiry to teachers in the
Cow country:—
How many children are considered unfit for instruction
by reason of their dairy work?
By way of a start, it got four replies, giving in-
formation that works out as follows :
Total Number Number
of Unfit for
Scholars Enrolled. Instruction.
1 .. .76 .. 7
2 .. 47 .. 19
3 .. 60 .. 9
* . 58 ..11
Total 241 .. 46
So, in the Cow country one child in every five, or
thereabouts, is pronounced unfit to receive in-
struction because of the excessive physical strain
put upon the baby population in the milking yards
and at the cream separators. Others besides
this 20 per cent, are no doubt subjected to the
same physical strain ; but they don’t show it—
yet. The worst cases, the association is told,
come from the farms worked on the “shares”
system, which means in plain language that it is
in order to provide the landlord with his tribute
that the unfortunate children of the helot are
being turned into something not far removed from
mere brutes, and stunted brutes at that. For
years Australia shied off a problem in relation
to one rural pursuit. It was assured that
without colored labor the sugar-growing business
was impossible. Finally it tackled the trouble
resolutely, having made up its mind that, though
the sugar industry was valuable, a White Aus-
tralia was more valuable still. So the semi-slave
labor of the black and tan had to go; and now
the industry, instead of wilting, thrives as it never
throve before. Something of the same sort will
have to happen with regard to dairy-farming.
The butter industry is valuable, but an enlight-
ened citizenship is more valuable still, and an
enlightened citizenship is out of the question
when such shocking tasks are put upon the grow-
ing child—the citizen of the future —that he is
physically unfit to receive instruction in the
schools. Not enlightenment, but inevitable degra-
dation, lies along that track. If the butter
industry necessarily involves this sort of thing,
then the price is too great, and the butter industry
must go. But the child-slave is no more indis-
pensable to the butter business than the black
slave was indispensable to the sugar busi-
ness. In fact, he or she is wholly super-
fluous at the Cow job. Remove from the
back of the poor little child-slave the land-
octopus, and most of his burden will be gone. In
some cases a parent’s inhumanity and greed may
be responsible for the grinding tasks set upon the
child ; but mostly it is the landlord’s tribute that
drives the whole Cow family to brutalising labor.
Nothing, then, will do much good except reliev-
ing the family of that tribute. The mere exchange
of the Cow-man’s position from tenant to landed
proprietor will do very little good so long as the
capital value of the land is made as high as
rules in most districts to-day. The unfortunate
child can be brutalised just as effectively in the
struggle to pay interest as in the struggle to pay
rent. The whole basis of land values is fictitious,
simply because the supply has been cornered ;
and the State that thinks more of its thousands of
children than of its mere handful of rich men and
the other handful of rich absentees will have to
attack this fictitious state of things with an axe.
For a start, a healthier public sentiment with
regard to land values must be cultivated; and,
instead of the country throwing up its hat at the
sight of its lands mounting up pound by pound in
value, it must be got to regard high values as a
curse, and as merely a means by which those
who own land and money lent on land extort
more from those who use the land. An acre
which has been valued at £lO won’t carry the
fraction of a goat more or grow the fraction of an
oat additional if its value is forced up to £2O or
£4O bvadvertising, booming, and market-trickery;
all that happens is that the owner can extort
twice or four-times as much out of the user,
while if the user buys, and mortgages the
holding in order to do it, he has to pay twice
or four-times as much interest. And in the
struggle to do that his children are condemned to
a slavery which deadens their brains and stunts
their bodies in the appalling manner described in
these school reports.

The Vicissitudes of Adolphus.
The political burlesque that has held the boards
at Melbourne Exhibition Building since July 2of
this year shows signs of drawing to a close.
The public has long since wearied of it, but the
star actor persists in bounding and capering before
empty benches, or to the jeers and scoffs of his
fellow-performers. His latest disaster is the re-
signation of MacKinnon and Peacock the
former allegedly because of the milk-and-watery
nature of the new Land Valuation Bill; the latter
because of ill-health. In Peacock’s case there
was probably the added inducement that a brick
was about to fall, and a brick hurts less if you
are not underneath it. MacKinnon’s case is piti-
ful. He is understood to be a capable, honest man,
with democratic tendencies and a clear perception
of the crying need for a land tax ; yet. though he
was aware of Bent’s notorious shiftiness, he
accepted honorary office under such a leader, and
when his own special piece of work—the Land
Valuation Bill—was disowned and thrown aside,
he still held on while men mocked. But he
speedily found there was no limit to Bent’s
folly, and, finally, he threw it up in disgust. Had
he grasped the chance when it came he might
have been State Premier within a week, with a
fair following and the friendly neutrality, if not
the cordial co-operation, of the Labor party. Now
he has to win his way again. As for Adolphus,
his political days are numbered. Whether he
survives his present troubles or not he is im-
possible any longer. His public gambollings and
prancings fail to amuse ; his promises no longer
distract; his muzzling of possible competitors or
opponents disgusts; his inaccuracies weary.
Even his comic songs are no longer taken seri-
ously, He used to be tolerated because his finan-
cial policy worked out in a fairly-satisfactory
way ; yet there has been a good deal of juggling
even about that. He wasn’t content to appear to
be doing fairly well; he must pretend to be doing
vastly better. And of late his financial policy
seems to have gone to the dogs. In practically
everything apart from finance, Bent hasn’t had
even the merit of doing fairly well; yet he has
pulled through by intrigue and make-believe,
often-times of a shameful character; and Victoria
can put up with the discreditable pretence no
longer. The career of Adolphus lies behind
him ; and the sooner Victoria bangs the door the
better for what little self-respect the Assembly
has left.

Cablegrams from England.
The amazing industrial and commercial downfall
in England suggests that the bottom is falling out
of Freetrade very suddenly at last. Among the
things reported are these :
(1) British trade, during the first eight months of 1908,
was £72,000,000 less than for the first eight months of
1907.
(2) Coal exports increased by £2,000,000 (otherwise the
slump in trade would have been £2,000,000 greater than
it was). The increase in the export of coal simply meant
a tendency backward towards the status of a primitive
community which supplies raw material for the use of
more progressive manufacturing States.
(3) Wages paid in Britain for the first eight months of
1908 were £15,000,000 less than in the corresponding
period of 1907. Some estimates make the slump even
worse.
(4) The number of unemployed among trades-unionists
rose to an estimated 10 per cent.
(5) The total number of male unemployed was esti-
mated at 645,000.
(6) A hunger strike reported in London. A hunger
strike seems to be a device whereby a multitude of men
decide to sit down in a public place and there die slowly
of starvation in order to draw public attention to their
misery. The police broke up the hunger strike.
(7) The hop industry having been practically ruined by
unrestricted foreign competition, the hop-workers organ-
ised processions through England to draw attention to
their wretchedness.
(8) Tremendous downfall in shipbuilding. One great
shipbuilding firm, with £2,000,000 worth of plant and
machinery, was reported as not having a single order—
not even an order for a punt or a skiff—on hand.
(9) E. Smith, a member of the London County Council,
urged that body to try to find work for the unemployed.
The starving multitude in the gallery cried “We are
hungry.” Police were sent for to remove Smith. Tre-
mendous struggle with Smith—who seems to be a good
fighter.
(10) Orders given in haste for the building of new war-
ships to find work for the unemployed.
(11) Offer made to take large number of unemployed
into the army, and give them food and a trifle of pay while
doing military manoeuvres.
(12) Mr. Burns, president of the Local Government
Board, said that within the next few months it would be
necessary to spend more in charitable relief than was
spent during the whole period of the Lancashire cotton
famine of the American Civil War days. He added that
no legislation to deal with lack of employment would be
introduced this year.
(13) The same John Burns reckoned that the job of
dealing with the question of poverty and distress will
probably occupy 100 years. And if Freetrade lasts, it is
more likely to occupy 1000 years. Meanwhile, the beef
and soup that are 100 years off look very remote.
(14) Thorne, M.P., having lost his head at the sight
of the distress which Freetrade seems powerless to
remedy, urged the unemployed to rush the bakers’ shops
and get bread anyhow. A summons was issued against
Thorne, M.P.
(15) Grayson, M.P., was hauled out of the House of
Commons by the Sergeant-at-Arms for growing excited
over the sufferings of the unemployed.
(16) Verdict of coroner’s jury, “death from lack of
nourishment,” or words to that effect, growing un-
pleasantly frequent.
(17) Numerous by-elections ; Tariff-reformers returned,
as a general thing.
The explanation of it all seems to be that the
growth of manufactures in the great Protectionist
countries, such as the United States, Germany,
France, Russia, etc., has at last put the world’s
supply ahead of the world’s demand. That pro-
duces a curious state of affairs. In the United
States, Germany, France and Russia the producer
has his local market secured to him by Protection,
and he has in addition as much of the outside
market as he can get hold of. But the British
producer hasn’t got his local market secured to
him by Protection ; he has just as much of the
British market as he can get in competition with
all the world and just as much of the outside
market as he can get in competition with all the
world. So when supply gets ahead of demand all
the world over, it is a cold day for Bull,, and but
for the fact that a number of his colonies and
foreign possessions give him a preference in
defiance of all the doctrines of Freetrade, the day
would be a shade colder than it is. Meanwhile,
as a side issue of the case, the anti-Defence crowd
in Australia might ask itself if it isn’t a low-down
thing to rely on a nation which has struck such a
patch of ill-luck as this to supply most of the men,
money, ships and naval capacity for the protection
of Australia.
MISS OLA HUMPHREY (her latest
photograph).

This letter comes to hand from Melbourne :
The Editor Bulletin. —ln your issue of this week there
appears a paragraph supposed to emanate from a well-
informed source. From first to last the paragraph is a
tissue of misstatements. John Wren bad no more to do
with the Carlton selection than yourself. In the first
place both the president and secretary of the Carlton
Branch of the P.L.C. were candidates for selection—both
popular officers. The selection body, consisting of mem-
bers of the Carlton Branch of the P.L.C. who were
financial members of not less than three months’
standing and all affiliated unionists residing in the
electorate with a similar qualification, gave Solly
an absolute majority on the first count, the selec-
tion being presided over by two specially ap-
pointed officers of the Central Executive, and I have
not heard one word of adverse comment regarding same
from any candidate or his supporters. As regards John
Wren’s participation in the selection, or the subsequent
fight which ended so gloriously on Friday last, it is too
absurd to seriously consider. I have never had the honor
of even speaking to John Wren in my life, but I am sure,
judging from what I have heard of the man, that we
would not have been put to such sore straits to finance the
campaign if he was personally interested in the struggle.
As regards Mr. Wren’s nominating Mr. David Gaunson,
he may have done so for aught I know to the contrary or
care. All Ido know is that Mr. Gaunson was the first to
announce his candidature, and that before even the
Labor selection was made. If your correspondent was so
well-informed as he pretends to be regarding Labor
politics in Victoria, he would know- that there is no
chance for members of a two-up school or anybody out-
side the ranks of organised labor to participate in the
selection of a Labor candidate ; but probably the whole
effusion is prompted by the working of an evil mind ever
ready to cry stinking fish and injure the movement,
which, however, is proof ag-ainst any such attacks.
C. J. Buckley, Secretary Carlton Branch Political
Labor Council.
James A. S. Brown, Returning Officer at Selection.
In reply to which The Bulletin’s correspondent
didn’t profess that John Wren went to the meet-
ing and openly nominated Mr. Solly, any more
than he professed that a Standard Oil Trust mag-
nate would be seen openly influencing a U.S.
politician in the public street in the middle
of the day. But most probably he, like
many other men, strongly believes that the
Victorian Labor party has got into a
state of general subservience to Wren, which
made the fact that Mr. Solly was, or was reputed
to be. the friend of that awful alleged “Demo-
crat ” a big influence in his favor. And the tone
of admiration in which the above letter alludes to
the ex-tote-shopkeeper doesn’t tend to dispel
ideas of that kind. The writers regard it as an
“ honor ” to speak to Wren.
. *
The latest developments of. the Smith-Lyne pro-
blem are interesting. Miss Grace Watson, the
very remarkable lady who seems to boss the
incoherent league of female Anti-Socialists,
alleged publicly that she and a Mrs. Smith once
called on Treasurer Lyne, and that that rude,
discourteous man coarsely addressed the lady over
and over again as just “Smith.” Lyne denied
the charge, and telegraphed to Miss Watson for
particulars. He got no answer at all from the
highly-discourteous lady, but later she made a
statement to a Sydney daily paper to the effect
that she hadn’t, like William Lyne, the Common-
wealth Treasury behind her out of which to pay
for her private telegrams, so she had to
reply through the press. The veiled sugges-
tion that Lyne annexed public funds with
which to pay for his private telegrams
looked a fairly mean business, and the
hint that Miss Watson, who apparently could
afford to live at one of the most expensive
hotels in Sydney, was only debarred from tele-
graphing full particulars of “Mrs. Smith”
through lack of the needful shilling or so, looked
ridiculous. Possibly it was also the want of the
necessary 2d. which prevented the lady sending
Lyne full particulars of Mrs. Smith by letter, and
possibly it was simply the want of Mrs. Smith
that prevented her giving full particulars to the
press when she had the loan of the ear of that in-
stitution gratis. Finally the S. M. Herald broke
ground in a new place with this paragraph :
The little controversy between Miss Grace Watson and
Mr. Lyne has entered another stage. Miss Watson has
given to us IN CONFIDENCE the name and address of the
lady who was addressed as plain “ Smith ” by the Minister,
and she is quite prepared to take the matter further if he
desires it. The charge of discourtesy is not only re-
peated but amplified, and the evidence bearing upon it
should be interesting if Mr. Lyne presses his contention
to a vigorous conclusion. We understand that the name
and address of “ Mrs. Smith"’ have been also given. in con-
fidence to the Melbourne Argus.
Miss Watson, it seems, is “quite prepared to take
the matter further ” if Lyne desires it, but all the
demands of Lyne that it should be taken further
evoked no response. And now it seems she has
furnished the name of Mrs. Smith to the Herald
“in confidence.” Why furnish the name as a dark
secret when she has already said the name is
Smith ? All this delay is calculated to rouse
suspicion. If Mrs. Smith had been produced at
once people might have believed in her. If she is
only dragged to light after long delay, there are
debased characters who will say that there has
been time enough to hunt up a person of loose
veracity who will say she is the Mrs. Smith in
question. And it isn’t advisable to give a handle
to debased characters like that.

The State Rights person has at least one consola-
tion amid all his woes ; and when the High Court
unanimously decided that the Deakin Govern-
ment was right about the Surplus Revenue Bill,
and that all the State Frights were wrong.
N.S.W. Premier Wade, on behalf of himself and
Bent and Evans and the rest, trotted out the
ancient political dope once more :
The framers of the Constitution, and those who voted
for Federation, I am sure, never contemplated such a pos-
sibility.
They must have been a deep-thinking lot, the
377,988 people who voted for the Federal Con-
stitution. According to Carruthers and Wade
and Bent and Newton Moore and the other
small local fry, these people looked ahead, and
foresaw everything, and contemplated everything,
and always they contemplated the way the State
Fright crowd contemplates. On every point,
from the wire netting steal to the attempt to
leave the Commonwealth with no money for
Defence and other urgent purposes, the defeated
anti-Australian finds consolation in saying that
anyhow the people who voted for Federation
thought the way he does. But though these
377,988 people are still mostly alive and able to
show their views by driving Deakin and Lyne
into oblivion they remain torpid. They take no
notice when Wade is so kind as to tell them
exactly what views the whole 377,988 of them held
nine or ten years ago. It is good of Wade; or
possibly it is darned impudent of Wade, but
anyhow nobody pays any attention.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “ The Merry Widow.”
Theatre Royal “ Diana of Dobson’s.”

Town Hall Newbury-Spada Concert (Nov. 2).

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ The Prince of Pilsen.”
Her Majesty’s “ The Scarlet Pimpernel”
Theatre Royal “ She Stoops to Conquer."
Bijou “ The Woman Pays.”
Kino’s “ The Midnight Mail.”
Opera House Rickards’ Vaudeville Co.
Wirth’s Park Circus and Menagerie.
Olympia West’s Pictures.
Coming so soon after the Women’s Anti-
Socialist Conference, “Diana of Dobson’s” (at
Sydney Royal) produces a distinct shock. It is a
shamelessly frank argument on behalf of a mini-
mum wage and industrial legislation generally.
Ten years ago the heroine of it would have been
hissed, as an Anarchist, from the boards of any
theatre frequented by the well-to-do classes; at the
least she is, undoubtedly, what the modern Con-
ferencer would call a “Socialist.” “Diana of
Dobson’s ” is a deadly shaft directed at those who
cling to the idea that the United Kingdom is a
bearable, much less a pleasant, place for the work-
seeker. This play, written by a Londoner, and
accepted by British critics as a faithful reflection
of contemporary industrial conditions, establishes
the amazing fact that a young, healthy, edu-
cated, and sober man, with no “encum-
brances,” and all the will in the world to
work, is liable in the Cold Country to starve to
death. The playwright would have adhered more
closely to the eternal verities had he made his toil-
seeking hero something other than an ex-Guards-
man and a cadet of a noble family. The half-
penny press of London constitutes itself a special
providence for the purpose of shepherding originals
of rank when they go forth on the trail of manual
toil. In real life Captain the Hon. Victor
Bretherton would not have been more than an
hour pursuing a pick and shovel job ere the Daily
Mail would have had a scare-headed account of
the phenomenon “set up” for next morning’s
paper. Thereafter offers of music-hall engage-
ments and lecturing tours would have rained on
the Captain like golden bricks, and he would have
left his bench on the Embankment for a bed at
Claridge’s. Diana’s lover should have been repre-
sented as the offspring of a moderately prosperous
member of the middling classes. Then the re-
sulting happenings would have followed quite
naturally.
* * * *
Diana is an employee at Dobson’s, a big
London millinery establishment. She has been
there for six years when the play opens, and by
strict attention to duty has achieved an income of
ss. a week, less deductions. The other young
ladies in the employ of Dobson also draw ss. a
week—when they don’t draw 3s. or 2s. 6d. They
are more or less satisfied with their lot. Not so
Diana. She cherishes no gratitude towards Dob-
son ; in fact, she despises and reviles him. She
says things about Dobson’s business methods
that are calculated to make the worthy folk
who yearn to see the working-classes “ keep in
their place,” and desist from selfish clamorings
fora “ living wage,” shudder disgustedly. Sud-
denly Diana learns that she has been left a legacy
of £3OO. Forthwith she goes “fanti.” She ad-
dresses painful truths to Miss Pringle, forewoman,
and decides to repay herself for the penury and
joylessness of her girlhood with one whole month
of gay, well-fed, decently-frocked Life. So she
goes to Pontresina, in the Swiss Engadine, and
there meets Bretherton and his match-making
aunt; and one Grinley, a loathly knight who is
in the same line of business as Dobson. Both
Grinley and Bretherton propose to her under the
delusion that she is a wealthy widow. Grinley
she rejects with contumely, to the delight of the
audience (his Private Enterprise principles and
practices have rendered him abhorrent to the
latter); but Bretherton has won her heart, and so,
womanlike, she tells him her life-story. It is a
severe shock to Bretherton, and, in the anguish of
the moment, he does not behave as well as he
might. Diana accuses him of fortune-hunting,
and taunts him with his worthlessness and
incapacity. The last act reveals Brether-
ton striving to earn a crust by the labor
of his own unassisted hands. He has not taken
to work-hunting of necessity (he has £6OO a year
of his own), but for the sake of his self-respect.
He wants to see whether he is really the econonii-
-eally valueless unit Diana said he was. He is in
the act of finding that she spoke sooth when the
lady herself happens along—also destitute —and
the two make it up and “return to civilisation,”
to live on Bretherton’s income.
* * * •
The part of Diana, disillusioned shop-
lady, fits Tittell Brune like a glove. This actress
does not handle stage nuances over-convincingly,
but in the portrayal of the honest, slightly
humorless type of young person, with the mini-
mum of “nonsense” about her, she is in her
element. And so her Diana is a joyous and life-
like study. Thomas Kingston presents a Captain
Bretherton who improves as he goes on. The
genuine Guardsman who took to walking with an
aggressive stoop, and arms violently waggling and
squared at an unnatural angle, and who in other
respects comported himself like a newly-fledged
remittance man, would be “ragged’’out of his
regiment before he attained to a captaincy. The
Bretherton of Kingston did things in Act 11. on
last Saturday night that would have driven his
brother-officers —had he ever possessed any—to
put jam or treacle on his beautiful hair. But as
the play wears on, Bretherton’s exasperating
“ lift ” wears off, and he becomes a natural and
attractive young man. Gregan MacMahon’s Sir
Jabez Grinley is a vulgarly unpleasant trader of
the conventional sort; and Mrs. Brough supplies
an artistic portrait of a match-making aunt. The
staging and mounting are excellent.
* * *
By “10-2-4” :
Both the Sydney dailies agree in greeting “Diana of
Dobson’s,” the new Theatre Royal production, with the
faintest praise possible considering that the show is
numbered among the “ good advertisers.” Said the S.M.
Herald (26/ll)/’08)
“For the cause of its very phenomenal success in
London one would be inclined to look to the audacity
of the first scene.”
This is tantamount to saying that London audiences
are so prurient that they would flock for months to a
play that showed a few young women in a dormitory.
The Telegraph reckons that audiences don’t like to hear
“didactic deliverances on the injustice of social conditions
and the tyrannies of sweating employers,” and it considers
Diana a “small-minded person.” Wherefore this thusness?
The explanation is simple. Diana is a breathing, moving
and speaking tract in advocacy of Sosh. She is a live
woman, taken out of a world where industrial legislation
of the sort the local Anti-Soshite denounces as “in-
famous ” and “ anarchistic ” has not yet fully penetrated,
and is presented before the eyes of people who can only
think when they are faced with object lessons. Diana’s
dreary lot is calculated to make folks sympathise with
the sweated shop-girl, though they never gave that luck-
less young person’s existence a thought before. And the
path between sympathy for the sweated and approval of
what the Herald and Telegraph denounce as Sosh is
very short. No, it is not surprising that the Anti-Sosh
papers decry “ Diana of Dobson’s’’ somewhat.'
* * *■ *
Two of the Cherniavskis are ‘ * dear little
fellows” and the third is “a fine youth.” The
youngest is 12, the eldest is 17. In point of
numbers the appallingly-musical family of Cherni-
avski is like most of the Russian Hebrew families
that have been chivvied out of Czardom. There are
grown men and women and babes in arms in these
families, and the units all seem capable of acquiring
wads of academic paper or of mastering any musical
instrument. The baby of the Cherniavski tribe,
aged two years, tears off her bib with rage and
throws her “ comforter ” at her astounded family
when one of her 12 (or 15) brothers and sisters
plays half a tone flat in the domestic symphony.
A babe like that must be a great joy to a chivvied
family !
A Grand Piano Underneath the Bough,
A Gramaphone, a Chinese Gong, and Thou (Baby)
Trying to Sing an Anthem off the Key—
Oh, Paradise were Wilderness Enow !
The Russian Yid baby, instead of getting appen-
dicitis, gets Tschaikowskyitis. Then it goes on a
tour as a wonder child, and later settles down as
a great teacher. The three Cherniavskis brought
here by Mr. Edward Branscombe, and now
appearing at Sydney Town Hall, are startlingly
ahead of their ages in their management of their
respective instruments —violin, ’cello and piano.
The pianist—he is thirteen years young—is the
best of the bunch. He and his twelve-year-old
brother wear wide white collars, knickers, and
Paderewski hair that has surely been tongsed.
All the motherly hearts in the audience went out
to the white-collared pair. Mrs. Marie Hooton,
a pleasant contralto with an attractive platform
manner and appearance, and Mr. Percival
Driver’s baritone enlivened the programme.
** * *
“The Belle of Mayfair” and her entour-
age at Sydney Cri. abandoned London W. on
Tuesday night, and are now putting in an all too
brief Wednesday-to-Friday at The Hook of Hol-
land. The maidens and their attendant cavaliers,
bloques, etc., do much the same things when they
are in Holland as they do when they are in Mayfair,
but they perform their feats so prettily that it is
worth journeying even that long distance to view
them. On Sunday they leave the Hook for the
city where the Wrongs come from, andtheMaState
is getting her hankies back from the wash accord-
ingly, and making preparations for a perfect gust
of sobs and misery.
** * *
“The Merry Widow” is coining just as
much money for J. C. Williamson now as it did
in its first week at Her Majesty’s (Sydney); it’s a
very slack night when everybody who wants to
get in can find enough room even for a hang-over.
Carrie Moore finishes her share of the business on
Friday ; then Miss Ohls arrives and wears the
gay weeds in the other’s place—wears them for
three weeks or more. By that time Margaret
Anglin will be back with something more ambi-
tious than Sydney has yet seen her in ; but which
of the Williamson houses will put her up hasn’t
yet been finally announced.
*- * * *
Johnson, the Banger, shows his muscles
working under a skin that shines as brown satin.
Then he rains blows on a harmless ball and anon
he spars with a curate-like trainer. All this goes
on at the Sydney Glaciarium, where West’s
Pictures take the eyes of large audiences. The
recent great R.C. procession, which intended to be
Eucharistic and caused such alarms and excursions
amongst those of other faiths in London, is filmed,
and shows a colossal caterpillar of humans boring
its way through dense swarms of onlookers.
Humorous pictures are interspersed just to keep
the corners of the aggregate public mouth turned
up.
* * *
“ Two Little Sailor Boys ” will occupy the
heaving deck of Sydney Palace for the last time on
Friday night. On Saturday Jo Smith’s Aus-
tralian drama, “A Miner’s Trust,” will be paid
over the footlights to an expectant audience.
The play deals with the old “digging” days
when our forefathers didn’t seek their fortunes
clad in red Crimean shirts, but in any old shirts
that came handy, and when the bullock dray
sped through the land like a demented snail.
*# * *
At Sydney Lyceum Mr. Spencer lias
rushed backward per flickergraph across the
wastes of Time to the days of the Yankee Civil
War. Then he hustles to England, where the
great Brazilian battleship Minas Geraes slides
down the stocks into the water, and shows the
grim outlines of the most powerful war machine
afloat on the seven seas. Then away to Constan-
tinople, when the city of the Sultan is a mass of
flame, and the pariah dogs, Turks, Armenians,
Greeks, and other wild animals fly in silent but
realistic terror before the conflagration. Stam-
boul being switched off, the audience finds
itself running lightly through Paris and paus-
ing for drinks at each place of historic
interest, and thence to the winter sports at Lyons.
The world spins round, and the gazer is back in the
Triassic period among the haunts of Pre-Adamite
man. The microscope having been comman-
deered, the balancing evolutions of the ordinary
blue-bottle fly prove that undesirable insect to be
a balancer and tight-rope walker of parts and high
degree. The moving picture has become an in-
stitution, and threatens to play a leading part in
education and the dissemination of news. In the
adjacent future nobody will buy the daily news-
paper. He will just wander into the daily cine-
matograph hall and see what has occurred.
** & *
The Bulletin paid its weekly visit of
inspection to Sydney Tivoli the other evening,
and made the acquaintance of Duncan’s Scotch
Collies—some 11 dog-humorists with beautiful coats
and tails like expensive ostrich feathers. They
skip and jump in an accomplished manner, and
one pup even somersaults ; but they are most im-
pressive as musicians and actors. Seated on
chairs, behind small harmoniums, they play care-
fully with their paws “The Blue Bells of Scot-
land.” The somersaulting pup has to paw the
last note of the melody, and the thoughtful way
it eyes its instrument before it gently presses the
keyboard is a joy to witness. The same dog plays
the part of the chauffeur in a motor-car tragedy,
and when something bursts with a loud report it
jumps off the seat and crawls underneath the car
quicker than the average motorist. The other
big turns of the show are the three McGregors,
Osborn and Brooks, Abdullah’s Arabs, Barnard’s
dolls, Ted Kalman, Fanny Powers, and the four
Rosebuds. At Saturday’s matinee J. W. Rickaby,
a comedian from London, and Frank and Jean
Latona, from Noo Yark, will face the Tivoli
music for the first time.
* * * JK-
Perhaps the point most really remarkable
in “ The Red Mill,” which has had a triumphant
week at Adelaide Royal, is the way in which
Sherras and Miss Olive Godwin take as merely
mature sentimentalists two parts which were
clearly written as elderly absurdities. Still their
singing would easily condone a much bigger crime.
Singing is not the strong point of theco,; dancing
is. Miss Fan Dango floats, and Ford and Leslie
flounder and fall and flop all over the place. It is
the first time Leslie has had the chance really to
dominate a stage, and he fully holds his own with
the amazingly agile Ford. For “ The Prince of
Pilsen ” the co. is strengthened by the dropping
in of Myles Clifton and Miss Ada Murphy, but
the stay of the Prince of the place the beer is
named after is to be very brief. At the Town
Hall, the Welshmen, making heavy play with
hymn tunes, have gone out with a blaze of piety
and national enthusiasm.
** * *
To-nighfc (Thursday) is the concluding
“ Twelfth Night ” at Her Majesty’s, and the end
of Miss Margaret Anglin’s season in Melbourne.
The revival of “Twelfth Night” was a happy
managerial thought. The house was crowded,
and the multitude appeared to find more pleasure
in this fairly-good performance of the ancient
comedy than it would have been likely to get from
another presentation of the leading lady in a
modern emotional drama of unreal life. To them
who take their Immortal William very seriously,
the present revival may have seemed wanting
in traditional Shakspearian manner. From the
general point of view, it was none the worse for
being nothing to rave about. No part of the per-
formance emphasised the inferiority of other por-
tions. It had its tit-bits and prime cuts, but, on
the whole, the joint was consistently underdone.
AT WIRTH’S CIRCUS.
BABY PASTORE
AND HIS LITTLE
BROTHER.
Viola, as personified by Miss Anglin, is
every inch a girl, and a frankly feminine young
spinster at that. If she fell in love with the languid
nobleman who sent her with messages of hopeless
passion to the other girl, Miss Anglin didn’t let
the audience see her falling. She concealed her
feelings underagirlv demeanor, and one thought of
her as being dressed up in boys’ clothes for a lark.
She was more charming and light-hearted than
some previous Violas, yet even less convincing
than most of them. Her “ disguise ” offered the
Duke Orsino and the Countess Olivia no possible
excuse for not seeing through it at a first glance.
Also her twin brother resembled her in about the
same degree that a stale potato resembles a fresh
apple. Consequently when the Countess insisted on
a sudden marriage with the male twin, the
audience reckoned she had discovered that
he wasn’t his sister. Miss Alma Vaughan
played Olivia with quiet effectiveness and a fine
pair of eyes. Toby Belch and Aguecheek (Fitz-
gerald and Stirling) avoided exaggeration as a
rule, and missed the full force of their characters
all the time, but in several respects they were
stronger than their colleagues in the broad-comedy
department. Dion Titheradge, youthful and un-
furnished in his clowning, partly redeemed his
failure with a pleasant singing voice that came
in useful at the end ; whilst Miss Susie
Vaughan’s conception of Maria was unsatisfac-
tory every way. In George Titheradge’s
realistic performance of Malvolio, the stiff-backed
steward, the one thing lacking was the pompous
personality that should go to justify the victim-
ising of him. He seemed insufficiently offensive.
He was fairly inside his character, but he didn’t
make it large enough. All the same, the fine
quality of his humor was a revelation to people
who had seen Malvolio’s vanity buffooned in
previous productions of “ Twelfth Night,” on the
same stage and at the Princess'.-!. Kolker, as
Orsino, was almost as picturesque as the scenery,
which earned sundry bursts of applause.

On Friday night the Dramatic Fund is to
get an interesting benefit at Her Majesty’s. The
Savage Club has undertaken a production of
“Society,” and the brightest of the club’s ama-
teur talent, assisted by the arts and graces of its
lady relatives and friends, will appear in one con-
secutive performance of Robertson’s comedy.
On Saturday comes Julius Knight in “The
Scarlet Pimpernel,” for the first time in Mel-
bourne. Miss Ola Humphrey and a William-
sonian company will be associated with the long-
lost Julius on this occasion, and the box-plan is
being ticked off with appropriate eagerness.
** * *
The tail end of the ballad-opera season at
Melbourne Royal looks somewhat limp and des-
pondent. Last week “The Bohemian Girl” was
staged with so much ill-success that two important
changes in the lady portion of the cast were
promptly announced. The bill for Wednesday
was “Les Cloches,” with Miss Stella Gastelle to
the fore; and, for the last two nights of the
season, “ Maritanathe “East Lynne” of the
lyric stage—is to resume duty. After which, on
Saturday, Miss Madge Mclntosh, Harcourt
Beatty, Gaston Mervale, and the rest of the
company which played in lurid drama at the
Royal long months ago, will reappear, clothed in
18th century costumes and in their right minds,
to play “She Stoops to Conquer.” Recent re-
vivals of Shakspeare are believed to have cleared
the Melbourne air and created a demand for such
intellectual matter as “She Stoops” and other
old-established comedies. Anyhow, Melbourne
is to have a series of them during the next seven
weeks.
** * *
At Melbourne Olympia, on Saturday night,
T. J. West crowded in his usual momentous
assortment of pictorial facts and fatuities. Of his
array, “ Paris from a Balloon,” was interesting,
in that it showed how little —bar Paradise and
Gehenna—is safe from the inquisitive picturo-
grapher. As for the film, it spread out that
essentially frivolous village in a queer plan—inter-
sected by streaks of roadway; punctuated by
parks, houses, chimneys, and etceteras; and freely
dotted with insignificant specks, which were
vehicles and humans. Another picture professed
to reveal how Mr. Burns and Mr. Johnson train
for their pugilistic encounters. If the film could
have possibly exhibited them training for news-
paper interviews, the audience would have been
stunned by their stamina.
** * *
“ Human Nature ” is progressing favorably
at Melbourne King’s. Nell Temple—served up
with panting majesty by Miss Duggan—continues
to rescue her unutterable Fwankie from the paws
of the baby planters, and that more matured
nipper, Dick, despite his growing delirium, still
has the gumption to throw his seven in about 79
agonising positions. Moreover, Laurence Dunbar,
who is always so languid, lanky and select, has
added to his aesthetic suitings and affluent
moustache a whole wad of Imperial chin
whisker, and has all the solemnity of newness.
“Human Nature ” is a weird medley of novelties.
However, to-morrow (Friday) evening sees its
finish, and Saturday is to witness a revival of
“The Midnight Mail,” on the usual completeseale,
at three, two, and one.
Who says there are no Australian drama-
tists? One of them, who calls himself “Karta”
on his programme because his name is Carter,
has accomplished six pieces for the stage—
melodrama, two dramas without the melo, a
dramatette and a comedy, exclusive of his
latest effort, which is seemingly a dramaling.
This was performed to a packed audience of
admiring friends at Furlong’s room, Royal
Arcade (Melb.), the other night. Its title
is “Destructo, ” and the story (in one
solitary act) deals with the invention, by
a military person, of a terrible explosive
which plays the name-part. One of the
amateurs concerned in this serio-comic
entertainment was Mrs. Maurice Dudley,
whose clever acting would be certain to cover
a multitude of sins.
The Baines’ troupe of one star and about
a dozen assorted starlets having clattered out of
Melbourne Bijou, that playhouse reopens on
Saturday night with Edwin Geach’s co. in “ The
Woman Pays.” According to preliminary an-
nouncements, the play solves the great i human
problem : “Is Marriage a Failure?”

Ada Crossley returns to Melbourne on
Saturday (which happens to be Derby Day) for the
purpose of singing in “ Elijah,’’ at the Town Hall.
Thenewly-formed Vic. Festival Choral Society will

give farewell concerts before making for Ade-
laide and the West.
* *■ *
At Princes Court, which is nowadays
Wirths’ Park, the circus and menagerie of those
distinguished landowners will rage and roar until
further notice. An exhaustive appreciation of
the circus appeared in last Bulletin. There
is nothing new to remark about the
Wirthian enterprise this week, except that
the odor of menagerie no longer penetrates
to the place where the -elephants gambol
together and the clowns mingle their little
jokes. The lion, the unicorn and kindred beasts
of prey have their cages in the open since they
were domiciled in the Park. Fresh air from
Heaven blows cool upon their fevered tails.
When the leopard puts his chin into the sawdust
and glances stealthily upward through the bars of
his prison he can sometimes see the Southern Cross.
Under these cooler conditions the polar bear is
recovering his appetite, and if the management
were to provide him with a pailful of iced drink the
animal would probably feel almost happy.

SOCIETY
PENFOLDS
WINES
IN TROUBLE.
Five thousand foot police and 200 mounted men
guarded the precincts of Parliament House, London, to
prevent a suffragette rush. — Cablegram.
MEMBER OF HOUSE OF C OMMONS: “ Why, it’s just
as if we was the Czar!”
The barque Fifeshire sailed out of New-
castle (N.S.W.) with a cargo of coal, and fled
2500 miles over the ocean until it found a nice,
comfortable reef. Then it sat down with a bump
and the crew took to the boats and rowed away to
an island, where they found a Chinaman called
Moses. Stripped of its newspaper trimmings,
the Fifeshire wreck is a simple story with a prom-
inent Biblical character hovering uncertainly in
the background, and a few thousand years hence
earnest searchers after the Truth will be found
fighting amongst themselves as to which version
is the correct one—the bulrush or the Fifeshire.
Unless a big gale has happened along since the
crew took to the boats, the Fifeshire is still bolt
upright in mid-ocean with the rags of its sails
flapping disconsolately in the wind. As the
mariners left, the Fifeshire looked less like a wreck
than a patient ocean hen who was trying to hatch
out a setting of reef eggs and make palm islands
of them.
Archdeacon Potter was very wrath with
A. Kookaburra Peacock, Chief Secretary (since
resigned), for allowing the Rev. Geo. Gladstone
and another dismissed Anglican parson the
privilege of continuing to perform marriages.
“Your action seems to me to be in direct opposi-
tion to the Church of England authorities,” wrote
the infuriated Archdeacon. It is hard to eliminate
from the Anglican clerical mind the idea that its
Church is licensed to boss the holy job in these
parts. Peacock replied that so far as his office was
concerned marrying was a civil business, and he had
merely given the gentlemen in question civil
appointments that had nothing to do with the
Church —which, by the way, in such circumstances
is never civil.
* * * *
Before the time expired for making claims
on the Vic. Railway Department on account of the
Sunshine disaster, no less than 50 summonses were
served, including 24 for the full amount allowed by
law (£2000), and one for £5OOO. These claims' only
account for 14 deceased persons ; and, as the De-
partment is known to have already paid certain
claims in full, probably it has already “parted”
quite as much as the £74,398 it is now being sued
for. There were altogether 44 killed and over 300
injured, and as this writer knows of one case
wliere a passenger got £lO for a trifling shock and
a torn overcoat, and another where a victim was
invalided for a week—and spent the time fishing
and flirting at a country resort—and was then
solaced with a trifle of £OO, he reckons Tait’s next
balance-sheet won’t look too healthy.
* * * *
“Hamer” : When the Clerks’ Union gets
out a report on the prevailing conditions of clerical
employment in Sydney, the details will make the
revelations in the Shop Assistants’ Arbitration
case seem almost tame. Quite a number of people
are supplying the Clerks’ Union with information,
and some of it is interesting. For instance, one
big importing firm which employs a staff of
women clerks pays the one in charge of the
four others the staggering salary of 12s. 6d. per
week. Details of the wages of the other four
women are not given, because apparently what
they get is not worth mentioning. Another
importing firm—the importing firms are far and
away the worst sweaters —has a clerk who, after
20 years’ service, rose to a position of trust and
importance, and a salary of 255. per week! But
there was balm in Gilead for that preposterous
Croesus, because recently the firm raised his screw
a princely half-a-crown “on account of the in-
creased cost of living under the tariff.” Also,
there are records of clerks who, as a regular
thing and quite apart from overtime, are expected
to work from early morning to 7.30, and up to
9.30 on Saturdays. No wonder the clerks who
occasionally write to the papers and say that they
get £2 10s. or £3 a week can’t see anything to
growl at. They are the Fat Men, the Bloated
Aristocrats, of the business; and the Fat Man
never can see anything to growl at —except the
rank ingratitude of the lower orders.

Architects are foretelling disaster to the
new Melbourne central railway station. Appar-
ently to save initial
expense, Tait is con-
structing the inner
wall of the long build-
ing, not of brick or
reinforced concrete,
but of Oregon studs,
covered with Oregon
boards, covered again
with zinc sheeting,
and covered again
with two coats of
paint to represent
brick in places, marble
in patches, and gran-
ite in spots. Of
course it is sham and
shoddy, and when the
zinc sheets warp and
buckle, as they will
in summer, it will be
very unsightly shod-
dy. But the worst
feature is the danger
from fire. As built,
that long, high, nar-
row building is practically a flue with an ideal
natural draught, and once that pine facing catches
fire all the water in the Yan Yean reservoir won’t
put it out.
At Banana (Central Q.), a family feud
similar to that which raged in mediaeval times
between the Montagues and the Capulets, and
having points of resemblance to the unpleasant-
ness that for many weary years marred the lives
of the Guelphs and the Ghibellines, flaunts itself in
public places, and refuses to lie down. The pro-
moters of the feud are one Carter and a photo-
grapher named Kirkman. The feud broke into
the local police court recently, and was respon-
sible for the following litigation '• —
W. R. Carter v. G. Kirkman. (Plaintiff charged defendant
with striking him with a heavy whip.) Defendant fined
£2, and 4s. 6d. costs.
J. Carter v. R. Kirkman (father of G. Kirkman). Charge
of abusive language. Dismissed.
R. Kirkman v. K. J. Carter. Charge of insulting lan-
guage. Dismissed.
R. Kirkman v. J. Paris. (Charge of inciting W. Carter
to do violence with a whip.) Dismissed.
G. Kirkman v. VV. R. Carter. (Charge of insulting lan-
guage.) Dismissed.
W. Carter v. R. Kirkman. (Charge of abusive lan-
guage.) Dismissed.
R. Kirkman v. E. J. Carter. Trouble about goods sold
and delivered. Dismissed.

Even if ib were true —which it doesn’t
seem to be—.that Treasurer Lyne addressed Miss
Grace Watson's friend Mrs. Smith as plain
“Smith,” there would be some excuse for his con-
duct. Supposing the same lady had been maid to
one of Grace Watson’s rich Anti-Sosh friends at
Toorak she would have been called “Smith”
quite as a regular thing. And if she had been
kitchen toiler at the same place she would have
been called “Mary Ann.” Miss Grace Watson’s
Anti-Sosh friends at Toorak are so lacking in
manners that “ Smith ” or “ Mary Ann ” is quite
common with them.
■* * * *
Waybaclc Queensland has come forward
with a special, exclusive, home-grown grievance.
Very few townships have more than one doctor ;
and when he is called away—on a law case, for
instance—the township just has to take any old
medicine that it happens to have on hand ; and as
occasionally the con-
sequences haven’t
been satisfactory, it
wants the Govern-
ment to appoint a
relieving doctor who
will drop in at such
times, and deal out
drugs in due form.
At Bouliasome time
ago, it is mentioned
by way of illustra-
tion, a couple of
men were com-
mitted to stand
their trial on a
charge of having
killed a blackfellow
at Bedourie. The
Boulia doctor ex-
amined the abori-
gine’s bones and
gave evidence as to
the cause of death.
Subsequently lie had
to go right away to
Charters Towers for
the Criminal Court
trial. There were
six or seven patients, some of them seriously
ill, in the local hospital, yet he had to leave
them to do the best they could for three
weeks. Last month at Wmton, another one-
doctor town, typhoid was very prevalent, but the
place was ten days without a physician, as the
local man had to go to Charters Towers in a
Supreme Court case. Places such as Cloncurry
and Georgetown, where mining accidents are
always a possibility, are often days without their
doctor, who probably is away explaining things
about a corpse to a jury at Norman ton.
A Bendigo (Vic.) jury has given £75 libel
damages to John Neeld, commercial traveller and
birdicide, who shoots pigeons under the romantic
name of “Locksley.” The defendant, a publican,
had accused him of being in league with the
books when he did murder most fowl, and
of making money—making Loeksley’s Haul
in fact —out of the alleged conspiracy between
him and Moses. This attempt to cast a
slur on the untarnished honor of his dead
bird roused Locksley to anger, hence the proceed-
ings. The jury’s decision clears Locksley of all
suspicion save that of being a habitual pursuer of
a heartless and callous game which ought to be
made punishable with three months in gaol.
Luck occasionally comes the way of the
poor sailorman, as witness the North Coast
(N.S.W.) boat Noorebar, which in August last,
running down the coast, found £40,000 worth of
property in the shape of the big steamer Komura,
sugar-laden, drifting on to the rocks near Smoky
Cape, with a broken tail shaft. Dozens of vessels
pass Smoky every day, and it was the Noorebar’s
luck to be the first to see the lame duck’s rockets.
After a heavy and risky tow the two vessels
reached Newcastle, and the rescuers promptly
fired a writ at the rescued. After a tussle in the
courts the little coasting boat gets a verdict of
£2OOO. Most of it, as usual, goes to the company,
which did nothing but spend a little extra on coal;
still the skipper gets a year’s salary (£250) for one
night’s work, the mate and engineer each get a
£5O-note, and the inferior ratings cut up £250
among them.
THE CUP THAT CHEERS (THE
BOOKMAKER).
A. man for whom the Victorian police had
vainly searched for 10 days strolled into the
Melbourne watch-house the other evening,
and, after getting himself arrested, told a
most amazing narrative. He had been manager
of a suburban pawnshop, and disappeared
on October 6; also £lOOO worth of household
treasures and coin disappeared at the same
time or soon after. His tale was that, being
in the city on October 5, he encountered two
strangers, who urged him to go with them to
Mentone, in Bent’s electorate, and price some
diamonds they had for sale. He said they had
some drinks, then went to Mentone, where they
had more drinks—several more drinks —and after
that he knew no more till he found himself in the
scrub next morning. Then he missed his keys,
and learning from the papers that the pawnshop
had been raided, became afraid to return. Also
he narrated that he had wandered about the scrub
in that locality for the whole ten days without
anvthing to eat or drink. It was a large size in ex-
planations, and takes a deal of swallowing ; but
still a lot of unusual things have happened in
Bent’s electorate.
THE AD. IRRESISTIBLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A firm of auctioneers offers for sale residential blocks
at Kogarah, setting forth in the advt. that they are
“ situated close to the premises of Mr. Joseph Garruthers.’’
You may talk of Swanee Rivers and of old Kentucky
homes,
Of the Fauburg of St. Martin and Lhe mansions of Mayfair,
Of the bosky dells that track you through the lines of
many pomes,
Of the water fronts of Mosman, and their beauties rich
and rare ;
But the very best credential
Of the site that’s residential
Is to hear a loud shrill yapping as the weeds you daily
hoe,
For he’s got the best position
Who is near a politician—
So come along to Kogarah and live near Joe.
The views are quite magnificent ; the soil is just the
thing,
The ground is nice and level, and the blocks are broad
and deep ;
While the drains are simply perfect, and the flowers in
early Spring
Throw a perfume o’er the landscape where the songbirds
trill and cheep.
But these things are merely' trifles
To a tower as big' as Eiffel’s
To the fascinating facts that make our hearts in rapture
glow ;
For there’s chances to erect a
House from which to look on ’Ector—
So come along to Kogarah and live near Joe.
For, if you’re an early' riser, you can get a glimpse of ’Ec.
In the backyard as he’s standing with the soap beside the
tap.
You can see that chief unbending as he bends to wash his
neck,
And on Saturday's and Sundays you can listen to the yap
When his speeches he’s rehearsing,
And the Commonwealth he’s cursing,
And your blood runs cold to hear the awful tale of Sydney’s
woe ;
For with drama we’ll provide you—
There’s Bland Holt laid on beside .you—
So come along to Kogarah and live near Joe,
N.S.’.V.

to
“ Bondite ” makes a few remarks concern-
ing the impertinence of certain young persons who
presumed to criticise their betters
Bitter experience had a lot to do with the Anti-Sosh
Women’s Congress refusal to meet Mrs. Macnaniara, of
the Sydney Socialists, in debate. The Anti-Sosh League
in Melbourne, which is the head and forefront of the whole
weird body, encountered trouble a couple of years back
over that same kind of thing. Its hired lady lecturer
had been doing a round of the suburbs, and her stunts in
Toorak and Malvern and St. Kilda hadn’t left a dry eye
in the whole assemblage. Then the Council got reckless
and decided that it was up to the lady-lecturer to get busy
in the industrial suburbs, and impart the elements of social
science to the lowly factory girl. Collingwood, it decided,
would be a nice place in which to start the Uplifting pro-
cess, and the hall was filled to the doors with Emma and
Jane and other damsels. Both the lady lecturer and the
secretary felt sorry for these poor girls, who had ob-
viously swallowed all that was told them by the rude,
brutal Socialist, and, in their blind and heathen ignor-
ance, had probably voted the Labor ticket. The lady
lecturer made the speech easy to understand, as be-
fitted the audience, and did her best for a solid hour
and a half. There wasn’t an interruption. The
girls seemed deeply impressed, but she was afraid it was
only stupidity ; and, with a view' to helping them out of
their darkness, the chairman announced that she would
now answer any questions the ladies present might desire to
ask. “ Right,” remarked a smart-looking Emma in busi-
ness-like tones as she stepped forward from the back. “ I
would like to ask the lady the name of the Socialist
writer she was quoting when she said that bit about
women being the property of the State, to be distributed
among the males by a department.” There was a hurried
confab between the lecturer and the secretary, and at
last the former explained that the quotation was from
Snitehski, the eminent Polish Socialist, 'writer. “Excuse
me,” interposed the questioner, “Snitehski is an
anarchist, and that quotation was taken from his work,
‘The Fallacies of Socialism.’” There was an awkward
pause. Then a voice was heard from the rear of the hall:
“I would like the lady to explain why she confuses use-
value with exchange-value. The distinction is one of the
most elementary economic facts, and entirely disposes of
the greater part of her argument about the relation of
productivity and wages.” There was another confab and
another explanation, a trifle more shaky and confused
than the economics had been. It was hardly finished
before a third Emma arose in the body of the hall. “ Will
the lady say from what part of Karl Marx’s works she
obtained the quotation about the marriage tie. I have
read all Marx’s works and I can’t place it.” The chairman
beamed delightedly as he saw a chance. “ Perhaps it has
not been translated,” he suggested. “No,” replied
Emma ; “ but I read all Marx’s books in German.” There
was no more asking for questions. The secretary moved
a hurried vote of thanks, and signalled deliriously to the
man at the gas-meter to lower the lights. Thereafter
Anti-Sosh restricted its efforts to Toorak and St. Kilda,
and the industrial suburb had to seek its humor on the
kerbstone as of yore.
* * * *
Judges on circuit in the backblocks are
sometimes a good deal worried about the midnight
reposefulness of their lodgings. One of them
lately put his head out of his bedroom window in
a western N.S.W. town and feelingly expostulated
with two young people vJko were vowing eternal
affection on the footpath underneath. “I have
most arduous duties before me to-morrow,”
pleaded his Honor (there was a larceny case on
the list), “ and do please let me have a sleep.”
Another threatened to cancel a publican’s license
if he didn’t silence some singers in a neighboring
room. Their Honors should congratulate them-
selves that they did not do circuit 30 or 40 years
ago. In those days most pubs kept open all night
at court time for the refreshment of lawyers, wit-
nesses, criminals, etc., ahd were noisily and fight-
ingly open besides. The late Chief-Justice Alfred
Stephen tells of losing sleep for three nights
running owing to the drinking, fighting, dancing
and singing that went on in the rooms about him.
And in much more recent years a minor Judge,
who went down in his dressing gown and spoke
with unusual pomp to a crowd of tank-sinkers in
the bar, was suddenly upended and flicked with
the bar towel'where the tank-sinkers thought it
would do most good.

South Australia is discovering that the
worst use you can make of a man may be to put
him in gaol. A local M.P., who went wrong two
years ago, was the one person who had made a
systematic effort at stopping the Coorong from
silting up. Since his compulsory retirement, says
the Board of Agriculture, the Murray mouth is
50yds. wider than it was; two enormous sandhills
have disappeared; and a small boat can hardly
pass where there used to be 30ft. of water. “In
a very short time the mouth will be probably four
miles wide, and so shallow that it will be possible
for a pedestrian to wade from Middleton to
Kingston, a distance of nearly 90 miles.” The
Surveyor-General agrees, and Government action
is to be taken to save that fisherman’s paradise, the
Coorong, which is an enormous lagoon running
eastward from the Murray, just inside the sea-
shore.
* * * *
The strip of soup between Port Mel-
bourne and Williamstown (Vic.) is churned up by
the most vivacious ferry service on earth—
when it runs. Sometimes, however, it
doesn’t run, but is scratched for many en-
gagements. There are, or ought to be,
four palatial, but tired, old sea monsters
conducting the service, though three of
them have lately been on furlough. Two,
the old Gem and Bald Rock —the latter a
superannuated green paddle veteran from
Balmain, which went Cabbage-gardening
when Sydney declined to stand it any longer
—have been out of the public eye for some
months on account of old age or acute
dementia, or something, while the other,
the Queen, lias been in dry dock for a
considerable time, suffering from pains in
the joints ; so the ’Town-to-Port or Port-
to-’Town passenger has been rushed across
the stew in a genial little tub with a motor
engine, which is always ready to spring
some violent excitement upon the passen-
gers. Occasionally it knocks off work en
route, and it takes quite a heap of time
and profanity to induce it to resume its
labors. Once—and that was just recently
—it struck work when within a few feet
of its berth at Port Melbourne, and only
some hastily-flung ropes saved it from
utter demolition against a dead end. For
the rest of the day it was defunct to the
world, so that the momentous Melbourne
Ferries Service Co., which owns the foolish
vehicle, had to hire a steam tug to carry
on the traffic. It seems about time that
State Enterprise hired a hearse to carry off
the co. without providing compensation to
its widows.
The National Mutual Life Association
people—the late Colonel Templeton’s office—got
a surprise the other morning when they found a
£lO,OOO libel writ waiting on the doorstep. Life
assurance offices get used to all sorts of claims turn-
ing up (even the man who is passed as a specially
select “life” to-day is liable to appendicitis or
orange-peel on the footpath to-morrow), but libel
writs don’t often come their way; those things are
the comfort and salvation of the newspaper man,
who otherwise wouldn’t have a thing in the world
to think about. Randolph Bedford, it seems, made
a few remarks about the Citizens’ Life people,
especially with regard to their industrial assurance
business, and the National Mutual is alleged to
have passed the remarks on to the world. Bed-
ford says he feels annoyed about the turn affairs
have taken, because it looks as though the
Citizens’ don’t believe he has got £lO,OOO. Judg-
ing by the counsel engaged, the National Mutual
isn’t going to take it sitting down ; and mean-
time, for the other office’s greater comfort, the
Citizens’ intimates that, whatever damages it
gets, it is going to pay them over to the industrial
policy-holders—the people whom Bedford said he
felt sorriest for.
The Sydney rockchoppers, in spite of
Wade’s Act, are striking again. The rock-
choppers are the men who cut tunnels through
the Hawkesbury sandstone whenever water and
sewerage or any such work is being carried out.
Theirs is one of the most dangerous and un-
healthy occupations known in Australia, and the
men who undertake it can dictate almost any
terms. Last year they intimated to the em-
ployers, chiefly the Water and Sewerage Board,
that they would only work for six hours a day.
They struck, and they got their six-hours day.
They wanted more for six hours than they used to
get for eight. Another strike, and they got their
higher wage. Various small strikes have occurred
since, and last week one member of their union was
found breaking the union rules. He was fined, and
the Water and Sewerage Board found it convenient
to hastily pay the fine. The secretary of the union,
however, told the secretary of the Board that
he wasn’t going for the cheque ; the cheque would
have to be brought to him. The Board’s secre-
tary swallowed this, but when a laconic message
reached him to remove the offender, the secretary
refused. Whereupon every chopper on the
Water Board’s contracts downed tools. The
Board talked of prosecutions under Wade’s Act,
whereupon every rock-chopper in Sydney and
suburbs dropped his tools also. The various
contractors are looking round helplessly for
more rock-choppers, but can’t get them.
Rock-chopping lias mighty little to recommend it
as a means of earning an honest coffin ; and if
there’s anything in the law of supply and
demand, which the daily papers talk so much
about to workmen, then it is pretty obvious that
even now the conditions are depressing enough
at the price. If rock-chopping had any points
about it the short hours and the wages would
bring a swarm of applicants.
* * * *
“ F.” : The train service from Caulfield on
Cup Day was delayed to admit of the State
Governor and suite being accommodated with a
“special.” Hundreds of people who hoped to get
away before the last race was run, found a saloon
carriage with engine attached, drawn up at the
platform in readiness for his tardy Ex. Mean-
while, for his precious “special” the line had
been cleared. In Victoria, rather more than in
the other States, perhaps, the railway system
dances attendance upon distinguished “dead-
heads,” of whom Bent is the most frequent cause
of inconvenience to the public. And how the
railway servants of the public kow-tow to these
non-productive passengers ! Verily, there is more
rejoicing over one free passenger than over ninety
and nine mere persons who have bought their
tickets.

THE LONE LOVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W l ROM here the world
LJ was once so fair,
-4L The skies were ever
tender then,
The clouds that laced the
horizon ’were
Suffused with kindliness
for men,
And sea and air, and sky
and tree
Bent hearts of friendliness
to me.
The gods were living, all
was right,
The wanton birds sang
wonder lays,
The blossoms brake from,
sheer delight,
And beauty couched in
many bays;
But Love that sat with me
has flown,
And now I sit and brood
alone.
The sky is an infernal
glare,
The clouds roll up in
senseless drill,
The sea is flat, and dull,
and bare.
The cursed birds are
never still;
The lisp of waves along the
shore
Js an intolerable bore.
The twisted ti-tree looks like bones
Of ghastly cripples dead and gone;
The spiky grass, these gravel stones
Are beastly things to couch upon;
And as for Nature's brooding trees —•
Well, she can stow her sympathies !
Vic.
’l'is folly prating of the grace
Of green hills bending to the sea ;
Why do I seek this wretched place ?
Because, in truth, I cannot be
So absolutely in despair,
So wholly dismal otherwhere.
E.D.
IMPOLITE OR IMPOLITIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. Mercer, of Tasmania, is evidently far from
convinced that chivalry always prohibits the laying on of
hands in the case of a woman. Lecturing at Melbourne
on chivalry and politeness, he said : “ I have met a woman
who would be a match for Tommy Burns. I trembled
greatly. I felt that even in a fair fight I might not have
a chance. The problem arose—Should I hit her ? ” The
Bulletin concludes from the fact that Bishop Mercer
doesn’t struggle around on crutches that he decided not
to hit her, or that, if he did hit her, he did it suddenly
and then ran hard.
And still the problem grim returns—
If he confronts a woman new,
Who’s got a punch like Tommy Burns,
And who for war and carnage yearns,
What should a gentle bishop do ?
If woman’s sensitive and weak,
Is clinging, tender, young and slight,
To kick her hard and make her shriek,
Or smite her on the other cheek,
Would be a course most impolite.
If, on the other hand, she’s out
With mighty fist and half a brick,
Is prone to bang, and bite, and clout, >
Then for a bishop soft and stout •
That course would be impolitic.
VIC.
SILAS SNELL.
THE PREMIER’S INEBRIATES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When theN.S.W. Assembly was counted out last evening
the Premier’s Inebriates Bill was being discussed. —
Sydney Daily Paper.
By all the Furies and the Fates
Who govern empires, reaims, and states ;
And by Beelzebub who sates
The thirst of fools and runagates ;
I rise to ask as one who hates
The humbug which he contemplates—
Who are those poor unfortunates,
The Premier’s Inebriates?
Should he who wearisomely prates
Of sacrifices, and who “ slates,”
Rebukes, reviles, and oft berates
The actions of the other States,
And he who from the Estimates
The deepest woe anticipates,
Be classed with those degen.rates,
The Premier’s Inebriates?
Is he who virtuously inflates
His bosom and reiterates
The same old story, and relates
How ginger-ale oft elevates
Our aspirations, and abates
The thirst for beer in kegs and crates —
Is he among those profligates,
The Premier’s Inebriates?
Are they whom Charles educates
Till they are finished graduates
In censuring the'sister States,
And whose abuse one’s conscience grates—
Are they whose whine.perpetuates
The rancor it precipitates—
Are they those poor unfortunates,
The Premier’s Inebriates ?
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

From “ Bunny Bill ” :
Pore old “ Oict! ” He gets sum rummy idees inter his
brane reeeptiekle at times. He ses (B. 1/10/’C>B), “ should
he” (meanin’ a blo-fiy) “ buzz in large numbers on a hot
summer day when other sentient things are decorously
comatose ”* (wot disgustin’ langwidge, enyhow), “the
cocky will predict a fall of rain within three days and be
right every time.” Lawd only knows whare “Oict”
gained his iggsperience with big game frum. I’ll bet
enythink he’s never bin out campin’ or boardin’ at a see-
side coffee-pallis. I’m an aged an’veneerable man now,
an’ my eggsperience has shown that th’ blo-fly gets out
his buzzer ’nd starts buzzin’ on th’ first day that gives him
eny hope of summer follerin’ on. Shood he start later,
well, he’ll keep on buzzin’ like L, th’ hole three weeks of
flamin’ drj' wether that usually follers ; an’ then th’ rane
cums. “ Oict ” must’ve started takin’ an interist in blo-
fiies about the eighteenth day. Enyhow, the only re-
liarble rane-sine I can tell him of is one handed
down from my grate-grate-grandfather. Take, borrer,
or acquire an ordinary respectible, suburbin waterin’-can.
Place it on a plot uv newly-turned earth. About two
yards distant fix up alance-wood fishun’-rod with th’ butt
end about six inches in th’ ground. Greeze the inside uv
the can with salid oyle, ’nd havin’ attached a cord ter
th’ top joint uv th’ rod, tie the other end to an ordinary
lady’s hare-brush (with tortus-shelled back preferred).
Next take a length uv skippin’ rope belongin’ to a school-
girl uv 13, attach same ter th’ back handel uv th’ can, an’
chuck the other end over th’ lowest boughs uv a ring-
barked bamboo tree. Havin’ filled th’ can with water,
place a pare uv steel-rimmed spectickles with one glass
missin’ upon th’ front dore step. Then place a cucumber
in your top vest pocket. Take your pipe firmly between
th’ teeth ’nd haul on th’ rope till th’ can’s well off th’
ground. A slight shower is almost sure t’ toiler.

CUTTING THE PAINTER.
“ Edyson ” : Reading some of the facts
about child-life in the backblocks, provided by
that N.S.W. sub-committee of the Public School.
Teachers’ Association, I am reminded of an
experience of my own in the Goulburn district? of
Victoria. I was resting and recuperating at
Coy’s, and late one night went ’possuming with a
native. At about midnight we came upon a
small boy,- who might have been nine, messing
about with rabbit traps on a hillside. I invited
the youth’s confidence. Wasn’t it time a kid like
him was in bed. He said there was lots of time.
He was going to turn in when he had fixed them
traps and skinned a “ string of kittens.” How
far was it to his home ? “ Oh, not fur ; not more’n
five mile.” Returning, we struck the same
youngster on the same hill. It was now about a
quarter past five, and the working boy was
gathering the early rabbit. “Now, look here,”
I said, severely, “haven’t you gone to bed yet?”
He regarded me with scorn. “Cripes !” he said,
“d'yeh think a bloke lives in bed? Why, I’ve
on’y jist got up.”
Some recollections of Black Brother ;
During the trial in Brisbane of the officers of the black-
birder Hopeful, charged with murder, the writer saw a
South Sea Islander, with bushy, lime-bleached hair
bunched round his head, in the witness-box, leaning
forward, looking gravely with wide-opened eyes at Chief
Justice Lillev. Among the questions asked before he was
sworn were these: “You good boy?” “Yes, I mis-
sionary boy.” “You know Jehovah?” “Yes.” “Did
you ever see Him?” “Not before to-day,” replied the
witness, reverently regarding the wigged and gowned
Judge.
The next witness for the prosecution was Messiah, the
ship’s cook, an educated American negro. Chubb, counsel
for the defence, asked him why, when the Hopeful, on its
way to Townsville, was boarded by men-of-war officers,
he did not complain to them of the tragedy. Messiah
replied : “ It occurred to me to do so, but it also occurred
to me that if I did, when the Hopeful got to Towns-
ville the ship’s cook would be missing.”
Then said Chubb : “ Have you ever heard the line of
Shakspeare, ‘ I can smile and still be a villain ’? ” “Oh,
yes,” replied Messiah, “I thought of it when I saw you
smiling at me this morning.”
And then Chubb sat down feeling worse.
“ Flinderino ” : Cooktown is the home of
the Australian curiosity shop. Every second
business establishment in the town is a
miniature museum. (The hotels are in between.)
A display of aboriginalities is on view in almost
every shop window, and is good bait for the visit-
ing tourist from the south, who always carts away
an assortment of King Billy accoutrements. The
dealer in curios has for sale boomerangs, waddies,
woomeras, nulla-nullas, dilly-bags, stone axes,
dug-out canoes, paddles, armlets, native fishing-
nets and lines, firesticks, several varieties of
spears, painted shields, grass girdles, ear and nose
ornaments, head decorations, charms, and dozens
of other sundries. The only aboriginally that
has to be ordered two days ahead is a corroboree.
Incidentally the shopman sells all sorts and sizes
of shells, pieces of coral, beche-de-mer, pearl
shell, tortoise shell, sharks’ teeth, stuffed alli-
gators, cocoanuts, burra-burras (match-box beans),
and a host of etceteras. All these things the
curiosity man collects at the blacks’ camp and
from the crews of beche-de-mer luggers. He
usually pays for them with plugs of cheap tobacco,
trash pocket-knives, or some other rubbish.
“ Dandalup ” writes in anger :
It made my blood , boil and my reserve vocabulary to
exhaust itself considerably when I read “ 10-2-4’s ” par
(B. 24/9/’08) re the “ Englishman in Australia” who told
the Sydney Evening News “ that Australia had no sea-
fishing for amateurs off its coast, as in the English
Channel.” I have fished in almost every State of the
Commonwealth, and my labor has been rewarded with
much spoil. Only last November I spent, with four com-
panions, three weeks on a fishing trip up the Westralian
coast about Geraldton, Shark Bay, Bernier Island and
Carnarvon. Two days after we first dropped our lines
my fingers were raw—the effect of pulling the fish aboard.
(Two 141 b. schnapper on your line in five or six fathoms of
water is no easy work.) Although, owing to very rough
weather, we only spent three daysin actual fishing during
the cruise, we caught over 650 fish, including schnapper,
cod, jewfish, tailor, and many other kinds. We also got
eight sharks, but they are not counted. My share totalled
over 100, and I’m oniy an amateur fisherman when com-
pared to some men who fish off that prolific coast. Most
of the schnapper landed were over 101 b. in weight, and
some weighed the scale down at 201 b. These big fellows
are called nor’-westers, and are beggars to pull. When
we arrived back at Geraldton we had over two tons of fish
on board, and I guess even then we left a few behind us.
Heaps of fish have been caught off the Westralian coast
and sent up to the goldfields on ice, and there are many
men even now making £4 to JEIO per week at the fishing
business. The Australian coast, and especially that of
Westralia, I consider a fishermen’s paradise. Finally,
this English Johnny who fabricated to the Sydney
Evening News is a liar.
