SO-AND-SO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(With apologies to Brunton Stephens' “ Celestial Chef")
’Twas a night of hot December, ninety-odd degrees from
zero ;
Pierce mosquitoes held high revel, and the moon was
mooning low ;
When upon the scene came cringing what I’m pleased to
call my hero —
Just a squat and ugly Ohow —by name we dubbed him
“So-and-So.”
He came sidling to us sideways, like a rooster scenting
battle.
He was hideous, with a face as hard as any anthracite ;
And a form of curves and contours liable to frighten
cattle—
As he slid into our vision on that hot December night.
We were mouthing over ghost-yarns, and occasionally
shivered
As we gazed into the darkness past our small mosquito
fire;
So that when into the light there peered a face a-grin
and livid
We were tempted to vacate, without delaying to inquire.
But he ventured forth so humbly, and his slippered feet
so slowly
Dragged and scraped along the ground as we sat gasping
in a sweat ;
And his ugly face was grinning, and his aspect seemed
so lowly
That within my midnight visions I can view him even yet.
“You want cookee?” was his question;—and we rose
and clasped him to us,
And we wept huge tears of welcome for the joys that
were to come.
“ Walk right in,” we sang in chorus ; “ walk right in—
we guess you’ll do us.
And we’ll have meat-pie to-morrow and a pudding of the
plum.”
“You give wagee?” was his query; —and we lovingly
assured him
That a king’s remuneration should be his, and nothing
less.
And with pledges and by promises we angled and we
lured him;
And e’en dared within our joy his filthy pigtail to caress.
He seemed sinless as an infant; and his eyes wentheav’n-
ward turning
As he begged us modestly our bonafldes to produce.
And we set his pigtail bobbing, and his piggy eyes
a-burning,
As we waved huge golden nuggets in his countenance
obtuse.
O we passed a pleasant evening on that hot night of
December,
As he vowed in pidgeon-English friendship, love, and
faith eterne.
And at supper-time he made us savory cakes to long
remember,
With a subtle hint of spice to start our stomachs on the
yearn.
O we went to bed delighted with the promise of the
morrow;
And we chuckled in our wisdom as we mused on So-and-
So ;
Of the pork-pies and the puddings—things to give sur-
cease from sorrow ;
And we went to sleep expectant, with our stomachs all
aglow.
In the morning, 10, he called us—had the tea and toast
a-steaming;
All his walnut face a-shining as he watched our glad
surprise.
All his infantine emotions in his coal-black eyesa-gleaming
As he set before our gasping selves as many hot meat-pies.
O we fell with zest upon them, never asking whence their
coming :
O our teeth played royal battle with the juicy hunks of
meat;
While he sat aside a-smiling, with his finger-ends a-strum-
ing,
And his almond eyes down-looking in a manner quite
discreet.
O what could we do but trust him ? O what could we do
but leave him
In proud charge of our belongings as we went to work
the patch?
Telling him our ev’ry secret, so as not to gall and grieve
him
With a hint of hard suspicion—this pure soul without a
match 1
So we swung our picks right gladly, and we chanted songs
right gaily
As we hauled the buckets brimming to the chanty of
“ Yo-ho! ”
And commented on the fact of fancy cakes and puddings
daily,
And adjudicated on the points of priceless So-and-So.
But the working hours dragged slowly, and our stomachs
waxed a-weary
Of the waiting for the twilight, when he promised we
should dine.
O we never grew so hungry as the day lagged long and
dreary,—
As our longings went to thee, celestial spirit superfine !
When we reached the camp we hailed him : “So-and-So,
dear friend, come hither 1
Let us feast our gaze upon you, and our teeth upon your
pies.
Saffron friend, we pray, come hither’’—only echo answered
“ Whither ? ”
As we searched with growing zest, and gazed askance in
our surprise.
But he came not, —O he came not 1 sweet and stately
Chinese lily !
Tho’ we called with voices drooping, and our spirits
waxing sore.
So we lit the same old camp-fire, and we boiled the same
old billy;
And we feasted on the same old bread and dripping, as of
yore.
Then it came to us so slyly, in the sugar-pot concealing ;
Game upon us in an agony of grief and cruel woe ;
Just a little note of parting, by its folded end revealing,
J ust a billet-doux of friendship from the truant So-and
so.
“Friends and brothers,” ran the missive; “tho’ an
Eastern son celestial,
By best college education have I learned the Western
wiles ;
Left behind me all my native tricks —so lowly and so
bestial;
Gathered ’round me all the glory from the city’s highest
styles.
“ I was ‘ stony ’ —so despairing, I was forced to ‘ raise the
breezes’;
It was time I did the mandarin in Sydney city’s streets ;
So I struck your camp at sunset, and wheezed out the
same old wheezes
As I’ve wheezed them o’er and o’er upon the same old
country beats.
“ But, dear friends, you were so easy, that I thought my-
self a-sleeping;
And I feared to swift awaken and discover it a dream ;
So I pity took upon you—placed your gold in safer keep-
ing,
Just to prove to you, my brothers, that things are not
what they seem.
“ Just to help me on my journey, I purloined your horse
at starting—
Don’t say ‘ steal ’; ’tis such a nasty word I—l know you
will not mind.
And the pies you ate with gusto—weep a tear-drop at the
parting,
For I guess if sought till Doomsday, Tom, the Oat, You’ll
Never Find 1
“Just a word, my friends, concluding: Let this be a
lesson to you ;
Learn that China, when she starts, is just the ’cutest
cuss alive.
If you train the wily heathen he’s in duty bound to ‘do ’
you,
To instruct you by your teaching just how many beans
make five.”
O we cursed him and his offspring 1 O we tackled his
ancestors
As we raged around the camp-fire on that hot December
night ;
And we sent him to the region where his soul must be
asbestos
If it stand the sultry climate, with the bonfires all
bedight.
In the morning by the sunrise softly, silently we
vanished :
For we’d worked the patch to death, and So-and-So our
fortune had.
Thus we set across the ranges grim, like patriots long
banished,
And we humped the lonely road to Sydney city “ on our
pad.”
N.S.W.

“ Flitiderino ” : A couple of years ago the
boxer hat was unknown in North Queensland. It
is said that a hard-hitter did show itself in Cook-
town late last century, but the wearer was pur-
sued by a mob of blacks armed with stone-axes
and dinner forks. However, the bowler, which is
the most senseless sort of hat to wear in a semi-
tropical climate, is now to be seen in hosts in the
Northern towns. A canefields ganger togged
himself up the other Sunday to go and do the
Romeo act. With saddle on his arm he gaily
vaulted the paddock fence and whistled up his
trusty steed. Blossom came at a trot, but the in-
stant it beheld its master it did a back somer-
sault and bolted in the direction of Africa. Bill
followed the noddy all over the place for an hour,
and grew desperate. His language became the
color of a murdered sunset. He had called the
scared animal 10 different kinds of a cow, and
was about to throw up the job in despair, when one
of the onlooking canecutters suggested that he
should take off his hat and plant it. Bill
tumbled. With his brand new hard-hitter hidden
behind his back, he walked quietly up to Blossom,
adjusted the saddle, and rode off to meet Juliet.
“E.A.” : “Henry Phelthatt’s ” descrip-
tion of how a bushwoman determines the real age
of an allegedly twenty-one-year-older is very
exact. The bushman’s genius for irrelevant detail
is quite as pronounced. The other day a horse-
man galloped down the road with excitement in
his whiskers and a wild glare in his eye :
“ Bill Somers’ Tom’s lorst in the scrub. Goin’ fer the
perlioe,” he shouted to a cobber in the way.
The cobber was unsatisfied.
“Here, Jim ! Hey I" he hollered.
Jim pulled up. “ Bill Somers’Tom ? Lorst f That be
d—-d.”
“By cripes, ain’t he I’’ said Jim, knowingly. “ ’E’s
lorst, all right.”
“ You don’t say ? ’Ow old wuz ’e ? ”
“Well, I can’t say exackly ’ow old ’e wuz, but you
know that there mare o’ mine ?—well, she wuz risin’ two
w’en ’e wuz a nipper. You mind the time we took them
wethers out to the mountains? That’s 17 year come
next shearin’. Well, I bought her off Paddy Shea (’im wot
used to ’ave the store w’ere Mick Nugent keeps the
pub). It was the day I swopped that there ’alf-bred kan-
garoo dawg ”
Cobber (brightly intelligent): “Not the big feller
wot got ripped that day we wuz musterin’ them steers fer
Bairnsdale ? ”
Jim (delighted): “ Y-a-a-i-i-r-r-s, that’s the cove.”
Cobber (equally delighted): “ That be d—d ! ”
Jim : “ Well that’s ’ow I reckon ’is age, anyhow, becus
that there mare wuz on’y a foal. I see ’er first standin’
over be the big gum where they used to ’ang the beasts
afore it wuz struck be lightnin’ in that big storm just afore
lamb markin’. It wuz the year that cove come up from
Goulburn an’ bought the mob o’ weaners—d’yer mind ?
The cove ” (growing excited) “ wot shouted the bottle
w’en we delivered ’em over ? ”
Cobber (thoughtfully, but with a little doubt) : “ Y-a-
-a-i-i-r-r-s.”
Jim: “ Well, that’s’im." And during the next 15 minutes
Jim managed to drag in the missus and the kids, the old
sow, one heifer, most of his dogs, the dead years and the
entire population ; and he had reached the woodheap
before he remembered that Bill Somers’Tom remained
lost, and galloped madly off again with the gale in his
whiskers.
A FRENCH TOURIST IN AUSTRALIA.
“Ze silly laughing asses, I vill destruction zem.”
A Westralian bushman was lately operated
on in a Perth hospital for appendicitis. In the
offending appendix were found 40 small balls of
solder, and some fragments of quartz. Bill
Wayout wasn’t a human ostrich ; he was simply
an eloquent example of the Tinned life. A long
course of tinned fish, tinned milk, tinned fruit,
tinned vegetables, and tinned dog had resulted in
this collection of solder. As to the samples of
quartz, they probably stuck to Bill’s damper
while it was cooking. The bush party has re-
covered to return to his tinned life ; but he hasn’t
any appendix now to catch the stray bits.
“ Polstan I allow that it is fairly re-
markable that some men can tell the time
to the minute without the aid of time-
pieces, and under any circum-
stances ; but more remarkable
still is the manner in which
birds and animals roll up
at meal times. Are they
merely ruled by hung-
er, or can they tell the
time ? There’s an old
white cockie in this
town that flies off to
bring his boss home to
dinner so regularly
every day that the
townsfolk set their
clocks by him. The
boss may be late or
early, but cockie is
there to the second. A
neighbor of mine had a
big yellow hound that
used to send up a wail
like the burst-up of Hades
at exactly four o’clock every
morning. I broke him of the
habit with a Winches-
ter at last. He w'as
a useful dog if you
wanted to catch the
early train, but as
I never wanted to
do that, I had no
use for him. Be-
sides, there was no
early train to
catch if I
had want-
ed it.
A MAN OF WEIGHT.
GINGER: "Ain’t yer goin’ to ’ave a toss, Cobber?”
COBBER: “ Toss! Blime, d’yer fink a man’s
goin ’ to be a kid orl his natural? I per-
scribes to Tatt.’s! ”
“Gilrooney ” : I have been voyaging down
the Darling River (N.S.W.) in one of those crazy
little steamboats that smoke, and fuss, and
whistle, and excitedly thresh the mighty deep
into foam without getting much further ahead.
And I have become familiar with certain signs.
For instance, when the captain (and there’s
generally about three of him on a Darling
River boat) stares intently at a well-
trodden pinnacle near the water’s edge
on the eastern bank, he is about to back-
paddle, swing to, and cast anchor, to
deliver a pound of tobacco to a lonely
fisherman. When he gazes violently at
a high, precipitous bank, which no uni-
nsured goat would attempt to climb, he
is pretty certain to stop for two hours to
cut and take aboard firewood for the
engine. (All the easy landing-places
have been denuded of fuel years ago.)
When the whole three of him manipu-
lates the steering wheel with one hand,
and holds his nose with the other, a
camel-team has just left Thargomindah
(300 miles away in Queensland) for the
Cooper or the Thompson. And when
the three captains, the cook, the crew,
and the pet kangaroo all hold their
noses with every hand they’ve got, and
let the boat steer itself, a river wool-
scour is somewhere within 50 miles. A
wool-scour, it might be mentioned, is an
appalling catastrophe. A man who
works about a wool-scour is Lost. The
smell of wool gets into his soul and turns
it black, and the yolk of wool enters his
language and turns it blue, and the
density and general cussedness of wool
impregnates his understanding and
makes it thick, and in the end he thinks
and acts like the wool-scour itself. P.S. —All
this valuable information is hereby offered gratis
to the “N.S.W. Tourist Guide”—and itcan have
the plug of tobacco as a bonus, and the precipitous
bank as an honorarium, and the camel smell as a
subsidy, and the wool-scour fragrance as an ever-
lasting annuity. It is too much knowledge forme
to hold in one lump.
■* * * *
“ Kati-Kati,” re rabbit extermination
Does any disease exterminate? Humanity has been
ravaged by deadly diseases all along, yet man is as great
a pest as ever. Disease tends to prevent overcrowding,
and removes the weak. The strong easily resist attack,
or become immune. The potato disease does not exter-
minate the potato. In unfavorable weather drops fail,
but the next year potatoes may be as cheap as ever. I
have had them die down with the blight in the autumn,
and come up again in spring and do splendidly. Again,
the groundsel is very subject to a fungus disease. In the
garden we see it die off all over the place, yet enough
always seeds to keep the place fully stocked. How would
it be for Australia to specialise, and become the rabbit
farm of the universe ? The climate seems favorable. The
only fence necessary would be the ocean. Devoting their
whole energies to the care, catching and killing of bunny,
the native born would soon develop the lissome limbs of
a cat, and the jumpiness of a kangaroo, so that trapping
and shooting would be superseded. Best of all, it would
be necessary to import everything but rabbits, and G.
Reid would die of joy.
• * * *
J. M. Hudson (M.L.) : Is it known to
science that mussels tame crabs ? On these coasts
every mussel keeps house with a pet crab. The
crab’s office is apparently to supply eyes for the
company. When the suckful schnapper ap-
proaches, the crab draws back into the shell,
thereby giving the mussel the office to bang the
front door. Ido not know whether the mussel
has adapted a special breed of crabs for this
purpose or whether it simply incarcerates and
trains young crabs that are wandering about. The
crabs are light in color and globular in abdomen. It
is possible they are pale from incarceration
(and deferred hope) and bloated from inactivity.
Why the crab doesn’t devour the mussel is a
mystery, but it is probable that the mussel has the
power of bringing pressure to bear on its partner
if the latter misbehaves. Practically every
mussel has a crab and no mussel ever has more
than one crab. The crabs have no shell, their
abdomens being quite round and soft and about
the size of a small pea.
“ Wrock ” : The Australian game of foot-
ball has pushed its way into some remote corners
of the Commonwealth. I witnessed a match re-
cently between a team of blacks from the Cum-
meragunja Mission Station (N.S. W.) and a band
of cookies’ sons from south of the Murray. The

the shady supporters,

All Blacks gathered what was

Three cheers were given for each side, the kan-
garoos streaked for the horizon, and the gins and
small fry crawled sorrowfully homewards, hurling
abuse at the umpire who, in their opinion, was
“ no plurry good.”
» « » *
Explorer “Larry” Wells, who returned
to Adelaide a few weeks ago from the Northern
Territory, after a couple of years’ hard trigono-
metrical survey work, brought with him some
splendid samples of cotton which he found grow-
ing wild at Kathleen Falls, on the Flora River,
70 miles from the Pine Creek railway station, and
50 miles from the Katherine telegraph station. The
cotton bushes had grown from seed-pods carried by
black brother from Old Delamere, on the Aroona
Creek, where Dr. Browne’s employees had previ-
ously experimented. The natives took the pods for
corroboree purposes, and the seeds dropped
under a tree a few yards from the river. Wells
says the clump was 20ft. in diameter and 10ft.
high. The rainfall thereabouts is from 50in. to
70in. a year, and the water goes over Flora Falls
at the rate of 100,000,000 gallons in 24 hours. A
good flow is continuous throughout the year, and
Wells made enough observations to satisfy him
that the land could be easily irrigated, and that
there was a large area suitable for cotton-growing.
“ Victor Wynne ” : Allow me to hold forth
on the theme of the N. Queensland stinging tree.
It is soft and succulent, with large, round leaves,
on which are the minutest of bristles. It looks
harmless. Touch it, however, and you learn
the difference. It stings without any trouble
through a stocking, and even through light
clothes, and one experience of its bite makes
the victim wary for life. The pain is ex-
cruciating for an hour or two. A nip on the
hand causes a painful swelling under the armpit,
and one on the leg ensures a like disturbance in
the groin. The affected part will irritate for days
and weeks, and on bringing it into contact with
cold water the sting can be felt often for months
afterwards. Cattle will brush past the stinging
tree without injury, but with horses it is some-
how different. The stinging tree cost many
a good steed his life in the early days
when pack teams travelled from the coast
to the Atherton and Herberton districts. The
horse is liable to brush up against a tree on
the edge of a narrow track. Wild and frightened
at the sudden pain, it rushes headlong into the
scrub, and probably encounters more stinging
tree. Finally, blind with agony, it rushes into
trees and fallen logs, and eventually becomes
completely entangled in tenacious lawyer vines
and dies from exhaustion. There are numerous
cases of men, while working in the scrub naked to
the waist, being stung, and in some instances the
victim has had to be strapped down to prevent
him running amok. In my opinion the State
ought to offer a reward for the scalp of the
stinging tree.

“ L’Fou ” writes about the Martyrdom of
Cow :
I know Cow is regarded as a pampered joss to whom
sweated worshippers are sacrificed, and to sympathise
with her argues yourself a pal of Belial. But here (on a
big, casual sort of dairy farm, where breeds are mixed
enough to please even a Smith) I am haunted by the faces
in the yard. If I was bear-drawer Lindsay, or that
feline expounder, Souter, I’d specialise in cows. There
are possibilities in cow physiognomy if people could only
get past their prosaic traditions. There is the new heifer,
wild-eyed and graceful as a deer, nosing the fence
for a chance of escape. There is a sanctimonious
old bovine that gets up mothers’ meetings all over the
paddock, and an aggressive cow with broken horns that
just as regularly disperses them. There is the ethereal
group, thin with a thinness not of this world, and with
more than a hint of tuberculosis ; the Spiteful cows that
surreptitiously gore their neighbors; the placid, benign
animals we all hear of, and a score of other types. But
over all is the sorrowful blank look that is “ past caring.”
I don’t know whether it is the eternal dragging at the
milk-fountain, or the constant forced motherhood, or the
dogs, or the mud, or what. And it isn’t a drought-stricken
herd with an improvident owner who has been trusting
to luck. The same mournful aspect may be observed
when the animal is in front of the biggest armful of
lucerne ever cow gazed upon.
But it is these spring nights that make me real maudlin
sentimental. Then a line of bereaved mothers congre-
gate around and bellow in varying piteous keys for their
offspring, and the hungry calves (which don’t take to
hand-feeding right off) reply in a minor key of desolation.
If there is a cessation of the misery, it is broken by the
jangling of wire fences as a horned head tests their
strength, or by the dog barking and rattling his chain.
And through it all—and through the papered-hessian
walls—Mr. Oreamcans and the whole Oreamcans family
snore peacefully, as if no heart-broken mother 'were
padding restless up and down beyond the fence, and no
cold calf with but a straw to lie on were fretting in the
unstilly dark without. The Martyrdom of Cow is a very
real martyrdom indeed.

A SIMPLE PRESCRIPTION.
Said to Cuke Rheumatism, Kidney Trouble
and Bladder Troubles.
We know that the readers of this paper appre-
ciate good advice, and we are glad, therefore, to
be able to publish the following from a prominent
physician.
It is astonishing to know the prevalence of
kidney trouble and other diseases, such as rheu-
matism, bladder and liver troubles, which are
almost invariably caused by weak kidneys. It is
said on good authority that fully one-third of the
people in Australia who have reached the age of
maturity are more or less afflicted v/ith kidney
trouble in some form. The most dangerous
feature of this disease is the fact that many
people have it without their knowledge, and con-
sequently neglect it until it has reached a danger-
ous stage.
The least symptom of kidney trouble, such as
backache, must be given prompt attention, and a
good reliable treatment should be taken, until
every trace of the disease has been eliminated.
Wg are glad to be able to give our readers a pre-
scription which we have seldom known to fail in
any case. It contains no harmful ingredients, is
pleasant to take, not expensive, and can be
supplied by any good chemist. It is as follows
Tincture Gentian Compound, one ounce;
Syrup Rhubarb, one ounce ; Liquid Barkola Com-
pound, one ounce ; Syrup of Ginger, five ounces.
Take a teaspoonful after each meal, and one at
bedtime.
We are glad to publish this prescription, as we
know it will help our afflicted readers who give it
a fair _ trial. If taken continuously for a few
weeks it will be found to be an almost absolutely
certain cure for rheumatism,, kidney and bladder
trouble, backache, and all allied complaints.
Note.—Other papers are privileged to copy the
above.,

V Solomon, the just departed S. Austra-
lian Parliamentarian, kept up his habit of quick
repartee even on his deathbed. He was tapped

EDWIN J. BRADY , POET, &c.
Died recently at Inverell (N.S.W.), at the
reputed and tidy old age of a century and six
months, Patrick O’Sullivan. The ancient was a
cousin of Daniel Henry Deniehy. He married
over 60 years ago, and his living descendants num-
ber 41. They comprise six sons, two daughters,
twenty-five grandchildren, and eight great-grand-
children.

A down-Soufch Parliament struck a painful
coincidence the other day. A former legislator,
who now looks upon the wine when it is red or
any other old color, stalked into the President’s
gallery in the Legislative Council Chamber with a
nxed glare in his rosy eye; and at the same
moment the Clerk announced that the subject for
debate was the Inebriates Bill. The ex-legislator
supplied the necessary awful example.
Some of the remarks which Prime Minister
Deakin will make about David Syme in his intro-
duction to the Syme biography are interesting.
Deakin's judgment is that Syme wasn’t the im-
placable person he has been often pictured.
‘A fierce adversary,” he says, “yet I do not
remember any vendetta that he was not willing to
conclude if openly approached, and hardly an
enemy with whom he remained continuously on
bad terms.”
Governor Le Hunte had the chance to stay
for an extended term as Governor of S. Aus., but
had to say “No.” He will leave behind him a solid
popularity amongst children and country folk ;
for lie has visited more schools and more town-
ships than any other man who ever held the vice-
regal job in the Wheat State. He does best at
Show dinners and such like functions, for,
although a wretched speaker, he is “a plain, blunt
man, who talks the sort of stuff that the farmer
likes to hear—about cabbages, pigs and the price
of oats.
Barrister Percy It. Waddy, one of the
well-known cricketing brotherhood, has abandoned
the law and taken up in its stead the Town
Clerkship of Deniliquin. In the matter of law-
abandonment the young man has extensive
precedent to back him ud. The list of barristers
who have chucked up their Chambers and taken
to other jobs than Bar-breasting is as long as a
bill of Costs. But in another respect the new
Town Clerk has established a record. He is the
only holder of the office in N.S.W. who is legally
entitled to keep his skull hot with horsehair.
Ex-Sergeant R. R. Ware, who, 40 years
ago, was in charge of the police at Maryborough
(Q-)> has been celebrating his 80th birthday by re-
counting the story of the discovery of gold at
Gy in pie in 1867. James Nash, the discoverer,
marched into the police office and dumped down
on the table a bag of nuggets. Nash had been
working on the Calliope, but met with an acci-
dent, and had to go to the hospital. On re-
covery he made off towards Gympie. The
prospects looked well, but for fear that he
might cause hardship by attracting men to
another duffer, he raised no commotion about
his luck till he was satisfied that the field was
good. Then Ware and others inspected the new
claim, and had a dig at it themselves. Amongst
thefindswasas7oz. nugget discovered by Ware him-
self. On that the news was given out at Mary-
borough. Ware ran short of miners’ rights in no
time, although he had 100 on hand, so he used
ordinary foolscap. Before he got any sleep he
had issued 1100. It was a memorable rush that
followed. A single ship from Maoriland brought
900 passengers.
Melb. ’ : Lately-deceased Bromley,
M.L.A., was the financial ruin of Maurice
Brodzky and his Table Talk. Yet Brodzky is
alone to be blamed. The paradoxical saying is
that when the man who never makes a mistake
makes one it is worse than anybody else’s.
Brodzky published a senseless, profitless libel on
Bromley. It is said that an apology would have
settled this. However, the obstinate man in-
sisted on fighting, and was cast in heavy damages
and Costs. This knocked him out. He said to
me, “ I’m a ruined man,” and that was all about
it. Table Talk was sold up. Brodzky first came
before the public about 30 years ago, in a Jewish
amateur performance of “Not a Bad Judge.”
Then he got on the Herald. He married Miss
Leon, daughter of a Geelong pawnbroker, and it
was her little dot that started Table Talk.
Brodzky told me that he fought on the French
side in the Franco-German war.
“Monty” Faithfull, who died
in Sydney last week, was something of a solicitor
(his firm was Iceton, Faithfull and Maddock), and
more of a sportsman. There was hardly any
branch of sport at which “Monty ” didn’t excel
at one period or other of his 61-year-long life.
Old-time cricketers talk with bated breath of his
bowling performances. He could have played for
his native land against England, on his ball-
hurling alone, had he wished it. He rode, drove,
card-played and raced with enthusiasm. He was
one of the best field-shots in the State. Also he
was a deplorably good hand at pigeon-slaving.
With his tall, soberly-clad figure, and his solemn,
benevolent-looking, side-levered face, he looked a
curious anomaly as he stepped to his far-back
mark at the N.S.W. Gun Club’s traps. The
spectacle was vaguely suggestive of a parson call-
ing for strong drink in a pub. Nevertheless—or,
perhaps, accordingly—the club made him its presi-
dent, and he held that office to the end.
The death of Yaiben Solomon removes the
finest beard from S. Australian politics. It was a
black and glossy bush, and the owner was proud
of it. S. Australia probably owns more public
whiskers than any other State. It is time imme-
morial since it even had a clean-shaven man on the
Supreme Court Bench. The greatest of its Judges
for 30 years past, Way and Boucaut, never shaved
at all. (By the way, the clean-shaven Judge is fast
disappearing in other places, too; only Barton
represents the hairless tradition on the High
Court Bench.) For over 30 years S. Australia has
also, with one exception, grown whiskers—and
mostly a full crop—on its Premiers. John
Downer was the one exception. Price, Solomon,
Kingston, Playford, Colton, Boucaut, belong to a
longdine of Premiers who never had to get up 10
minutes earlier and pull their faces out of shape.
Of this lot, Solomon was probably most jealous of
Colton ; but in his political days Colton’s jaw-mat
was more like a long silver tail. And no doubt
the prevalence of whiskers in S. Australia doesn’t
merely happen ; the bushy face tells a good deal
of the population’s bucolic character, and of its
general disinclination to spend much time on its
personal adornment.

Died last week, at Kiama (N.S. W.), where
Parkes’ ghost used to flap its wings over the Blow-
hole every election-time, Dr. Kinross, for 25 years
I rincipal of St. Andrew’s College within the

The lately-deceased Vaiben Louis Solomon
once threatened to punch Kingston's head, and
was very near making the attempt. That was in
the S.A. Parliament, and the betting was 5 to 1
he would be killed if he tried the experiment.
Kingston in those days was a bitter orator, and
. Solomon’s Northern Territory Times had lost
three Chinese advertisers, so Solomon’s temper
was somewhat sour. Solomon’s first and last
Ministerial office was the Premiership of the
Sudden Ministry, which lasted seven days. His
elevation, instead of mellowing him, set his nerves
on edge ; by the fourth day of his greatness he was
an embittered man, and enemies sprang up like
blades of grass. At the first Federal election
(1901), when S.A. was a single constituency,
returning seven Representatives, Solomon got in
as sixth man behind Kingston, Bonvthon, Glynn
Holder and Batchelor. At the 1903 election he
contested Boothby against Batchelor, and there
was no more Sudden Solomon in Federal politics.
In 1905 he recaptured his old N. Territory seat in
the S.A. Assembly. The Territory had two
members for 1070 electors, as compared with
Adelaide’s four members for 27,179 voters, so
Solomon represented bare spaces mostly.
By this paper’s Oldest Inhabitant:—
Mr. 11. M. Faithfull, the “Monty Faithfull” of the
University cricket team of 30 years ago, when Tom Gar-
ret, Tommy Iceton, Joe Coates and other cracks were at
the game and taking high rank with bat and ball, died on
Thursday, after a long illness, at his residence, Elizabeth
Bay, Sydney. He was aged 61 years. Mr. Faithfull was
grandson of William Faithfull, who was connected with
the old New South Wales Regiment in the early days of
the colony. It was quite by accident that he found him-
self in Sydney instead of in Spain. He had enlisted with
the intention of joining some regiment then fighting in
the Peninsula, but was tagged on to tne N.S.W. (or Ruin-
selling) Corps; which, after all, was for the best. On
leaving the regiment he became manager of Colonel
Foveaux’s properly, and eventually settled on the
Ilawkesbury. In 1804 he married Miss Susannah Pitt,
whose family had just come to the country. One of the
sons, William Pitt Faithfull, settled at Springfield, near
Goulburn, and there “Monty” was born, his mother
being a Miss Deane, whose family came to Australia in
183/. Monty was a student of St. Paul’s College, and a
graduate of the Sydney University. In the early
’seventies he was admitted as a solicitor and went into
business with his old club-mate, Thomas H. Iceton. The
latter died last May. Mr. Faithfull married a daughter
of “Old Tom” Buckland, a director of the Bank of New
South Wales.
Old F. C. Brewer, printer, reporter and
journalist in and about Sydney for about 70 years,
received congratulations the other day on achieving
his 82nd birthday. He is now blind, but, like
Milton, sees clearly with the mind’s eye. His
articles of indenture show that he began the
printing craft under Edward Smith Hall, of the
Monitor, a pen-fighter of the early days. Hall
—Monitor Hall as he was called—paid fines for
libel when he could and went to gaol when
he couldn’t. Wentworth, then beginning at
the Bar, took up the ancient printer’s distresses*
and with Justice Dowling on the Bench, turned
the tables on Hall’s enemies. Hall gave up being
a chronic defendant, and started to proceed
against his prosecutors. In the first action the
verdict for Hall against R Bench of magistrates
for,malice brought £lO ; the next, against Hely,
Superintendent of Convicts, £25; two actions
against the Rev. Ralph Mansfield realised 40s.
each ; another action against Mansfield proved
worth £5O, and an action against Archdeacon
Scott panned out for £25. By this time it began
to be recognised that the Archimedean lever was
hitting back, and the supercilious powers of the
day acquired a sudden respect for Hall.

The N.S.W. Premiership is now officially
considered to entitle the holder to someminor sort
of Imperial gaud, should his hunger and thirst take
that direction. G. H. Reid looked another way
when the ribbon was dangled, but Twaddell and
Wade, though mere neophytes, dream of it. It
was thought that in Carruthers’ case a few old
things would have stood in the way, but the
British authorities have long ceased to mind any
few old things like that. Of the old-time Premiers
William Forster and James Squire Farnell were
the only ones to escape, and in Farnell’s case the
amazing story is told that it was Mrs. Farnell who
made her husband promise to say “No” if he
were ever given the chance to say “Yes.”
“ Eegeebee,” from a Sydney suburb :
That par. (B. 24/9/’OS) relating to the wealth of the Dud-
ley estates in Staffordshire (England) is as true as gospel ;
and, as might be expected, the ways and means by which
said wealth has been amass d are those of the wily and
the wicked. This scribe has good authority for know-
ledge of many a ruinous strike caused by the avarice of
the grandfather of the present earl, and one of the most
disastrous in the history of British labor was that which
occurred in the middle ’fifties, when the ironworkers of
one of the Staffoidshire estates had their miserable pit-
tances of 3s. to 3s. Cd. per day reduced 3d. all round.
The men, aroused by the iniquity of a proceeding
which threatened to make their wretched lot one
of utter misery, struck at once, and in less than
a fortnight more than 30,000 had joined their
ranks, and all the coal mines and iron works
in the county closed down. Deputations were met
with the agent’s unvarying reply that the estates could
ot *ly afford to carry on under the proposed reduction,
yet subsequent events proved that the mines alone netted
almost uncounted thousands for the capitalistic skinflint
who owned them. The men held out until reduced to a
condition of absolute beggary. Thousands tramped
throug h England, begging food and clothing ; many were
literally starved to death. Capital won the day, and the
men returned to work, six months later, under the new
and reduced order of things. More than one old emigrant
of the early ’sixties, now living in N.S. W., remembers the
Dudley Ward ironworkers’ strike.

Ho ! tie me a gage to my saddle-bow,
And fill me my drinking-horn ;
On a knightly errand forth I go
For a damsel all forlorn.
And haste ye, haste, my esquire bold,
For this is no idle myth—
I fare me forth o’er waste and wold
In quest of MISTRESS Smith.
The name of Smith goes down the years
From the ages dim and dead,
’Tis a noble name, and of William Lyne
It never shall be said
That he failed to g'ive a ladye fair
Her cognomen a handle with,
Then saddle me up my brave brown mare
For the quest of COUNTESS Smith.
May the rivets drop from my suit of mail,
And my good sword rust and rot;
May the hairs drop out of my charger’s tail,
If it happens I find her not ;
May I ever be banned by all my kind,
And shunned by my kin and kith, ’
If I homeward come having failed to find
Her Majesty QUEEN Smith.

This bank didn’t have the thorough cleaning- up that
some of them had after the great crisis, and there
were a lot of remnants to attend to afterwards. The dis-
closed earnings for some years were therefore poor. They
may have been actually better than the accounts showed ;
in that case the excess was used to write down values
that were sti 11 regarded as fictitious. Circumstances have
favored the bank. As late as 10 years ago the balance-
sheet showed an item “ Other real estate, at cost (less
£108,098 written off), £430,774.” Gradually that relic of
wild times has been got out of the road, either by sale or
by being written off out of profits, and to-day the
item stands—“ Other real estate, at valuation, £61,745.’*
Over £150,000 of this unsatisfactory item has been got rid
of in the last three years. Recently, having pretty well
cleaned up the “Other real estate” item, the bank has
been attending to the “Bank premises” account. For
years it stood at about £459,000 ; in the pr st.*uo balance-
sheet it stands at £437,307, and another £„000 has been
appropriated to write it down further. (By the way, since
various sums have been appropriated to write down
premises, why present the balance-sheet item thus—
“ Real estate, consisting of bank premises, at cost to the
Bank, £437,307 ”?) The bank’s earnings are still not so
large as to suggest that the assets are by any means
under-valued, and certainly the bank premises account
can do with a lot of pruning yet; but it is obvious that
much effective cleaning up of old wreckage has been done
in the past few years.
Profits calculated on the basis of capital and re-
serves combined have lately been as follows :

been a pretty considerable shrinkage. But really the
fact is that during the last two terms profits have been

As far as the accounts show, there has therefore been a
continuous upward movement in the matter of profits ;
it is only the special appropriations that spoil the look of
things to some extent.
Movements during the past 12 months* have
been as follows :
Sept., ’O7. Sept., ’OB.
Notes in circulation.. £256,810 £258,631 Inc., £1,821
Bills, etc 882,713 511,410 Dee., 371,303
Free deposits .. .. 2,883,760 2,848,906 Dec., 34,854

As in most cases, there has been an increase in
advances; in this case, however, there has been
also an increase in deposits, and that hasn’t been
a general experience. In the aggregate, Victorian
deposits (quarterly average for 11 banks) have
shrunk from £36,829,221 to £35,499,732 in the past 12
months; or, excluding Government deposits, from
£33,716,603 to £33,159,509. The National has, however,
its connection outside Victoria, and that has levelled
things up. On Sept. 30 it held 29 per cent, of its bor-
rowed money in liquid assets—a moderate proportion ; it
wouldn’t be satisfactory to see it go lower. In that
respect the Victorian banks seem to be disposed to take
more risks than those of the other States. Still, though
a bank needs a good cash holding to meet a sudden crisis,
two things govern the ultimate safety of the depositors’
money : (1) The proportion of shareholders’ funds to total
liabilities; (2) the quality of the non-liquid assets. As
to the former, assuming that the capital and reserves are
intact, then this bank only owes an almost exact £5 to
every £1 of the shareholders’ money; and that £1 consti-
tutes a very good insurance fund—a better fund, of course,
than if it had to protect £7 of depositors’ money.

The foregoing record starts from the half-year when the
ordinary shareholders got their first taste of dividends
after the boom smash. When that trouble happened,
part of the debt to the public was compounded for pref.
shares, of which there are 31,184 paid to £9 15s. each
(£304,044); the balance of the capital is in ordinary
shares—77,27B paid to £1 155., and liable for another
£2 10s. The pref. shares take the first 5 per cent. ; then
the ordinaries come in, but they all share alike if over 5
per cent, is paid. When the'pref. shares have got 14
divs. of 7 per cent, or more, their preference as to capi-
tal ceases ; but they still continue entitled to their first
5 per cent. As the above list shows, they haven’t yet
scored one towards the necessary 14 divs. of 7 per cent.
Except in one line there have been no great
movements during the year :
Sept., 1907. Sept., 1908.
Notes in circulation £110,241 £110,950 Inc. £709
Bills in circulation,
etc 191,766 81,935 Dec. 109,831
Deposits at interest 1,939,783 1,960,451 Inc. 20,668
Free deposits .. 1,041,759 1,059,045 Inc. 17,286
Liquid assets .. 854,707 823,756 Dec. 30,951
Advances .. .. 2,751.364 2,730,186 Dec. 21,178
Premises, etc. .. 245,794 247,107 Inc. 1,313
The total debt to the public is thus £3,212,381; and the
liquid assets amount to £823,756, which represents only
26 per cent, of the liabilities. It is quite true that the
proportion of fixed deposits is rather large ; but even so
the percentage of liquid assets held by the bank
is too small unless there are special circumstances which
the accounts do not disclose. Considering that so much
of the available funds is at work, and that the bank has
very nearly £6 of the public’s money for every £1 of its
own in the business, the profit earnings are just moderate
they are certainly not so large as to suggest that the
assets are undervalued or that there are other hidden
reserves in the cupboard. The recent profit figures, on
the basis of capital and reserves combined, have gone like
this
Mar., 1905 .. £7 11 11 p.c. Mar., 1907 .. £8 3 8 p.c.
Sept., 1905.. 7 1 6 „ Sept., 1907.. 7 2 4 „
Mar., 1906.. 7 711 „ Mar., 1908 .. 8 7 2
Sept., 1906.. 7 6 9 „ Sept., 1908.. 8 6 4 „
The reserves are moving along satisfactorily, but there is
still a good way for them to go before the capital can re-
gard itself as safe. Apart from the liquid assets there is
£2,977,296 out at risk—-in advances, premises, etc. If
that lot does not realise £2,977,296, then the share-
holders’ funds (capital and reserves) have to stand the
racket. Now the reserves represent only lOd. in
the £, or a trifle over £4 in the 100, of those
risks ; so that the shareholders’ reserves, which con-
situte the assurance fund for their capital, is only
capable of protecting the shareholders’ risks against
a slump of a shade over 4 per cent. Of course, this is not
enough for safety. That, however, is looking at the
matter from the shareholders’ point of view. The
depositors’ point of view is quite different. His capital
is protected by the shareholders’ capital as well as the
reserves ; so that he has a protection of about 24 per
cent. In other words, every asset (other than the cash,
etc.) could shrink from £124 to £lOO before the depositors’
money would be in danger.

Formed in 1888 this bank arrived rather too late to get
into the same trouble as most of the others. It had a
paid capital of £300,000, and by March, 1892, it had only
£433,678 of deposits. Moreover, it had always maintained
a large reserve of liquid assets ; so, having in consequence
a comparatively small proportion of advances, it was not
hit so severely by the slump in values. It had when
the trouble arrived practically only £2 of non-liquid
assets to every £1 of its own funds, while several other
banks had as much as £6 and £7 at risk for every £1 of
their own money. The Royal, a comparatively new bank
and possessed at the time of nothing much in the
way of prestige, was thus able to pull through without
locking up any deposits ; and that fact ought to make one
think. There is a tendency to go on piling up deposits
in banks which already are heavily weighted with them.
In some cases, the bank does something on its own
account by getting in more capital, and thus giving the
depositor more protection ; but there isn’t any sign that
the average depositor wants the bank to do it—from all
appearances, he would rather put his money into a bank
where the deposits are huge in proportion to capital and
reserves than in one where they are moderate.
* * * • •
. The Royal pulled through, then, not by penal-
ising depositors in any way, but by wiping out its re-
serves, by dropping dividends for four years and then
resuming them at 3 per cent., and finally by cutting its
capital down from £300,000 to £150,000. Since then
it has made steady progress, and throughout
has made large additions to its reserves. Further, con-
tinuing the same careful policy, it now issues 100,000
shares at 2s. 6d. premium ; this (a previous issue of 50 000
at ss. premium having been made) brings up the capital
to the old £300,000. Even without the new capital, the
debt to the public was only £4 3s. for every £1 of the
shareholders’ funds. That was a very substantial insur-
ance fund from the depositors’ point of view ; and the
inorease of capital makes it more substantial still.
The principal balance-sheet figures compare
with those of 12 months ago like this

Owing to the ebbing of the boom-tide, the deposits have
shrunk, while more accommodation has been sought by the
public j one result is that liquid assets now represent
only 29 per cent, of the liabilities to the public, which is
much less than this bank usually maintains. Still, the
directors no doubt had in view the early arrival of the
new capital. The earnings continue very good. These
figures show the return calculated on the basis of capital
and reserves combined :

can’t be many unprofitable lines on the books. And the
return for the last two terms would be rather better than
they are but that fairly extensive head-office alterations
have been paid for out of profits not included above.

This bank is gradually pulling itself out of its difficulties.
Though it wrote off £1 per share, and called up £2 10s.,
making the shares £6 10s. paid, it never had a complete
cleaning up ; so that it unquestionably had a large
amount of bad debts to make good out of earnings. This
was not a satisfactory policy to adopt; it kept a lot of
fictitious figures in the balance sheet, and the necessity
to gradually work off the old dead horse practically com-
pelled the bank to show less profits than no doubt at
times it was actually making on current business. It is
highly probable that that same condition of things still
exists. The bank has invariably paid in dividends
in recent years almost the whole of the dis-
closed profits; and if the disclosed profits have
been the real profits, the management would
deserve to be spoken to very severely for laying by so
little. Looking at the table above it would certainly
seem that the Royal of Queensland’s was an improvident
management whose only concern almost was to pay divi-
dends ; yet there is very good evidence that it is 'by no
means that sort of management. The truth most likely
is that, knowing that there are no real reserves at all,
since the capital is not intact, the management hesitates
to pile up reserve " figures ;so it uses undisclosed profits
to write down the assets which ought to have been
written down out of capital long ago. The misfortune
is that, while this is being done, you get no idea of what
the real profits are : also you have no idea of the size of
the hole which still remains to be patched up.
On the basis of capital and reserves combined,
recent profits have been as follows :
Dec., 1904 .. £3 13 5 p.c. Dec., 1906 .. £4 2 11 p.c.
June, 1905 .. 314 1 „ June, 1907.. 4 5 5 „
Dec., 1905 .. 315 7 „ Dec., 1907 .. 412 7 „
June, 1996 .. 316 4 „ Sept., 1908.. 411 9 „
Owing to the issue of pref. capital, which apparently can
be taken up at any time, the last two or three lines are
only approximate. In a case of this sort, since there is
every reason to believe that the balance-sheet figures
don’t mean exactly what they say, one has to trust the
management to a very great extent. Presumably all
that the management intends to do is to give a general
indication of the way things are going, and the hint con-
veyed in tiie figures which work out as above can only be
one thing—that the assets are now a much more produc-
tive lot than they were.
The figures for two recent periods compare like
Dec., ’O7. Sept., ’OB.
Bills and notes .. £42,888 £52,597 Inc. £9,709
Deposits .. .. 1,075,710 1,103,927 Inc. 28,217
Liquid assets .. 400,912 443,195 Inc. 42,283
Advances.. .. 1,211,788 1,226,097 Inc. 14,309
Premises, etc. .. 63,712 63,458 Dec. 254
The balancing days have been altered lately ; the Royal
in that matter took a step which others might well
follow. The liquid assets represent a substantial 38 per
cent, of the total debt to the public. The capital is now
£492,252 ; this is made up of £454,994 ordinary capital
(£6 10s. per share paid), £11,458 ordinary prepaid capital
(this carries the ordinary dividend), and £25,800 pref.
capital. Even allowing for considerable over-valuation
of assets, the creditors are well protected by reason of the
large proportion of capital. On the published figures,
there is only a shade over £2 of the public’s money to
every £1 of capital and reserves; but some of that capital
is not supposed to exist. The market says the £6 10s.
shares are worth £4 15s. That means a discount of
£194,870, reducing the capital to £369,759 and wiping
out the reserves. Assuming that that represents the real
position, then the shareholders’ funds would still be equal
to £1 for every £3 3s. of the public’s money ; and the
security for deposits, etc., would still be very good indeed.

A few months ago Tiie Bulletin said that Tooth and Co.,
Ltd., would be issuing some new shares soon ; and,
though it was subsequently explained that the directors
had not dealt with the question in any way, it is still
pretty obvious that something of the sort is more than
probable. True, some of the big Tooth-family holders on
the other side of the world have, it is fairly well known
to brokers, been selling a large parcel of these shares;
but that is understandable enough. Anybody who lives in
England and is interested in breweries could hardly
have felt anything else but a bit nervous a few months
ago during the anti-beer excitement; and, in view
of the special licensing courts in N. S. Wales, they
probably concluded that the same anti-beer excitement
raged in N. S. Wales. There certainly is enough danger
in the air to lead to brewery investments being included
in the risky class, along with gassy coal mines, gun-
powder factories, wayback sheep stations, and such like ;
still, as far as they have got, the best of them are not
doing too badly. And Tooth’s is distinctly among the
best of them. It went along very cautiously in the
matter of paying dividends ; and its position (apart from
the general brewery risk aforementioned) has been made
one of unusual strength. This comparison shows in a line
or two the aggregate movements since September, 1900
Sept., 1900. Sept., 1908.
Liquid asssets ..£127,453 £287,316 Inc. £159,863
Stock.. .. .. 51,582 121,062 Inc. 69,480
Book debts.. .. 210,037 204,763 Dec. 5,274
Total •• ..£389,072 £613,141 Inc. £224,069
Less debts to public 10,938 21,276 Inc. 10,338
£378,134 £591,865 Inc. £213,731
This represents an increase in liquid assets of £159,863 in
eight years, notwithstanding the steady 8 or 10 per cent,
dividend. Moreover, in the same time book debts have
been reduced from £210,037 to £204,763; and though
that, of course, may be due to debts having been paid
off, it is also possible that it is due to undisclosed profits
being used to severely writedown everything that looked
at all like a doubtful debt. As a general thing, £5OOO
per half-year is appropriated to cover depreciation of
plant and machinery. Something besides is apparently
written off and charged direct to profit and less ; but the
amount is unspecified. The various items have moved
recently as follows :
1900. 1907. 1908.
Debts to public .. .. £10,938 £17,478 £21,276
Properties, goodwill, plant 671,661 613,433 606,843
Public stocks and deposits 99,349 206,745 262,502
Cash balances .. .. 28,104 36,627 24,814
Stock 51,582 100,780 121,062
Sundry debtors .. .. 210,037 210,626 204,763

etc 113,562 145,731 168,451
Management, wages, etc. 74,611 86,843 100,547
This, to some extent, is an amplification of the figures
previously given. It shows the gradual reduction of pro-
perties, goodwill, etc.; yet it will be noted that profits on
brewing, etc., don’t shrink, from which it, seems fair to
conclude that it is only in the books that properties,
plant, etc., have shrunk. Practically there is only one
point at which you can tap these accounts without
getting the impression that they are wonderfully solid ;
and that lone spot with the hollow sound about it is the
goodwill portion of the lumped item mentioned above.
How much goodwill stands for there is no way of finding
out; and it is as likely as not that it is kept there on
general principles, and for the sake of appearances. For
it doesn’t always pay to appear too prosperous.

For many years, from 1898 onward, this co. represented
Newcastle as a place with a thirst so even that if it had
been frozen over it could have been used as a skating
rink. Year after year just enough profit was disclosed to
pay the 4 per cent. div. and allow £3OOO to be written off
freeholds and plant—mostly £2OOO off freeholds and the
other £lOOO off plant. The fact, no doubt, was that the
co., which set off with too much paper (representing
goodwill) among its assets, had loaded itself up with
depreciated properties and doubtful debts ; and year by
year it had to make a large provision on this account.
Gradually the total of book debts has been worked down ;
and probably a great deal of old bad debt has been
replaced by newer debt, which so far looks and smells
better. This shows the movement in that and some other
respects:—
1901. 1907. 1908.
Liabilities to public .. £68,307 £60,896 £57,259

vision for bad debts).. 48,623 37,400 39,618
Sundry debtors stand for £26,175 less than they did seven
years ago ; how much of this has been paid off and how
much has been written off there is no way of telling.
It is a, good movement in either case. The amount of
goodwill in the lumped item “ Properties, buildings,
machinery, plant, goodwill, rolling stock, etc., £227,478”
is not ascertainable either ; and that is a weak point in
the situation, and no doubt largely explains why these £1
shares are quoted at anything from 10s. to 125., in spite
of the alleged reserves of £48,565. If the assets were
worth 20s. in the £ they would cut up for 235. lOd. per
share instead of about the market valuation of lls. 6d.
It seems to be a case where a cleaning-up is called for.
The position at present is that the public values this pro-
perty at a trifle under £150,000, while the balance-sheet
figures represent it to be worth £298,565 ; and either the
public or the balance-sheet is right off the target. And
when the public observes that for 11 years no more than an
annual 4 per cent. div. has been paid, and very little more
has been admittedly earned, then the public is justified
in saying that prima facie it is right, and the balance-
sheet is wrong. Which isn’t a satisfactory conclusion to
have to come to regarding any balance-sheet.

This curiously-named but consistent dividend-payer has
lately had its 21st birthday. Stray balance sheets avail-
able go back to the early ’nineties, when 5 per cent, was
being paid. The capital was then £9124 ; there have been
small increases since, and the capital now stands at 10s.
short of the level £lO,OOO. Ten years ago 5 per cent, was
still being paid ; ditto in 1900 ; but two years later 8 per
cent, was available. The co. has consistently written
down plant and rolling stock ; 5 per cent, is written off
the former and 10 per cent, off the latter each half-year.
The occasional bigger writing down shown above is in
connection with the freeholds. The co. has steadily built
up its holding of properties—presumably hotels—out of
undivided profits. This comparison shows how various
items have moved :
Aug., Aug., Feb., Aug.,

It appears to be a safe little business run on careful lines
■ —assuming, of course, that one regards investment in
pub. property nowadays as safe business at all. For the
pub. property £750 is pretty often paid for the building,
and £1250, or more, because it is licensed to be the habita-
tion of a beer pump ; and as one can’t be very sure what
will happen in these times to the beer pump, pub. values
are in constant danger. And even if nothing happens to
them, practically every brewing co. in the country has
to dip its hand into the till and pay something substantial
to a defence fund.

The old Federal is a-going it. It made a big splash during
the boom and a bigger splash when the boom broke and
the Federal fell with the others. In 1892, when the un-
pleasant splash occurred, it owed £389,939, chiefly money
received on deposit. Then for 13 long years it kept its
nose to the grindstone, but in 1904 it reckoned that it was
about time to look up and see if there wasn’t some new
business to be got. There were then on the books pro-
perties and other assets valued at £122,839 ; so a new
society was formed, with a capital of £96,351, which left
nominally £26,488 of reserves. The subsequent proceed-
ings are partly indicated above and partly below :
1904. 1906. 1907. 1908.
Overdraft, etc £11,960 £18,691 £25,048
Advances £63,160 109,415 124,510 136,217
Properties in possession.. 54,071 32,924 26,252 20,124
Cash balances .. .. 4,779
The properties in possession were taken over from the
old society ; and there has been a fine clearing out here,
and at a profit, too, as the first table shows. Of the
property now in possession £7992 is vacant land; there
has been a fair reduction of that item since 1904, but not
proportionately so large a reduction as of the buildings
item. On the average the properties (including land)
bring in a shade over 3 per cent, gross ; the advances,
however, show about £7 Is. 6d. per cent, on the average
—and they don’t call for any outlay in rates;
moreover, the mortgage hasn’t got any gutter that leaks,
and it rarely requires a new door-handle. The wisdom of
exchanging the foreclosed properties for mortgages when
the opportunity offered is, therefore, pretty obvious.
What may happen to those mortgages in the future no-
body, of course, can say for certain ; but on present
appearances, and judging by the return they give, there
isn’t much the matter with them. The liabilities are still
not large, though they are increasing; so far, however,
the deposits total only £B996—most of the balance is
overdraft. No doubt deposits would be welcomed, and
there is excellent security for them ; but, in spite of that
excellent security, the society would probably find it a
good deal easier to get in public money if it put by more
to reserve, and wasn’t in so much hurry to rush up its
dividend.

In the calculation of working costs in concen-
trating mills and workshops, every small item should be
taken into consideration. It is the small and uncon-
sidered factors, involving waste of power, that make for
high working costs. The type of bearing employed has a
marked effect on the power consumed in overcoming
friction. Recent exhaustive tests emphasised the marked
superiority of the Hyatt Flexible Roller Bearings over all
other types of roller, sleeve, and other anti-friction
bearings. Under equal conditions the loss of power due
to friction in Hyatt Flexibles is 23 per cent, less than
with the best solid roller bearing, and 65 per cent, less
that with babbit metal journals. Messrs. W. and J.
Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, will be
glad to supply further information to those interested.*

All philosophic works, and Holy Writ itself,
teem with warning of the uncertainty of life. Deaths by
natural causes have been pushed far down the years of
civilised life, but with the increase of that very civilisa-
tion the liability to accident has enormously increased.
The railway, the ferry, the tram, and the murderous
motor car inevitably cause thousands of people to place
their lives and limbs in the hands of entire strangers, of
whose ability they can have absolutely no knowledge. It
would seem, therefore, to be only common-sense to guard
against the financial consequences of misadventure by
taking out an accident and sickness policy with the South
British Insurance Company Limited ; £5OO Death, £3 per
week Accident and Sickness Compensation for £3 per
annum. Head Office for N.S.W.: 12 Bridge-street, Syd-
ney. Geo. H. Moore, Manager.*

Five copper converters are being manufactured
for the Electrolytic Refining and Smelting Co., of Port
Kembla, N.S.W., which will be the largest in use within
the Commonwealth. They are 10ft. 6in. long ; 7ft. 6in.
diameter ; hold 8 tons of copper matte ; and when lined
and charged weigh 29 tons ; they are turned by a rack
gear operated by electric motors. The work is being done
to the order of the manager, Mr. B. Magnus, by the
Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South Melbourne, which
firm has made a speciality of copper smelting and con-
verting, in addition to all other methods of ore and metal
treatment.* [2

That famous engineering feat, the Zigzag,
near Lithgow, will soon disappear, its place being taken
by a series of tunnels. The Railway Commissioners, who
are carrying out this work, have ordered eight Reavell
Compressors as being the most suitable to instal for this
class of work. Reavell Compressors are coming rapidly
into favor with mining men, who appreciate the absence
of suction valves and the high volumetric and mechanical
efficiency of these machines. The sole licensees for Aus-
tralia are Babcock and Wilcox Ltd., Haymarket, Sydney.*

The underwriting profit last year was thus £1569; and
only a shade under that much was added to the reserves.
For the previous few years the whole of the dividend was
paid out of interest; which means that more than the
total amount by which the premiums exceeded losses and
expenses was added to reserves, for the further protec-
tion of policy-holders. In five years the premiums have
exceeded the losses and expenses by £7450, while the
additions to reserves have been £7652 ; so that the
shareholders have not absorbed [uite all of even the in- •
terest earned by capital and other funds. However, the
dividend has now been put up t step, so it will probably
take a bite out of the under 'riting profit for a time.
The weakest feature of the cc.’s finance is that nearly
everything the co. has is out on mortgage ; and if any-
thing large and unpleasant struck the co. it might have
considerable trouble in speedily realising its securities
without making a serious loss. There are 37,500 shares
in the co. paid to £l, and liable for another £4. It is a-
very tidy liability for shareholders to carry about with
them ; but the reserves now amount to very nearly £1
per share, and that is a good insurance policy against
calls.
* ■* # *
The recent labor crisis in Broken Hill surprised
some of the more conservative mine managers. A week
or two before the crisis the men talked about the im-
pending struggle over the wages agreement as calmly as
though they were ordering steak and onions ; and if any
eager individual mentioned “ strike” they simply smiled.
But no sooner had the word to “down tools” gone forth
than the whole body of workers bristled like the fretful
porcupine. It was boiling water that was in the pot.
And it is still boiling.
* * * *
Broken Hill people have become tired of asking
“ When is the British going to start?” Asa matter of
fact, when the mine was first closed down, the directors
intended to reopen when lead got to £l5 a ton. But
time has beaten the market, and lead doesn’t show any
sign of rapid recovery ; and now the directors are faced
with a labor trouble. So the probability is that the
management will continue pegging away at the develop-
ment in TJhompson section, and leave ore-extraction and
the arrangement of a contract for the sale of concentrates
to the future—which is always full of promise for a
mining man.
* * * *
Bourke’s Hill, one of the Melbourne-owned tin-
sluicing cos. in the New England country (N.S.W.),
showed a balance of £149 to the bad in the past half-year ;
and £490 written off for depreciation brought up the total
to £639. The co. is in better ground now, though it will
take some months to work off the overdraft of £735.
