* * * *
An inquirer named Beale, who seems to have
done his work carefully, has compiled a comparative
table of the position of the Boulder, Horseshoe, Ivanhoe,
and Kalgurli, which shows (1) that during 1905, 1906 and
1907 the four mines treated among them 2,082,198 tons
for £5,991,026, and distributed £2,677,500 in dividends ;
(2) that the present ore reserves approximate 9,000,000
tons, as against 8,500,000 three years ago ; and (3) that
the ore now in sight is sufficient to last 4J years at the
present rate of reduction.
* * * •
Associated Northern (W.A.) has taken an
option on the Harbor Lights at Leonora. Some time back
8., M. and 00. took this show on appro., but after spend-
ing £ll,OOO they refused to buy it. Several lodes have
been exposed, and the Northern Co. intends to put in a
few bore holes to see how they shape in depth. So far
about 12,000 tons have been taken out of the property for
an average gold recovery of 12dwt.
Westralian State mining engineer Montgomery
visited Southern Cross the other day, and dug up the
interesting fact that, though Fraser’s is one of the oldest
and most constantly worked mines in the West, its
deepest shaft is only down 366 ft. To date this show has
produced £678,000, and its shareholders divided £24,000
in the ’nineties. Later on the shutters were put up, and
later still Vincent Shallcross, Boulder Hamilton and a
few' other wideawake persons came along and picked it
up for a few’ thousands. Since then they have got their
money back in tributors’ royalties, and a bit more
besides. Every now and then the local paper gets
annoyed over the dawdling, and asks the Government to
shake Shallcross and party up. So far, however, they
have escaped molestation. Fraser’s certainly has possi-
bilities if worked on a big scale ; but as this would neces-
sitate the expenditure of say £lOO,OOO, and as a doubt
exists whether the recoverable contents of the ore are
sufficient to justify such an outlay, capital is shy of the
proposition.
Peak Hill Goldfield (W.A.) shareholders are in a
bad way. The present Board took over 400 acres of ground,
representing £300,000 (the par value of the shares). It
has since issued £25,000 in preference shares, and has
pawned the area for £50,000 to the first debenture stock-
holders ; and now it has practically handed the whole lot
over to a few persons termed prior lien bondholders for
£10,004, of which the co. had at a recent date only re-
ceived £2501. If, as London papers state, no bondholder
can be legally compelled to pay anything further, this
means that the prior lien lot has its thumb on the whole
of the assets of the co. in return for the application
money. Unless things improve enormously shareholders
are pretty certain to be left out in the cold.
* * * *
Santa Claus, at Randalls (W.A.), is again on
the sick list. Some months ago it was estimated that
200,000 tons of payable ore were in sight above the 200 ft.
level; but since then all the money in hand, as well as
that derived from two calls and a crushing, has melted
away ; and the estimate is looking queer. This paper has
repeatedly said that the co. is more likely to want a
coffin than a strong room, and it still sorrowfully clings
to that opinion.
* ■* . » *
A syndicate, of which Charters Towers Mills is
the back bone, has been prodding the bowels of the earth
alongside the Mount Morgan deposit. After sending the
diamond drill through formations typical of the big mine,
the core brought up ore from 1200 ft., corresponding to
over 900 ft. vertical depth. The possibilities of the dis-
covery are great, and developments should be full of
interest.
* * » *
Charters Towers is in for a lot of exploration
involving hard work and a string of calls. The
Day Dawn reef having been proved in No. 7 level nearly
halfw'ay across, the New Brilliant Freehold has now be-
come the objective of the Brilliant Blocks. Its shaft has
been sunk below the Brilliant formation, which of late
years has given the Blocks an existence. The Towers is
famous for rapid shaft sinking, and Manager Harris,
lately of the Mexican, w'ho has taken over the New Bril-
liant Freehold, is the man to make things willing.
Whether the Day Dawn is a distinct formation from the
Brilliant has been much discussed, but the important
question is whether this series of ore shoots extends much
further east. With a face of stone 4ft. wide at the end of
No. 7 level, 520 ft. from the boundar3 r , the inference is
strong in favor of continued developments.
* * * ■*
East Mexican, Charters Towers, reported an
improvement in No. 5 level just before work was sus-
pended, owing to a machinery overhaul; and with No. 6
also at a standstill the market requires more proof that
No. 5 has at last intersected a main ore shoot and not a
patch, though a patch often means something more solid
further on. New Brilliant Freehold had also to stop
driving for want of compressed air. No. 7 was looking
well, and No. 8 on the improve. A rise above No. 8 seems
to have got through the poor zone, in which this level
for the most part has been driven. If No. 8 entered a
long shoot of ore similar to that in the upper level it
would inspire more confidence.
* * * *
Brilliant Deep, Charters Towers, is crosscutting
at a vertical depth of 2413 ft. on the track of the diamond
drill, which it was expected'to verify. But the reef was
very flat, so the borehole gave a misleading ’ idea of the
width of the reef. All the same, the market produced by
the good assays has not collapsed. Bonnie Dundee, which
pays a sixpenny div. next month, has an improvement in
the main level; but there are poorer prospects at the end
of No. 2 East intermediate. This mine has a good block
of ground to work, and a second, div. may follow. The
Buby crushed 474 tons for 13600 z., which is better than
the expected £8 per ton. The deepest level (No. 8) is in
60ft. east of the underlie, with 6in. of heavy mineral stone
in the face. The end of the Western level, however, has
only leaders in the formation. A sixpenny div. has been
declared.
* • * »
“Billycan”: What has become of those good
judges who considered the No. 7 level in Mills United,
Charters Towers, was better than the No. 6 or No. 8 level
(B. 3/9/’08)? Quite a number of other things alleged by
those “ good judges,” re .the upper levels of Mills United
near the Mexican boundary, will yet be found all wrong.
Holders of Mexicans can rest assured that, just as No. 7
level is better than No. 8, and No. 6 better than No. 7. "o
will No. sbe better than'No. 6 when it is brought '. or on
to the right tack. It is interesting to note that, although
two levels and sundry crosscuts were necessary to pro-
spect and work Mills United and the Freehold, one poky
level is considered sufficient at No. 5, East Mexican, and
timid shareholders are induced to part with their
scrip because there is no reef in this level. An-
other very amusing incident is that a survey has
been ordered before the reef has by means of a crosscut
been located at the No. 5 level. That a survey under
such conditions is likely to be misleading is as obvious as
the probability that it will bring shares back to a splendid
buying price. It is marvellous how a few judicious sales
by the Mexican “bear” and a little extra limelight on
the poor prospects of the No. 5 level can hold Mexicans
back, whpn a magnificent crushing taken from their
foundations is so necessary to keep Freeholds good in the
market.
* * * *
“Phoenix”: Two pars (8/10/’08) refer to the
reefs at the Two-Mile, Gympie. The Homeward Bound
and the Peter and Paul are mentioned as showing coarse
gold ; and, what is far more important, it is added that
the slate beds in which they are being worked are iden-
tical with the prolific slates of the famous Phoenix reefs.
After all these years, and the enormous amount of capital
and labor uselessly expended in testing the deep ground
in that locality, it is very gratifying that the Phoenix slate
should be discovered there and that prosperous times are
in store at last. But who is the authority for stating that
it is the Phoenix slate ? The second bed too ! The slate
formation of the Phoenix, Smithfield, Monkland and
Glanmire reefs have a gradual incline (similar to a coal
seam) in settled country, and are from two to three miles
distant from the Two-Mile. Besides, whilst these reefs
were well-defined and gave regular payable crushings for
years, the reefs at the Two-Mile were barren at a depth,
as the patches of gold-bearing quartz were got from the
shallow workings. The Bristol, Sadowa, London (the best
of the group), Chatsworth, Canadian, Homeward Bound,
Peter and Paul, and Hibernian were all of this character,
and the gold obtained near the surface was sunk in
testing the deep ground. The Peter and Paul (recently
floated into a co.) was . always next door to
a duffer, and, although referred to as showing coarse
gold,, its shares are still quoted at Is., with no buyers.
A reef showing coarse gold in the Phoenix second slate-
bed having no buyers at Is. —what are local residents
thinking about? There are some mighty shrewd specu-
lators there, too. The reports which have been published
re Great Northern almost make one’s month water ; yet
shares can be had for Is. 9d. —and no buyers. Now, Ido
not profess to be able to see through soiid rock any far-
ther than others, and should like to see a revival in the
prosperity of the locality ; but when informed that reefs
are showing coarse gold in the old settled slate forma-
tion of the. Phoenix, and then when I see the shares going
begging at,a paltry “bob,” I conclude that something is
wrong somewhere.
* * *■ *
Mount Hector (Q.) has its new furnace in blast,
and has given it a preliminary run for 5£ days. The
average day’s run was equal to 60 tons burden—in all,
320 tons material. Of this total 177 tons were smelting
ore, 103 tons flux, and 40 tons reverberatory bottoms.
The product was a 47 per cent, matte, containing 14
tons of copper. (Note that the charge contained 40 tons
furnace bottoms. How much of the 14 tons of copper
came from this? And what of the ore supplies?) In
Welch’s stopes the ore is a “ little low grade in places.”
Judging from the above figures, it is doubtful whether
ore of a “little low grade ” is worth smelting. Develop-
ment Work has been suspended except at one point in
Welch’s. Fluxing ore seems to be the only material
there is plenty of—very desirable stuff too, if there is
anything to flux.
* • * *
Unless something bad happens to the metal
market, Smith’s Greek (N.Q.) will probably re-open at the
beginning of 1909. If, as alleged, the ore is worth 2£ per
cent, tin, it ought to be a payable proposition if run in
the right wav. Mining has been done for 10s. ; milling
should not cost more than 10s. at the most; and, allowing
ss. for transport, realisation, and various expenses, there
would still be 10s. left on the 355. ore. The chief
stumbling block is the inefficient plant, which, although
costly to work, gives no satisfaction.
* * * *
Croydon (N.Q.) has had still another set-back
owing to the poor opinion expressed by Government Geo-
logist Dunstan regarding the prospects of the Highland
Mary and Bennion’s Block. In the Highland Mary they
have to find stone ; in Bennion’s gold-stone was certainly
found beyond the break which up to that time had
marked the end of the field ; but Bennion, who tackled
the job single handed, was driven out by water. Now,
however, pumps are at work, and 84,000 gallons a day
are being got out; and within a few' weeks—by the end
of the year, at any rate—the shareholders who have had
to hand out 11 penny calls in quick time will get some
idea of the run they are likely to have for their money.
Their chances are not as horribly gloomy as the atmo-
sphere of the field, which is in a pitifully dead-beat state.
In the six months to the end of the first week in
October, Cathcart (Vic.) received £12,651 from gold won,
and spent £11,003, which showed a gross profit of £1648
for the term—a poor result in view of all the unprofitable
work that had been done to make ready the ground
which has lately been worked. From "first to last,
under this co., the outlay has been £38,248, and
the gold W’on has contributed only £26,446 of
that total. No mine has had more puffing; the
people who, bought at high prices W'ere induced to
forget all about the 20 years during which it had been
proved a heart-breaking sink for money. 8., M. & Go.
for a time had the idea that it was something else ; but
they dropped it. The people who follow'ed had the luck
to strike a good patch ; possibly they knew of its existence
beforehand. Judging from the way shares have been
supplied, it is very doubtful, though, whether some of
them ever had any confidence that it was anything more
than a patch.
» * * *
Cathcart has to pay on roughly 50,000 shares ;
and, seeing that ounce dirt for six months, with the
benefit of work done by 8., M. and Co., only led to the
earning of £l6OO profits, it is evident that divs. won’t
roll in. The mine is now stranded for nearly three
months to let the wash drain. Drives across the leads
from Nos. 4 and 5 shoots are reported to have collapsed,
due to the rotten ground. If so, the whole plan of work
wants re-modelling. The main level should be pushed
ahead and a block of the lead cut off 1000 ft. long. This
should be allowed to drain, and then tackled. To go on
as now rheans working the wash so wet that, even under
favorable circumstances, all the gold will be wanted to
pay exes.
•*■ • *
Latest figures from Duke and Main Leads,
Maryborough (Vic.), support all former experience that
well on the way to £lOO,OOO is needed to get a big deep-
lead mine going. This is how the money went at the
Duke and Main Leads: Machinery, £i8,000; wages,
£47,000; ' fuel, £17,600; ironmongery and timber,
£lO,OOO ; mine itself, £4505 ; and management, £3414.
Now the mine is in swing an idea can be got of what’s
ahead. First, the wash at No. 1 shaft has been tackled
seriously, so there is over 1000 ft. of fairly well-drained
ground to work. The wash at No. 6 rise is somewhat
patchy, and with a lot of cement. Still the average
grade of the dirt, so far, is nearly 17dwt., which leaves a
margin of profit. The co. is now financial and has a
strong Board to keep funds straight. Heavy outlay goes
on at the old Main Leads north shaft. Here the dirt is
worth under 13dwt., so that it is touch and go between
profit and loss. Things are not very encouraging at this
point, but luck may come when deep ground is got in the
west branch drive.
* •* * *
Maori Queen, Bright (Vic.), has always been a
dredging beauty that people squabble over. ’Way back
in Langford’s time it was boomed as big as the Briseis
Extended, and then it pretty well burst. The co.’s plant
is as old as dredging at Bright, so a new dredge is being
got to shift 7000 cubic yards a week. Why there should
be a fuss over the co. again just now no one is quite
clear, unless the reason is a lust of power by some of the
newer generation of shareholders. Costs with the old
dredge pan out at nearly £6O a week, while gold won
averages 28i0z., leaving a profit of a shade over £5O a
week, exclusive of renewals. The co. has tacked 65 acres
to its holding, and now has 410 acres to work, out of 460
held. It wants, not one new dredge, but two.
♦ * TT *
Forest Creek, Castlemaine (Vic.), put in a satis-
factory half-year with its two plants, considering that
No. 1 was short of water and in tough ground. Now it
goes into Hundredweight Gully, and better gold should
be ahead. Six acres gave 10670 z. This is a better average
than is got at Bright with the dredges, as the buckets
used there cannot get into the crevices and cracks. On
the other hand, costs at Castlemaine are twice as heavy
as they are at Bright, owing to the scraping and cleaning
work. Castlemaine dredging is less profitable than it
was, and there seems no chance of a revival, as most of
the good spots have been turned over—really for the last
time now.
• * * *
Surplus, £7 13s. sd. This is the mite that the
Briseis Extended (Tas.) flings on to the table to show that
the last copper has not been spent. As £6 10s. of that
sum is represented by outstanding calls which may not
be paid, the surplus comes down to £1 3s. 5d., or just
about enough for a round of drinks. With various trim-
mings cutoff, the position is—Tin and gold, £1664 ; debts,
£1943. This is not so bad as it looks, because a lot of
dead work has had to be paid for, the barge is
on good tin where the bores showed none, and pug
and clay have gone out of the face. The bottom drifts
don’t seem to have been turned over by any prehistoric
miner, and once a new blower is put in they should add
to the returns. There should be at least six months’ clear
run ahead, and as there is “ Up-lads-and-at-’em ” methods
about the management nowadays, the market would like
to see the show have a turn of luck.
* » * *
A Melbourne co. is still nursing those great
Blythe River iron deposits to which Alex. Montgomery
drew attention when he was Government Geologist of
Tasmania. Years later, when William Jamieson hatched
his scheme for establishing iron and steel works some-
where near Sydney, preferably on the Parramatta,
the Blythe River deposits were to be the source
of supply ; but that great project fell through,
and by way of a 16th half-yearly report chair-
man Jamieson now merely has to say: “ Until
either a Bonus Bill or suitable tariff is passed, our policy
must remain one of inaction.” The co. has about £5500
in hand, and it reckons that that will see it through until
Australia wakes up. Meantime, the Lithgow (N.S.W.)
works, though they have got Government contracts at
special prices, find that the foreign imports are still
strangling them so far as general orders are concerned,
and this week 300 men have been put off temporarily.
* * * *
“Quidam”: Apart from the bad people who
steal gold and the worse people who illicitly buy it, there
is a steady feeling of resentment in Victoria against the
Gold Buyers Act and the politicians who passed it. No
one who knows anything, and who has no axe to grind,
denies that a great deal of gold stealing has gone on, and
apparent^'still is going on ; but what rankles is that the
outcry against gold stealing should be used as an excuse
for extracting more revenue from an industry which was
already shedding about as much as it could spare. For
instance, there are in Victoria hundreds of men run-
ning little cyanide plants, either employing labor
or doing their own, and against whom there has been no
suspicion or hint of gold-buying. From these very small
men there are various gradations, up to the big mining
cos. ; and almost without exception the reputation of the
proprietors was spotless. Under the Act, however, in-
stead of receiving licenses to smelt their gold at a nominal
fee, each license carries a fee of £1 per annum, and if a
cyanider has two or more plants, he must procure a
separate license for each plant, while if the area is not in
his own name (as would be the case were he a lessee or a
tributor, as he often is) he cannot obtain a special license
at all.
# * * *
Seecomb, a contractor at Dunolly (Vic.), while
sinking a post-hole the other day, unearthed a 440 z.
nugget. It was in this district that Victoria’s largest
mass of the precious metal was discovered, and then also
the finder was sinking a post-hole. Leeson was the finder,
and the nugget was the Welcome Stranger, which brought
£9OOO. There seems to be luck attached to post-holes.
* * * *
A very substantial English co. is being formed
to work the Woodside gold mines (S.A.). A few shares
have been put aside for Adelaide consumption, but it is
said that flotation in London was assured anyhow. The
success that the Kittieoola mine (Reedy Creek) has been
getting lately has turned English attention favorably to
the often-abandoned mines in the S. Australian “Hills”
country. Metallurgist Opie, who abandoned the Mitchell
River gold-antimony proposition under the remarkable
circumstances already described, is now managing the
Kittieoola.
* * * *
Miss Clara Kleinschmidt, a Lobethal (S. A.) girl
of 18, for whose contralto voice Ada Crossley prophesies
great things, is to have the same distinction as Gilbert’s
Duke of Plaza Toro, and be floated into a company. Ade-
laide, always ready for a promising gamble, has put up
£lOOO to take her to Europe. In return, she binds herself
for 10 years to the shareholders, her total earnings to be
thus allotted : (1) The first £3OO a year for herself ; (2)
the next £lOO to go in reduction of capital; (3) of every-
thing above that the shareholders are to get one-fifth as
profit.

“ G.M.H.” (who is a girl) writes :
The quaint assaults upon Socialism made at the just-
dispersed Women’s Congress remind me. During the last
Federal elections I was staying in Gippsland (Vic.). The
station dames of those parts, when not engaged assailing
each other’s moral characters or making patchwork quilts
for the Chinese mission, used to discuss politics and read
“papers” on “ The Keeping Down of the Working Classes,”
and so on. As election day approached politics took pre-
cedence over slander as a topic of conversation, and the
Chinese quilts were laid aside. The atmosphere grew
quite electric. I asked the leading politician of the band
who she meant to support one day. “ Oh, I shall vote by
the Argus,” she said. “You know the Age is for the
Labor party.” The spirit of untruthfulness entered into
me, and I replied : “ But the Age and Argus are both
run by the same man. They only take opposite political
sides to be sure of getting everybody’s pennies.” The
poor old thing got quite hot and unhappy. “Well,” she
said, miserably, “if that’s so, I sha’n’t vote at all.” There
were five worthy dames at the least in that district who,
to my certain knowledge, “ voted by the Argus.” They
all believed that, if the Labor man won, the banks would
be invaded arid looted by scoundrels in bowyangs, also
that their homes would be destroyed and the Christian
faith displaced in favor of atheism. And in the end the
Labor man did win—and the curious thing is the banks
are intact, and the local homes still stand, and religion
has not been injured or even assailed. Wasn’t it funny—
or was it sad ?
I’m the Robur Tea Girl.
I’m having a jolly time ! I’m
here, there and everywhere and
I talk to everybody—men too —
and everybody takes notice of
what I say, except perhaps,
grumpy old women who are
only jealous because they can’t
attract attention like I do —I’m
a very lucky girl, don’t you
think ? to be connected with the
best people in the tea trade—it
makes my job of introducing
Robur a comparatively easy
one, doesn’t it?—and there’s
a lot of satisfaction in
knowing when I do succeed in
inducing people to use our tea
I’m doing them a good turn
as well as doing good to my
firm, for Robur really is
pure, wholesome tea.
Our No. 2 is the grade we
recommend for everyday use—
the next time you are buying tea
try it—you will, won’t you?

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 27, 1908.
My Dear Arint,—
Miss Grace Watson has gone back to Melbourne,
leaving Sydney dizzy at the recollection of the
amount of work and pleasure she was able to
digest. (River picnics, morning teas and garden
parties come under the label “pleasure,” I
suppose.) She has nothing to complain of as
regards Sydney’s press. It treated her conference
well, but with a tinge of amused tolerance. Con-
sidering the ridiculous ‘ ‘ Mrs. Smith ” yarn, it could
hardly do otherwise. The delegate from Jeetho,
in Gippsland (did anyone ever hear of Jeetho till
now ?), was a good dresser, and exploited a peach
of a Merry Widow coat. Another delegate from
(xippsland was good to the drapers here, and wore
pretty frocks, and some of them (the delegates, not
the frocks) had their hair waved to the minute. In a
pause of the conference work Dr. Arthur handed
over a number of letters from English women
who were said to be desirous of emigrating to
Australia, and to these inquirers Miss Watson will
reply. . One girl who wrote said that she and her
sisters lived in a village of 800 inhabitants, and
there wasn’t an unmarried man in the population.
I here were several families, seven strong, of
grown girls, nice young women who would come
under the awful designation of “ladies.” The
writer further explained that domestic service for
her and ner girl friends would mean ostracism in
their own country, but they would willingly under-
take that or nursery-governessing in Australia,
bhe wanted information and guidance. There
must be thousands of this sort of girl in caste-
ridden England, and if the Eeague will but
send Miss Watson and her friend “Mrs. Smith”
there to yard them for Australia, this country will
forgive the League for some of the horribly foolish
papers that were perpetrated here under its name,
and which it couldn’t even read intelligently.
That much-paragraphed person, Mr. Arthur
Mahaffy, passed through Sydney last week on
Ins way to the Solomons, where the boiled mis-
sionary is kept on the sideboard. Miss Beatrice
Gnmshaw went by the same vessel—the Moresby.
All well-frocked Sydney went to “Glen-
J-rrah, to Mrs. Bertie Kelly’s garden fete in aid
-A.ll baints Church, WoolJahra. One hundred
beautiful sovereigns were realised.
A crowd of well-known Sydnev-siders leave for
foreign parts at the end of the year. The Darleys
are going, so are the “Fiona” Knoxes, and their
house will be taken by the Macarthur Onslows.
Mrs. J. 1. loohey is another intending goer. To
balance these departures, the Lucas Tooths will
return to Sydney at Christmas. The lady is said
to wear the latest in clothes.
A fellow passenger of Lady Dudley’s is Mr.
Lroctor, the gentleman who is supposed to have
started his voyage with the intention of appro-
priating that blessing in skirts, Carrie Moore.
Mr. Henry Montague Faithfull, who died last
week, was the father of that ornamental damsel,
Miss Pearl Faithfull, who used to act the good
fairy sister by Grace Palotta. Her stature and
her fathoms of fine red hair make her a huge
success in theatrical tableaux of the “Queen
Elizabeth” and “The Sea Hath its Pearls”
variety, and in mere human or fleshly tableaux on
sea beaches where bathers breast the surf and
swallow pints of ocean, and come up gasping.
The above mention of Queen Elizabeth reminds
me that films showing that ruddy Queen and her
beautiful rival, Mary of Scotland, at different
phases of their wild career, are on the screen at
West s Pictures. When Mary is escaping down a
desolate mountain slope after the battle of
Langside, Elizabeth, correctly dressed and
ruffed, but not hatted or crowned, rushes up
with a horde of minions and helps to take the
g ? l G en ' haired one P risoner - But it is at the trial
of Mary (according to West) that we see the best
stage effect. Elizabeth, well in the foreground,
presides at the trial (West treats history as glaziers
do putty), and after the sentence Mary marches
down, draws herself to her full height of
about nine feet, clenches her little hand
across her black velvet bodice, throws up
her chin, and looks contempt and defiance
at her rival. You can almost hear her hissing the
word “Ginger!” Once in real life I saw one
woman cut ’ another. It was done after the
best traditions of factory girldom. The cutter was
a small fat party, and she jerked her short skirts
and her chin sideways with a simultaneous move-
ment, and at the same time pushed up her lower
lip, and cast down her eyelids as if she meant to
drop them on the floor. Then she said “Honk ”
like a motor car. The “cut” party, shaking with
inward laughter, did not recover in time to reply
“Toot.”
Another film shows Johnson, the colored stoush-
merchant. As Lady Dudley has presented him
with a breast-pin for being the best-dressed man
on board the liner on which she travelled, he
really must be threatened with intelligence.
Perhaps he appeared at the ship’s fancv-dress ball
as a cake-walker. In that character he would
probably deserve the pin. Johnson’s “ high ball ”
talk at Fremantle is a beautiful pretence of per-
sonal feeling against Tommy Burns. Squires and
Burns talked as if they didn’t love each other
even in step-brother fashion, but the night after
the fight Mr. Squires dined with Mr. and Mrs.
Noah Brusso. The incident was not mentioned
by the Society papers.
The stork’s beak-1 uggage deposited last week
with the Alec Gordons (she was Miss Margaret
Thomas) was a son.
The most striking bit of young womanhood at
present decorating Sydney ’is Mrs. Gouriet, wife
of Victor Gouriet, who gets himself up like an
old faun to play the Ambassador in “ The Merry
Widow.” Slie isn’t of the starved type on which
the empire or directoire gowns hang to such un-
speakable advantage. Her favorite fixings are
pale tan frocks, cerise edgings, and a black hat on
wonderfully red hair—just such a head of hair as
Lovey Mary wears in “Mrs. Wiggs.” Lovey
Mary’s may have been a wig, but, if so, it was the
wig of the Cabbage Patch, and a beauty. But
Mrs. Gouriet’s is undoubtedly the home-grown
article.
An imposing group of distinguished merchants
came together at the Australia Hotel on Monday
afternoon to tell Mr. A. H. Norman, of Gavin
Gibson and Co., of their sympathy with him
in the recent vexatious litigation into which
he had been forced. Mr. David Storey,
from his eminence in the chair, made
a genial and sympathetic speech, and the toast
of the guest was supported by Mr. Alec Thomp-
son, of Mosman, who incidentally mentioned that
the musical growth of Mosman was mainly due to
Mr. Norman’s efforts. The presentation of an
illuminated address and a gold watch backed the
appreciatory speeches.
Most of Sydney’s pretty young women who
can afford it are exodussing, or have already
exodussed, to Melbourne for the Cup. A few
will enjoy free board and “scoff’’with friends
(Mrs. Herbert Marks, for instance, is to be put up
by the Willy Kellys), but the majority are hasten-
ing to long-booked rooms in the Yarra-side
hostelries.
Dr. Tange, of Mosman, was married last week
to a Mosman pretty—a Miss Harden.
Lady Cynthia Almina Constance Mary Need-
ham, who is about to wed Viscount Villiers, ex-
Governor Jersey’s eldest son, is daughter of
Francis Charles Needham, who, as Viscount
Newry, visited Australia in the ducal Edinburgh’s
suite in the ’sixties. When here he was heir to
bis grandfather, Earl Kilmorey, an Irish peer of
County Down. He came into his kingdom in
1880, so F. C. Needham is now Earl Kilmorey
himself, which same is pronounced Kilmurry, and
don t you forget it. The rather ancient peer (he
is 66) is said to have 46,000 acres of land. It is
understood that the meek shall inherit the earth,
but they seem to have to wait a long time before
coming into their property, and meanwhile the
Kilmurry class is using the earth for all it is
worth.
Sir Harry Rawson and suite put up at Coolan-
gatta on their trip through the South Coast dis-
tricts. Coolangatta is the ancestral seat of the
Hay family, and a very pleasant place to sit down
in it is, too, by all accounts. Apropos of which I
often wonder if ourGovernorsand their hosts realise
that in these fleeting visits minor history is being
made, and a theme given for unlimited lying on
the part of future generations. “ Here,” one con-
ceives young Mac Smith saying haughtily to some
upstart friend a couple of hundred years hence,
is the Blue Room. It was at this identical table
my ancestor won four bob from the State
Governor of the day. The Governor’s name was
either Carrutliers or Bigwood; I have for-
gotten which. The game was a now extinct one,
named Bridge. The Gov. settled with an I 0 U,
which he never redeemed. There it is in the
frame over the electric stove. We would not part
with it for a fortune.” And so on. When Charles
11. insisted on butting into some loyalist mansion
during the troublous period of his life, and so
laying the owners open to proceedings for harbor-
ing him the while he made violent love to anv
skirted thing that crossed his grip-radius, the
romantic interest of his presence went unap-
preciated. The same remark applies to Queen
Elizabeth, Henry VIII., and other sovereigns who
sped from place to place and put up at their
subjects’ abodes in the intervals of speeding. It
is to be hoped that our indigenous nobility with
the example of history behind them will not err
in this fashion. The cigar the contemporary vice-
regality said he’d be blanked if he smoked, the
old and useless pair of boots that he stowed
furtively behind the wardrobe ere departing,
the pool-ball which he drove through the billiard-
room window in an earnest attempt at a “long
pot,” will all have points of absorbing interest to
coming generations. The modern who aspires to
win the regard of posterity will attend to these
matters.
I looked in at “Diana of Dobson’s” just in
time to hear the bounder knight of the piece
ask Diana “’Ow would you like me for a
usband ? ” and didn’t find Miss Brune a bit
objectionable in the part of the London shop-
girl. She looked well, too, frocked in buttons and
some brown, clinging, silky material. Buttons to
right of her, buttons to left of her, both
arms outlined with buttons from shoulder to
wrist, buttons at the back of her waist, and
before her from neck to skirt-
hem. She looked quite pretty, and if she didn’t
wear a cabbage of blue ribbon under her
ear she would look prettier. It thickens her neck
and has naught to balance it on the other side.
Yet if she wore another chou under the other ear
she d look like a pony when it wears those rosettes
which we know so well in equine garnishings.
Mrs. Brough—a cerise and blade Mrs. Brough—
looked stronger and better than of yore ; and
Kingston is it possible any man, even in London
society, could look and act so like an imbecile and
not have a keeper lurking in the shadows of
Aunty’s drawing-room?
A mostly feminine audience was asked, on
Saturday night last, to credit that Diana could
frock herself in Paris gowns and things from
head to foot on the paltry sum of £3OO, and be
able, moreover, out of the change to put in a
month at a hotel in the Swiss Engadine. The
idea is as preposterous as anything that has yet
been written in an Anti-Socialist “paper.”' A
Doucet, Redfern, or Worth confection is liable
at any time to cost a couple of hundred
pounds on its lonely own. It could
not possibly come to a maravedi less
than 25 or 30 sovereigns cash down. “Paris
models ” in the way of blouses vary between five
and 10 guineas. Hats touch the 20-guinea mark
with appalling frequency, and no self-respecting
establishment on the correct side of the Seine
would dream of stocking one of less intrinsic worth
than a five-pound note. A female with shaky
grammar and a desire to be taken for the possessor
of £3600 a year could not inhabit a Swiss health
resort with less than (say) a dozen frocks and a
like number of skull coverings. Remains the
question of boots, shoes, convincing toilet-table
fixings, jewellery, and above all, “undies.” The
last item alone would, at a moderate estimate,
swallow up half of Diana’s £3OO. There are
but two brands of “undies” known to Parisians
—the right sort and the “other sort.” The
“ other sort” are moderate in price, but the wear-
ing of them by a travelling widow, genuine or
shaln, is tantamount to social felo de se so soon as
the other maids’ maids shall have inspected them,
and reported on their quality at headquarters.
The right sort are the most ruinously expensive
items in all haberdashery. Provided Diana’s
trinkets were the cheapest class of Parisian goods,
she might conceivably have got the collection she
sports nightly at the Royal for between £5O and
£lOO. But I doubt it. Anyhow, the modern
health-resort frequenter is an expert judge of
paste, and a shameless hand at begging the sus-
pected gaud from its owner, and—under a hypo-
critical mask of admiration—subjecting it to a
rigorous examination. No; Diana never fitted
herself out and achieved that glorious month on
£3OO. It is one of the few intelligence-scorning
items in a clever play.
The name of Diana reminds me that I’ve just
read Mrs. Humphrey Ward’s latest, “ Diana Mal-
lory.” Diana is such an impeccable gentlewoman.
And she couldn’t be a Mrs. Ward heroine if she
wasn’t puddling in politics. But it is a book that
holds, and the writer is at her best when she’s on
the analysis of human motive. Also Diana has
“ deep blue eyes,” then she has “short-sighted,
intense brown eyes.” It is useful to be able to
change your eyes to match your frocks and hats.
Good news for the melomaniaes: Katharine
Goodson will play with the Amateur Orchestral
Society on her return to Sydney next month.
At the Musical Festival meeting in the Town
Hall, H. N. Southwell fired a bomb in the shape
of encouraging letters from some great guns of the
musical world. The new G.-G. agreed to be-
come patron, and in rapid succession letters
were read from Lady Northcote (with a fiver),
Kubelik, Canon Duckworth (who wrote from
County Cork), Melba (whose letter had a world-
known coronet on top), Lady May Trefusis (well-
regarded as Lady May Lygon), and from
Dolores, who sent four pages from Amsterdam-
four pages of common sense and kindliness, say-
ing she would feel it an honor if the committee
would accept her services to sing at the opening
concert.
Someone writing about the frocks of Alice
Crawford (the Bendigo actress in London) in a
new play mentions that one of her gowns has
buttons from the throat to the hem. Which
reminds that the blue silk matinee gown in which
Ada Crossley made her last appearance in Sydney
had big, blue silk buttons set closely together back
and front, from one pole of the garment to the
other.
Some interesting criticism of actresses’ com-
plexions appears among the London advts. of
Helena Rubinstein, that remarkable Russian girl
who started a skin-food business in Australia, and
has extended it to all the big cities of the earth,
seemingly. The advt. professes to quote from a
lecture given by the skin-food lady before a
Viennese Society of Actors :
Now while I have already mentioned that the fineness
of the skin-fabric of actresses is a noteworthy fact that is
largely due to the abundant lubrication it receives, it
would, speaking with all frankness, be a vain endeavor to
look for healthy, fresh skin-color among them. And this
leads us to the con. of the grease-paint question. The
lubricants used, being inert, inactive, bland, and in-
different substances, not only fail to stimulate the skin
to properly throw off the injurious effects of the pig-
ments, but they depress and flatten it. as it were. This
circumstance more than anything else in the theatrical
life is responsible for the typical tired face of the actress.
This Suggestion of cause for effect is merely inci-
dental to an advt., and, possibly, is specious non-
sense, but it certainly gives the “actress” face a
true complexion. With few exceptions the stage
woman who has passed 30 is a tired or faded
looking charmer. And this appearance is doubt-
less due, mainly, to the grease she rubs into her
face skin, and which serves to fill up the pores and
give a fineness to the “fabric,” as the advt. de-
clares. But there remains the question as to
whether grease by any other name will act differ-
ently towards the complexion. It may smell
sweeter in its character of skin food, but it con-
tinues to be grease.
“ Mario ” writes from Brisbane :
The day this gets into print, or is published in the dust-
bin, the Chelmsford lord and bis liege lady, flanked by the
smileful Verney, will be in your midst, going in search of
the Cup that cheers when the favorite comes in first, and
groans miserably when it doesn’t.
It’s a queer, topsy-turvy world, and most things seem to
happen “ reversally speaking,” as the lady remarked in
“ Davy and the Gobelin.” Therefore, it is not strange to
find that among the energetic people who are to reform
the milk trade, save the babies’ lives, and strike terror into
the heart of the destroyer of the infant Australian,
few have ever possessed a nearer infant relative
than a nephew or niece. Spinsters, doctors femi-
nine and single, doctors male and single, and mar-
ried doctors who are “without encumbrance,”
as Ann Gwendoline of the kitchen is expected to be else
she won’t get the job, and a few celibate clergy are in
the van of the battle and the forefront of the feeding-
bottle. It’s the same with the new Child Study Associa-
tion, which teems with unappropriated treasures and
clergy (can this be the cause and effect ?), while in the
local Ministering Children’s Deague there are more
bachelor-ladies than infants.
The Chelmsford lady presided over an erratic meeting
of the said Child Study Association this week, and
listened with patience and resignation to a long-wdnded
paper on how to train the young mind. Judging by the
manners of her own infants, however, she could give the
lecturer points.
Rumor is strong again this week touching the matter
of a master for the newly-acquired marble halls in
London, which constitute the premises of the Agent-
General for Queensland, and some say that handsome
Mrs. Joey Bell will yet attain her ambition and reign
therein.
Among Brisbane visitors to your harbor side this week
are Mr. and Mrs. Sc-hleussener. The latter was better
known until Wednesday last as contralto Haidee Lamb, a
sweet-voiced singer who somehow never got her full
share of recognition. And, talking of such things, I hear
that a certain local girl doctor, who is at present attend-
ing the Medical Conference, is about to throw up the
medicine bottle and the amputating implements in favor
of the domestic sausage machine. She does this at the
earnest request of a brother in the profession.
Attorney-General Blair (or he was such up till Friday)
opened the picture show of the new Society of Artists in
the crumbling walls of the council chambers. It’s a
strange show, wherein the very good and very bad
are jumbled together cheerfully. That the bad is bad
there’s no denying, and a portrait of James himself belongs
more or less to this category ; but the good spots are very
good indeed, and the work of Wirth, Elliott, and others
is refreshingly Australian, and cheerful Australian at that.
On the whole, the show proves the seriousness of the loss
sustained by the old society, while in point of popularity
it beats the ancient institution hollow.

Mrs. Kidston, Premieress of Queensland, had a special
suiprise packet awaiting her upon her recent home-
coming. A maid had been installed—a luxury the frill-
less, domesticated little Scotchwoman had never previ-
ously possessed. She had hitherto done all her own
housework.

PIANOLA
PIANO.
“ Never in my whole life have I received a present which gives me so
much pleasure.”— From a recent letter which is typical of many.
The pleasure derived from a Steck Pianola Piano begins with the very moment it is placed
in the home. It continues each and every day of the year. The whole family can enjoy it, and
even the giver himself will take pleasure in it.
The popularity of the Steck Pianola Piano as a gift is witnessed not only in the great
demand for it, but also in the many imitations with which the market is now crowded, yet none
of which contain the important features that have made the Pianola the standard instrument of
its class throughout the world.
The Pianola Piano is an upright piano of the highest grade, and in addition contains within
its case a complete Themodist and Metrostyle Pianola, the only Piano-playing device endorsed
by the greatest living musicians.
It can be played by the fingers on the keyboard in the usual way.
It can be played by means of a Pianola action within its case.
There is nothing to move up in front of the keyboard. To change the method from hand-
playing to Pianola-playing it is only necessary to slide back a panel in the front of the case and
insert a perforated roll of music. ... ,
There is no outward indication which catches the eye to distinguish the Pianola Piano trom
the heretofore type of Pianoforte. . . ,
A cordial invitation to call is extended to everyone who may be interested in hearing tne
Pianola-piano, irrespective of any intention of purchase.
If unable to call, write for Catalogue C. It will be sent free for the asking.
The Pianola Company Pty. Ltd.,
COLLINS STREET, MELBOURNE. Also at BRISBANE and ADELAIDE.

The Vic. Minister for Public Works had
one of his slack days lately, so a few of the Vic.
country shires took an opportunity of looking him
up, just for old acquaintance sake. There were
13 of them in all, and, while spouting apropos of
nothing, or something less than that, thev were
struck with an idea. It was sudden and unanim-
ous. It had occurred to them that, while they
were on the premises, they might as well ask the
Government for a trifle. It was probably more
from habit than necessity, but here is the trifle :
Mildura, £250, road works ; Tungairiah, £lOOO, road
repairs ; Omeo, £ll5O, bridge repairs ; Avon, £5O, road
works ; Sebastopol, £250, channelling ; Upper Yarra,
£4090, roads and bridges; Newham, £2OO, bridge;
Benalla, £lOOO, road repairs; Chewton, £9OO, roads and
bridges ; Mfc. Franklin, £l5O, road; Woorayl and Nar-
racan, £707, road ; Mirboo, £1675, roads ; Buln Buln,
£3220, roads and bridges, and £5OO, drainage works.
That was all. They merely requested that the
city taxpayer should make them a present of
£15,142j and, as Bent wants every vote he can
secure, they will probably get it. Adolphus must
placate them or burst his boiler.

Reliability, durability and efficiency may be considered
as going hand in hand as the first considerations in the
manufacture of the most desirable Motor Tyres and
in no tyres will this trinity of features be found more
pronounced than in the ever-popular Continental variety.
The Continental Co.’s interpretation of the word
“reliability” is “ability to get there, and not only
to get there, but to get there well; to be certain at the
commencement of a journey that the end is in sight.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 26, 1908.
My Dear Yarrik,—
The announcement of the death of Mrs. John
Grice, mentioned last week, was a most regret-
table error. It can only be hoped that the lady
will now enjoy an. extra long and happy life, by
way of compensation for the mistake.
Henley on the Yarra is growing. It had the
benefit of perfect weather this year ; but that was
not responsible for all. The crowd was thousands
bigger than before, and the innovation of shunting
houseboats and reserves to the other side of the
river gave the swarms room not only to
tnove, but to show-off. The women’s frocks
went almost one better than those they displayed
at the races. The far side of the river—owing to
steady years of honest improvement—is now a
grassy terrace, with rose gardens and shrubberies
within, and the fine tan curve of Alexandra
avenue without. It all made a beautiful
and un-Yarra-like approach to the houseboats.
These have grown in .number and gorgeosity.
Even the backgrounding rocks that wall the summit
of the terrace put on Henley garniture. They
are covered with that tenacious little creeper that
only shows its flowers in the sun. Children call it
“ pig face.” I struggle with its true name, which
is Mesembryanthemum, because, like Meso-
potamia, it has a comforting sound. Anyhow,
the mesem-etceteras roused . themselves in the
glorious sunlight to colors like soft Persian rugs
hung over the balconies of Heaven. Grassy ter-
races and beautiful frocks and the heaps of pretty
girl had not only this wall for a frame, but also
the green upward sweep of reserves and gardens
that hold Government House on their summit.
The Carmichael,Governor people had a houseboat
on their blue-and-golden own. The Alexandra
Club—which is the high-toned Woman Club—
covered its watery abode with vast derangements
of red, red roses. ' The Australian Club wore
pale, anaemic blue. Mrs. Donald Mackinnon
and Mrs. L. K. S. Mackinnon had rival
boats on either side. The Wallabies just
shrieked in yellow—hot, mustardy yellow.
The Bohemians gave a modest,little echo in pale
primrose with lilies round about. The Medical
Congress boat—with a heavy list to port through
having too much grey matter on that side—
.shouted in red—blood red. The medicoes like the
color; it reminds them of well-paid operations.
And so on went the houseboats, way round the
curve.
The small craft on the river were every-
thing and anything—smart little motor launches
that whizzed about ; boats weighed down with
girl till they draggled their decoration petticoats
in the water; paddle canoes and everything
that would float, decorated in some carnival
way to hide the clumsy old timbers. The just-
returned Clegg girls' drooped on pink cushions
dressed in Early Victorian gear—but the boat was
plain Bill and undecorated, and the languorous
maidens looked forlorn and out of their .century.
The smartest motor launch out was the Ermerance.
I took its name and number in order that I
might report its disgraceful behavior. , It
was the only boat without a girl. In fact,
it was chock-full of Man—black crow-like
man—and it dashed past everything else
like a swallow. No one Seems to have discovered
yet who was the magnificent Titian-haired
goddess in Wedgwood blue who lounged among
blue and white cushions under a blue parasol.
She and the lucky man who rowed her had the
boat to themselves, and there were some few
glasses pointed that way. The Athertons, with
much red about their furbelows, had Jack
M‘Comas rowing. Otto Linden’s beauty daughter
sailed in pale cool blue, with Harry Whiting as
attendant swain. Sporting Bob Whiting was
hosting on the Athenaeum Club houseboat,
the most perfectly-appointed craft on the river.
It was bedecked in blue and brown by day, and
showed masts and headlights and an outlining of
blue and gold electrics at night. The pretty
Chicago girls wore blue and brown, and ladled ou t
the cooling ice or “something warmer.”
Much visiting went on from one boat to another,
and the Savage Clubbers, who have their coming
theatrical show to advertise, took possession of an
effective strip of terrace for their tent—a terrifying
place with scalps and masks and clubs and horrid
knives hung at the entrance.
At night, Henley forgot it was playing on the
plain old brown Yarra. All the terrace was
lighted, and on the slopes deck-chairs spread
themselves like yacht sails on a holiday after-
noon. A huge searchlight played about to help
craft in danger, and the houseboats blinked in soft
radiance. The big masses of crowds on the unre-
served off-bank looked like a mosaic or a carved
wall of white faces and color. On the river there
were no spare inches, except round some sputter-
ing, spitting rockets that had a base in the centre.
Everybody on the small craft alternated the
slow shove down the crazy carnival stream with
supper parties on the houseboats. It always
meant climbing a bridge of intervening boats to
get there. It was the night of the Petticoat.
The girl who was not trimly clad shoeward or
without something particularly fetching and frilly
in underskirt simply had to moan in a back seat.
Yet in the daytime Fashion has doomed the fasci-
nating ruffles of Petticoat. It exists no more
with the sheath of day dress, and tights and silk
socks push it into the background at night.
But it had its revival at Henley.
The Athenaeum Club added to its other hospital-
ities by engaging half-a-dozen motor-launches for
tripping its guests down the river, and a fascinat-
ing little boatload of singers in mask and domino
was anchored at the steps to make sweet music.
I think I recognised Baritone Turner, and a red
mask and domino sounded very like the warble of
Topsy Conn. They had violins to accompany
them, and even a small pocket affair that may
have been a harpsichord.
The inaugural meeting of the Medical Congress
on Monday afternoon was at the University. The
meeting that followed in the evening was a semi-
social affair in the Town Hall. It was social in-
asmuch as womenkind were invited. They all
sat in rows on chairs and listened to Governor Car-
michael, Minister Swinburne, and President Pro-
fessor Allen talk at length against a background of
important medicoes —a grim black wall on the
platform. Lady Carmichael and Mrs. Prof. Allen
sat at their feet. Wives and medical husbands
filled the hall to its farthest wall, except in the
south gallery, where nurses, like a host of white
angels, sat arrayed in white uniform and a sancti-
fied odor of iodoform. The night was as cold as
the top of a charitable iceberg. The paper Prof.
Allen read was long—very long. It was also in-
teresting—fascinatingly so to those who could hear
—but the voice did not carry well to the back bal-
conies. Some people, wedged in there with cold
feet and ears spread out like angels’ wings to
catch something of the discourse, got tired and
sneaked out quietly to a warm Vienna Cafe
supper. They had just got gaily to work on a
devilled bone when another medical contingent
sneaked in. Then more contingents arrived, all
with cold feet and a wild cry for a devilled bone.
The local medical fraternity has been extensively
dining its visiting brother-in-saw during the Con-
gress. Clever Surgeon Moore, who tops Collins-
street, gave a big dinner t’other night. Daven-
port, that most sporting of medicoes, gave a
20-guest-power man-dinner at the Athenaeum
Club of which filtered tales drift to envious
feminine ears.
The Carmichael Governor has been a frequent
and most welcome guest at many of these Man
dinners. His Governorship hath a soft and pretty
wit with his slow speech.
President Prof. Allen and Mrs. Allen gave a
be-a-utiful party at the University on Friday
evening, in honor of the visiting “ meds.” The
reception room was the wide, open stretch of the
grounds clothed in soft, mild moonlit night—the
paths and the trees and the lake made just visible
by thousands of soft-colored electrics. In this
mystical light quite a thousand well-dressed
women trailed pale silky draperies. The men
were sombre silhouettes. Gowns and hoods were
taboo, and in their conventional black and gleaming
white attire they looked like a herd of melodrama
villains. The dull black crush hat of every impor-
tant medico from far and near was collected there.
What became of the conglomerate patient this
last week it’s hard to say; but he probably died, or
got quite well. It was a great chance for those
patients who had been on a fair way to recovery.
They were left free from interruption. Guests
who had no soul for lighted gardens and grey,
ivy-clad walls, had a brilliant concert in the Wilson
Hall to fall back upon, and supper in the Conserv-
atorium. The Conservatorium Hall, once the
Museum and stony tomb of long-dead bones,
rejuvenated its grim self with a mile or so of pink
poppies, and an orchestra that lilted the latest
waltz.
Professor and Mrs. Allen received Everybody
on a scarlet carpet,under an awning just outside
Wilson Hall. There were a couple of thousand
or so of guests and the avenues were lit with an
unbroken line of acetylene motor eyes. The
Carmichael Lady, attended by her lord in black
and white, was a graceful presence in blue—
sapphire-blue cr6pe that had silver tissue to
mount it. The Wrixons were there, the
better half in purple. Big John Forrest brought
his lady, muffled in furs and wraps—all that ap-
peared to the naked eye was the top of her head
and a gay little wreath of silver leaves. There
are some beautiful women among our doctors’
wives. They were all at this party, in their most
fascinating frocks. The women doctors them-
selves are not too keen on worldly dress, but
Dr. Helen Sexton looked quite striking in a
classical cloak of dark blue covering a white dress.
At the concert, Conservatorium girls quartetted in
song and white gear. Miss Hesketh Jones
warbled, and Alberto' Zelman played divinely in
solo, and with ’Cellist Hattenbach. Hattenbach
mentioned incidentally that he is going to bring
his big fiddle to bear upon a Beethoven concerto
with Rita Hope, when that bright young pianiste
gives her show at the Town Hall next month.
The Congress of the medicoes has been respon-
sible for a furious outbreak of Garden Party.
Toorak caught the complaint in its direct form.
Unfortunately sleet and hail and rain assailed the
garden movement. I should like to record that
the Fraternity and its aggregate wife, being
authorities on hygienic clothing, attended these
wet functions in bathing suits, and took vigorous
exercise and a warm rub-down afterwards, but I
can’t. The authorities upon the exact way to live
wore the idiotic frockcoat and the plug-hat that
holds a quart of rain when it is turned the wrong
way up, just like uninformed mortals. And their
wives dragged soggy chiffons over the grass, and
exposed their pneumonia areas through trans-
parencies, and sat in damp, dainty shoes while
they ate the cold, cold trifle and the chilly ice-
cream. So one’s faith in the learned gentlemen
who have been discoursing so eloquently on up-to-
date health measures all this week is reduced to
the quantity that is less than nothing. Ah, me !
as the old-fashioned maiden raves in the novelette
wherein the beautiful young Countess de Slump is
imprisoned in a fortress by her cruel uncle.
Ada Crossley’s houses have been steadily grow-
ing. Her final vocal outpouring, on Thursday,
packed the Town Hall to the point at which there
was standing room only—and the organ loft over-
flowed with angelic, white-robed woman choir.
Throughout the season Miss Crossley has worn
such a series of wickedly fascinating frocks as one
could scarcely hope to see outside a ballet. One
of them was a pink as tender as a baby shell,
glowing into a luscious rose where the light darted
through its texture. A yellow sheath, with no-
thing to spare, had fantastic dragons climbing and
clambering all over the premises. One night the
lady was green from her shoe tip to the summit of an
osprey hat that shouted like sudden spring. Miss
Crossley has now gone east to the land where the
Big Trees grow, and where the soft air of the hills
turns every milkmaid’s voice into melody. The
Big Tree Land is the birth-place of the songstress,
and she has taken her wonderful frocks and her
wonderful smile and her voice and her husband to
stagger the natives and the woolly bears with
wonder.
Margaret Anglin leaves us at the end of the
month for Adelaide. She has two weeks’ engage-
ment there and four nights in Ballarat to follow.
On November 21st she is due again at Sydney
in “The Taming of the Shrew.” She stays
there a month, then she and sister Eileen leave by
the Mongolia. They propose to land at Port
Said and travel to Cairo for a fling before journey-
ing on to the Thames-side city. From December
the lady means to make one long vacation until
September of next year. Manager Nethersole
has booked her for the Hollis-street Theatre, at
Boston (U.S.A.), for that month. She is to
appear in “ The Awakening of Helena Ritchie.”
Clarence Weber’s Victorian Health and
Strength College is expanding into new and
larger rooms, with up-to-date fittings, that rejoice
the heart of the modern Hercules. The new
college had a double-barrelled opening ceremony
last week. There was a night for men and
another display—mildly tempered and more
clothed—to which ladies were invited. The
amazons of the classes were to have exhibited on
the ladies’ night, but when men appeared as
escorts, most of the muscular ones shivered into
modesty, and refused to “ expose,” as the French
say. Nevertheless, some muscular young men
in very thin clothes went through jiu-jitsu exer-
cises and marvellous feats of “ lifting,” and fired
the ambitions of soft and flabby ones among the
onlookers. Weber himself spent the time in
graceful flannels between a womanly Guild expo-
sition in the Masonic and his own classes.
As well as classes for mere male man, Weber
has a school for girls—adoring creatures who go
through their jiu-jitsu as rites before a shrine,
and are glad from their top hair-pin.down to their
boots when they discover the steadily increasing
suppleness and improvement of their figures.
A physical culture girl is adaptable to all the
new;-figure changes that fashion flings at cringing,
subservient women.
Poor old Herbert Flemming is dead. Flem-
ming, despite his ill health, was always cheerful.
“Just wait till sunshiny weather comes again and
I’ll be tip-top,” were his hopeful words. Sun-
shine’s been late in coming this year, and the
good soul went under, hopeful to the last. On
more than one occasion he had been on the point
of dying in his dressing-room, within ten minutes
of the curtain rising on a comedy in which he
presently went on and played his part.
Percy Grainger has again distinguished himself
—this time his feet are the performing members.
Last visit he walked some incredible distance jn
Maoriland within some remarkable period. This
time he has been doing the same sort of business in
Victoria. From Yarram to Sale, a 46-miles step, in
11J hours, is an allegro furioso performance. And he
finished allegro risoluto over loose sandy soil to a
capital audience of cyclists and hoof-padders.
If any man says the distinguished player is handi-
capped with, his name of Percy and his golden
hair—well, that man hath sought an invention.
Which is the Biblical manner of intimating that
he has been bowled out.
Mr. Roderick Murchison, that most amiable
and hospitable squire o’ dames, came back by
the China from far places. He was welcomed ’
with heartfelt joy by any number of widows and
confirmed spinsters and nursery belles. The
vivacious old gentleman makes a hobby of being
a dear to nice youngsters, and a perfectly sweet
person to the relict who is charming but lonely,
and the maid who isn’t as young as her prettiest
sister.
Mrs. Janet Clarke is commencing to feel some-
what perkier. After all her trials of the flesh,
she is sufficiently recovered to be chaired and
carried to a sun-warmed balcony from which she
can look at the world for an hour or so. She is a
changed Dowager these days. The jetty hair shows
touches of white, and the clever, energetic face is
a grey mask in which the black eyes look three
sizes too large. But the lady’s pluck and cheer-
fulness have never failed, and she means to be on
deck again before Christmas, and probably she’ll
arrive there looking as handsome as ever. Courage
can work wonders.
A farewelling tea was given in The Chicago to
Mrs. Fred Danby, a most popular dame with a
Voice, who goes to try her luck in the West. The
pleasant Henry Butler, as spokesman for her
friends, presented the departing one with a purse
that held close on a hundred golden guineas.
From “Rosna,” in Adelaide
Our Exes, went moting to Mount Gambier last week,
and spent a few days with the Clarkes, of Mount
Schank. The Mayor and corporation, and the local
slice of our leaning army, and all the celebrities the
town could show, met them, and sang anthems at
them, and they even survived the excitements of the
Mount Gambier Show. It was a sort of a farewell visit,
and, before they left, the viceregals ran up the Mount,
shook it kindly by the crater, and said they were
pleased to have met a real volcano, even though it was
moderately *dead. The town presented his tall Ex. with
an address. He’ll be able to paper his ancestral castle
with the addresses that have been thrust at him.
The Bony Personage with the Scythe was busy in
Adelaide last week. There passed out V. L. Solomon, the
jolly member for the Northern Territory; architect
Bayer, of Glenelg; and Mrs. Charlie Hamilton, wife of
the medico, a charming woman who could be ill-spared.
The Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals
is having a busy time over the tramway business, and
Mrs. Ennis roams the landscape daily, keeping hours that
amount to cruelty to woman. While the electric lines
are being planted the old horse ruins run on single lines,
and the consequent overcrowding is enough to make a
horse’s mother weep for her child. Mrs. Ennis lately
spent one whole day on the kerb in front of the City
Baths, that being the place where the poor, rackety
steeds come in for most maltreatment; and she has her
inspectors stationed all over the place. She has even
succeeded in persuading a throng of North Adelaide folk
not to use the horse cars, but to walk back and forth
until the new vehicles are ready.
The Kindergarten movement grow T s apace, and the
latest report shows that every day more infants than can
be accommodated are clamoring for admission and pound-
ing with their feeding-bottles on the door. There are
three free kindergartens with 150 kiddies, and 17 nice
girls are being trained for the work by the young
principal, Miss de Lissa. Mrs. Percy Morice, wife of the
Parliamentary Librarian, is the energetic secretary and
prime mover, and anyone who is bowed down with
shekels is advised to communicate with her.
The marriage of the Charles Angas’ daughter Beryl
and ex-aide Captain Ryder occurred at St. George’s,
Hanover Square, Fogville, last month, the deed being
done by Bishop Harmer, erstwhile of this See. The
bride wore white, satin brocade and had six brides-
maidens and two train-bearers. The benedict has a
brother who is a journeyman earl : he kindly vacated
one of the ancestral castles so that the pair might honey-
moon therein. Also, there are about eight titled sisters
in the family. The Tennyson twain were among those
present.
Adelaide folk, gazing on the sweet countenance of
Olive Godwin at the Royal, can’t cease to marvel. When
she was here last year, and the appendicitis fiend had her
down and was sitting like a big oak bookcase on her
chest, nobody thought she’d ever charm the multi-
tude again. The authorities reckoned hers was the
largest case of appendicitis they had ever struck, and her
recovery is considered something out of the common dog-
trot of events.
Muriel Matters, the Adelaide elocution girl, who went
seeking laurels in Bullopolis a few years ago, has turned
suffragette. She spends her days addressing indoor and
outdoor meetings, chalking pavements, and in other
equally in tehee tua l pursuits, but whether she has reached
the altitude of being gaoled yet we do not know.
Yours affectionately,
J OHANNA.

CONTINENTAL SQUARE TREADS.
The new Continental Square Tread Model de Course Motor Tyre is the
heaviest and strongest pneumatic tyre in the world to-day. It is so
designed that the rubber thickness reaches its maximum strength where
strength is most needed, viz., on the tread. The wear and tear falls
precisely on that part of the tyre best able to stand rough usage. The
defensive powers ” of the tyre have been massed in just that position
where the road attack is severest.
CONTINENTAL ANTI-SKID LEATHER TREADS.
The new Anti-Skid Leather Tread Continental Motor Tyres have been
specially designed to meet the requirements of stony road and wet weather
conditions. The leather is cured to the rubber by a secret steam process—not
a chemical process as is usually employed—and it is impossible for the leather
to cause any trouble by lifting. This new type of Anti-skid Continentals
reaches the highest point ever attempted in non-skid devices. Motorists
who have tried and tested the new tyre are loud in their praises of it.

All who are interested in Australian
national politics should read “ The Commonwealth
Crisis,” by C. H. Kirmess, the great serial now
running through The Lone Hand. Forecasting
a “peaceful” Japanese colonisation of the
Northern Territory, Kirmess traces the political
and war consequences with a masterly hand.
See that you do not miss “The Commonwealth

RHEUMATISM.
Cubed by Dk. Williams’ Pink Pills, and the
Proof is Hebe.
Nothing better demonstrates that rheumatism
is a disease of the blood than the fact that it is
hereditary in certain families. It may not develop
until conditions of cold and dampness favor it, be-
cause Nature is always fighting to keep the body
in health. When disease does get the upper hand
the rheumatism is seated in the blood. A remedy
that will revitalise and renew the blood is the
simplest and best means of relief.
A remarkable instance of the value of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills in this disease is shown by
the cure they effected in the case of Mr. James
Walker Peacock, St. John Street, Pyrmont,
Sydney.
“I had rheumatism off and on for about two
years,” he said. “I was attacked from the neck
to both hips, across the shoulders and down the
arms. Every now and then I’d have to knock off
work and lay up at home. At times I could
hardly move in bed; at other times I could not turn
my head nor move my arms. It was a great
trouble for me to lace my boots; bending at all
gave me a great pain. 1 was rubbed with turpen-
tine and various liniments, but I only got ease for
a time. Every rubbing made the parts so tender
that I could not bear anyone to come near me.
The least movement and I suffered fearfully. I
could only walk very slowly, and very often I was
so stiff that it was all I could do to drag myself
along. I never knew when an attack was going
to take me. Perhaps I’d go to bed all right and
wake up in the morning practically unable to
move. I’d be like this for days before I’d get a
little ease. I might get a sudden bout when work-
ing and have to come home and take to my bed.
My mates can tell you how I couldn’t get my coat
on with the pain ; I had to get one of them to just
throw it across my shoulders. Often the pain was
so fierce that it took me all my time to keep from
singing out with it I went off my food com-
pletely and lost weight fast. I went to a doctor
and took his medicines, but I can’t say that I got
much better. A friend of mine in New Zealand
told me to give Ur. Williams’ Pink Pills a trial.
I did. and it was marvellous the way they cured
me, and they cured me for good, too, for I have
never had a return of the rheumatism since. My
health in general is excellent.”
A Warning.
It’s important to note that Mr. Peacock was
cured by the genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
not by something that the shopman says is
“ just as good.” We’ve never heard of a “ just as
g»od ” imitation curing anyone, so insist on get-
ting Dr. Williams’. They are sold by most
chemists and storekeepers, or if not locally obtain-
able will be sent direct by the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co. of Australasia, Ltd., Sydney, for
2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d.
