POLITICAL
POINTS
Two Queensland Ministers, anyhow—
Peter Airey and George Kerr—have fired their
portfolios back at State Premier Kidston, and
refused to touch any Philp coalition - with a long
pole. And there is still hope that some more
may follow their good example shortly.
With the minting of its own silver and
copper coins, Australia will come into some pro-
fits which it should have enjov’ed long ago.
According to official estimates, 'the profit on
£1,000,000 worth of minted silver, after replacing
worn coins, is about £440,000. The profits on
£lOOO worth of copper coin is, roughly, £5OO, with
copper at £6O per
ton. The total
currency will re-
turn about £36,000
per year.
THE YASSURER A CAPITAL.
Colonel Ryrie (who represents the district in the N.S.W.
Assembly and lives in it) savs that the land between Can-
berra and the coast is so poor that “it would not materi-
ally matter how much of the country we give to the Com-
monwealth.”
“You can have it, you know. It's no earthly
good to us, but it's good enough for Australia."
Up in Queensland, where the Tory and
black-labor crowd says the white man can’t work
owing to the excessive heat, the cane crop lias
been badly damaged by frost. There seems to be
a discrepancy somewhere.
Melbourne Age is in a painful position
with regard to Victorian politics. The paper is
done with Bent, and is writing him down daily, but
apparently it has no one to put in his place, and
certainly it has no party to fill the Ministerial
benches. Prendergast is there, of course ; but the
Age hasn’t yet brought itself to take on a Labor
party with Prendergast as Premier. At the two
recent by-elections it therefore couldn’t barrack
for anybody, because the candidates that weren’t
branded Bent were branded Labor. What the
Age is going to do about it isn’t clear ; it is under-
stood to be praying hard for somebody to turn up
as a leader.
Sickness causes a loss of both time and
money. You lose the time and have the expense
of medical attendance, entailing’ a double loss.
This can be avoided by using some reliable remedy
at the first stage of the sickness. The purchase of
a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic, Cholera and
Diarrhoea Remedy often proves a profitable invest-
ment, for, by its use at the first appearance, any
unusual looseness of the bowels, a severe attack of
diarrhoea or dysentery may be averted, that might
otherwise compel a week’s cessation from labor.
Every household should have a bottle at hand. It
never fails and is pleasant to take. Get it to-day.
It may save a life.*
The velocity of the Victorian Lands
Department calls for a special speed
gauge. Lately—which, in the Depart-
ment’s system of reckoning, was two
years ago—a person residing at Moe ap-
plied for 1200 acres of land, paying at
the time £2O for survey fees. Subse-
quently he was informed that the neces-
sary papers would be ready in a month or
six weeks. He hasn’t heard anything
further since. Indeed the matter has
been so long receiving attention that he
has been wondering whether anything has
happened to the attention. Probably it
has. The last this paper heard of it was
in connection with an application from
Noah for a lease at Ararat. And then it
wasn’t any too fresh.
When Australian Governments
which harbor that expensive item of
curious haberdashery known as the Black
Rod found the public gorge rising at the
continued appointment of an Usher to
look after it, they took to disguising
the objectionable office by tacking on to it
other offices of some little real use. N.S. W.
was the first to play this game, and Black
Rod still lives and draws a spanking
salary there. Now the Australian Govern-
ment gazettes G. H. Monahan as Clerk of
Select Committees, Usher of the Black Rod, and
Secretary of the Joint House Committee, at a
salary of £475, thus sandwiching the condemned
ham between an upper and lower layer of apparent
bread.
The Parliamentary Committee that is in-
quiring into the question of Victorian Govern-
ment importations struck a suggestive fact the
other day. Premier Bent, when challenged as to
the excessive railway importations, had laid before
the House a return setting forth that the total
railway importations for 1906-7 amounted to
£8969. The Customs Department, however, in-
formed the investigating committee that the rail-
way importations for that year amounted to
£64,644 worth of dutiable goods, and £66,793 of free
goods, or a total of £131,437. As the committee
couldn’t for the life of it make £8969 and £131,437
tally, it pondered hard. Then it recollected that
Adolphus was Minister of Railways, and—well,
Victoria has begun to know its Adolphus.
Considering the futile way Britain sits
down and looks at its unemployed, the Commons
need not have been so uppish with the White-
chapel Hebrew who cast a bunch of papers from
the strangers’ gallery, and invited the members to
read his scheme for dealing with the problem.
The cable says that the Jew was “ejected and
admonished ” —presumably whilst his papers were
being removed with the smoking-room tongs
wielded by the Usher of the Murky Pole. In the
present chaotic condition of British legislation,
the Commons is not entitled to reject any sug-
gestion without exhaustive inquiry, and this reck-
less casting of Solomon out of the Temple may yet
prove a most expensive indignity.
Another of Adolphus Bent’s little land-
transactions has got something the matter with it,
and isn’t thriving. A while back he
bought the Maribyrnong Estate, osten-
sibly for closer settlement. But the
Closer Settlement Board declined it en-
thusiastically at the price Bent had
paid for it. Then Bent said he would
cut it up for workmen’s homes, but
that was as far as that project got. At
present the land is temporarily used for
grazing, but when the Bravbrook Coun-
cil asked the occupier for his rates, he
asserted that he was only a “ permissive
occupant” and knew nothing about
rates, and anyhow, as it was State
land, it wasn’t rateable. Now the
Council is worrying the Closer Settle-
ment Board, and is asking Bent what
he is going to do about it. And all
Bent can say is “ Dunno.” The number of things
he “dunno” now almost equals the number of
occupations he said he followed when he was
young.
Premier Bent’s new Yic. Land Valuation
Bill, in place of the one he recently withdrew
because the Country party barked, is
such a trifling- circumstance as to be
hardly worth noticing.' It merely
orders the municipalities to make a
valuation which shall set forth (a) the
nature of all improvements; (b) tiie
unimproved value of the land ; (c) the
improved value; and ( d ) such other
particulars as may be required. The
Minister of Public Works is to pay
half the cost, and if he doesn’t ap-
prove of the valuation, he may order a
fresh one ; or, if he fails to get satis-
faction then, the Governor in Council
may appoint valuers to do the job
honestly. Notwithstanding the new
valuations, the endowments to munici-
palities are not to be interfered with ;
and the most valuable feature of the
earlier Bill—the power given to the
Valuer-General to resume estates at
the owners’ valuations—is dropped
overboard altogether. But as that is
almost certain to be what will happen
to the whole Bill, the discussion is of
little consequence. In fact, nothing
that Bent does nowadays matters
much. His political working days are
done.
Herbert Parsons, about the ablest
of the younger members of the S. A.
Bar, who accompanied Premier Price
on his recent tour through England,
will probably run for the late “Vabe” Solo-
mon’s Northern Territory seat in the State
Assembly. His father was a former Govern-
ment Resident there, and also represented the
place in the Assembly. Parsons has money
through his relationship with millionaire Angas,
and he is a son-in-law of Advertiser Bonython ;
also he is a law-partner of Glynn, M.H.R., and
served his articles to Kingston.
STATE FRIGHTS LOGIC.
Dr. Cullen, in opening- the case, said it was laid down
in the Constitution Act that the Treasurer of the Com-
monwealth shall ascertain each month the balance of the
revenue over the expenditure, and shall pay that balance
to the States as surplus revenues.
Mr. Justice Isaacs said in this case Parliament had
voted £250,000 for the defence of the country, to be ex-
pended in not a remote future. It had not yet decided
on the form of the expenditure, but said it wanted the
money, and yet Dr. Cullen said that money was surplus
money.
Dr. Cullen: It is, until the money is expended.
—High Court, N.S. W.v. Commonwealth.
CHORNIC CADGER: “I tell yer, I claim every
cent in your pocket!”
BILL: “ Don’t be foolish, old man. It's mine,
and has to carry me through the coming year."
CHRONIC CADGER: “It's, yours? Well, I like
that! When you've got money it's mine, an’
it ain’t yours unless you've spent it!”
David Gaunson, the blusterous solicitor
who itries to cover his hopeless Toryism with a
glaze of Democratic sentiment, couldn’t even save
his deposit at the Carlton (Vic.) by-election. He
was there, seemingly, to split the Labor vote so
that the Bent candidate might get in, but the
Labor vote didn’t split worth a cent. 8,. H. Solly
won easily; the Bent.ite (who got 927 votes)
tagged along some distance behind; and David
Gaunson, with a miserable 82 supporters, was hull
down on the horizon, and his angry countenance
couldn’t even be descried with a telescope.
“ K.” writes ;
Labor-organiser Scullin, who denied (B. 8/10/’08) having
said that “the dairyman with a large family is a curse,”
has suffered simply from over-condensation. I happened
to be at the meeting where the alleged statement was
said to have been made. What Scullin stated was that
one of the worst evils of tenant-farming (which he
denounced) was the fact that it consisted largely in the
sweating of children. The farmer with the largest family
was able to offer the highest rent, so that that farmer was
(instead of the blessing he ought to be) really a curse. At
the same meeting Scullin hurled his righteous anathema
at those farmers (and his meeting was in the heart of the
leading dairying district of the Commonwealth) who
sent their young children into the milking-yard,
and so spoiled them physically and mentally.
Scullin courageously added that, though saying
these things might lose his party a few votes,
he intended to say them all over the Victorian Western
district. Of course, he was perfectly right. All over the
district named there are large families, either as tenants,
or on the share system, or simply on contract, which are
being sweated unmercifully by the rich landlords. And
it is an evil that is steadily growing, for though the
families are increasing in number, there is no increase in
the, area of cow-country. It is perfectly obvious that a
man with eight or ten strong sons and daughters can,
when the sons and daughters are willing to work simply
for their food and clothes, pay a lot higher rent than
the man who has to hire his labor. God knows, country
labor is cheap enough, but it is never so cheap as the son's
and daughters (especially the daughters) of the tenant-
farmer. So The Bulletin is hereby requested to restore
Scullin’s curse, and to print it in capital letters.
THE WOULD-BE POT BOILER.
AUSTRALIA: “ Say, Wade, old man, it's no good
you blowing up that fire. It'll never blaze
up enough to make the pot boil.”
“I’ll follow the Bent Government to the
bitter end,” said David Gaunson. in support of
his candidature at Carlton (Vic.) the other night.
“ Take the first mourning coach, Davy,” replied a
sepulchral voice from the chairs.
Ihe good dictum of Australian High
Court Justice Barton, when giving judgment in
the Surplus Revenue cases, the other day :
The Commonwealth is not the mere agent of the States
to handle part of their revenue, and take a dole for its
work ; that is not any part of the purpose of a national
Government, especially one to which the Customs and
Excise have been assigned, with exclusive control as to
imposition, collection and management. As well might
we foster the assumption that exists in some quarters,
that the Commonwealth is an alien body, an invader in
Australia without rightful foothold on her soil, lacking
citizens to enjoy the national protection and defend the
national rights.
October 21 was busy day at Moree
(N.S.W.) lands office. Eight settlement leases
are available in the district; and no less than
1200 applications were lodged and £12,500 fees
deposited before the office closed. The winning
marbles will be drawn as soon as the details can be
arranged. Meanwhile Wade’s Bill to put down
sweeps is getting along nicely, and the daily
papers are still satisfied that the demand for land
is being quite well supplied without resorting to a
graduated land-tax.
P.M.-G. Mauger has been quietly carrying
out reforms in the Post Office, while the Postal
Commission was busily trying to recommend
them, wherefore that body is aggrieved. In cor-
respondence with the Prime Minister, it is said to
be complaining that Mr. Mauger deals with
evils which ought to be left untouched until it has
delivered its formal curse upon them. Obviously
its fear is that Mauger will anticipate it to such
an extent that its beautifully-drawn-up report,
in about 4708 folios and a morass of language,
will miss the ’bus or the four-wheeler or the
Cobb’s vehicle, or something.
MAORILAND ELECTIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Whip up the votes ! The battle day is here,
The General Election draweth near,
The air is shrill with candidates and beer.
J. Ward is curled and oiled to meet his fate ;
Large of moustache, and of a sprightly gait,
He putteth on his blandest smile of state.
The Massey pipeth in the wilderness
Of settlers’ wrongs, loud howling for redress,
And Tory watchwords of deep cussedness.
The leagues of Anti-Bung are on the trail,
The hosts of stricken Beer make doleful wail,
The air of battle has grown very stale.
Wild hair and whiskers waggle on the stump,
But no one really seems to care a dump
Who gets the job to mend the parish pump.
Here is no issue worth a tinker’s curse,
Save who can best protect the public purse.
Ward’s pretty bad ; the others might be worse.
A mighty lot of things need badly doing,
A mighty lot of nasty things are brewing ;
But who’s got time while borrowed cheques we’re blewing ?
Here ends the lay ; and will you please observe,
If politicians have less sense than nerve,
We’re getting just about what we deserve.
MAORILANJD.
D.M.W.
THE WEAKEST LINKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
il.liout much searching, it is easy to recognise in the
eltort.s to belittle State Governorships and urge their
abolition a set determination to promote the detachment
ot Australia from the Empire.—Argus.
You’ll kindly note the lesson clear :
The links that keep the Empire whole,
And cause Australia to adhere
To Mother England, are the queer
Old gents who come here, spic and span,
To posture at so much per an.
They may he dull—they often are ;
They may he weak —it happens so ;
But if we banish them ’twill mar
The mightiest Empire known, and jar
It all to bits, the Argus thinks
(Lor’ save it if these he the links !).
The proposition’s fairly plain.
I know it true. You’ll please observe :
The strength of any rope or chain,
As gifted experts will explain,
The strength is of its weakest strands—
And so the Argus understands.
VIC.

WORMS IN CHILDREN.
A Veky Prevalent Complaint.
In very young children much of the irritation
and fretfulness is often due to the presence of
worms. Restlessness, fitfulness, sudden starts and
wetting the bed during sleep, hardness and ful-
ness of the belly, irregular movements of the
bowels, excessive secretions of saliva from the
mouth, are invariably indications of the irritation
caused by worms.
In older children the same symptoms are pres-
ent, but more distinctly defined. Grinding of the
teeth, violent movements of the legs and arms,
and undue flow of saliva, which causes frequent
efforts to swallow. The bowels are irregular.
The breath is usually foul, and the child is languid
and peevish ; the tongue preternaturally red or
alternately red, and covered with a white slimy
mucus. A short, dry cough ; emaciation, slow
fever, irregular pulse and sometimes convulsions.
Loss of appetite frequently alternates with a crav-
ing for food. Indeed, the latter symptom is most
pronounced in many cases, and many mothers
have experienced the insatiable appetites of their
children without realising the cause.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
contain specific properties which destroy the
worms but cause no injury to the stomach or
bowels, and do not weaken the system. They are
prepared in the form of a confection or lolly, hav-
ing no disagreeable taste or smell, but, on the con-
trary, inspire children to take them as they would
an ordinary lozenge. Obtainable at all chemists
and stores. Price Is. 6d. (6 boxes Bs. 6d.), or
direct from the W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd., 58
Pitt Street, Sydney, on receipt of remittance.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The last race of the last day at the recent Caul-
field meeting- has been pointed to as proof of the neces-
sity for an amendment of the starting-gate system. Here
was a case in which the field should have been recalled
for another start, as in the days when the flag was in
vogue. Some of the horses were practically out of the
hunt the instant the others got going, but because the
gate had been raised at the starter’s command, and the
gate is officially supposed to be fair beyond question, the
victims of this ragged dispatch couldn’t hope for the ad-
vantaged horses to be called back. Some of the starts
nowadays are worse than the worst that ever happened
when the man with the flag bore all the responsibility for
his mistakes. The fact that the best and the medium
ones are better than they were of yore is no satisfaction
to the owners and backers of horses which somehow get
left when the machine rises. These cases of palpable in-
justice couldn’t occur if a steward with a big bell were
stationed on a perch 20yds. above the starting post, with
instructions to ring a very ragged field back to line. The
quick beginners will always sprint away in front, but the
others should get going on fairly level terms.
The Victoria Derby, which has been the
public trial of several Melbourne Cup winners and placed
horses, is this year regarded as a match between Parsee
and Alawa. There are others—notably Monobel, Tiercel,
Baw Bee, Lord Nolan, and Mother Goose—that might run
much better than is expected in the Derby and start at
a short price for the big handicap next Tuesday. Thus
far Parsee looks a very good thing for the Blue Ribbon,
for he beat Monobel and others easily enough in the
A.J.O. Derby, and cut down Alawa in the Caulfield
Guineas. The latter proved himself a smart three-
year-old by subsequently winning the Eclipse
Stakes (11 furlongs), which performance suggests
that the thick-set son of Malster is a stayer,
likely to make a bold bid for the Cup. But
just as much, or more, may he said for Parsee on the
strength of his brilliant victory at Randwick, nor is there
any visible reason why Baw Bee, Monobel and Mother
Goose should not also be equal to a two-miles journey.
As to whether the best of the three-year-olds can beat
the best of the older Cup horses on w.f.a. terms or there-
abouts, there has been very little comparative “ form ” to
swear by. The position is this : The merits of Parsee and
Alawa have not been fairly tested by the older horses
that are now backed at short prices for the Cup. On the
other hand, Monobel, Baw Bee, Mother Goose and
co. have been made to look small potatoes by
Mooltan, Peru and Maranui. Therefore, if Parsee
and Alawa aforesaid run first and second for the
Derby next Saturday, the other three-year-olds will look
smaller potatoes than ever, whereas if one or more of
them perform brilliantly in a fast-run Derby— i.e. , beat
Alawa and stretch the neck of Parsee —the performance
will indicate improved “ form ” and a chance in the Mel-
bourne Cup. The writer takes Baw Bee and Monobel to
be of the improving- kind, and reckons that either of
them is worth backing at very long odds for the Double
event —Derby and Cup. All the same, the Derby tip is
Parsee 1, Baw Bee 2, Monobel 3, Alawa 4. The colt that
won the classic event at Randwick should do likewise at
Flemington, but this is not to say he is worth backing at
short odds for the Double. The chances are against any
three-year-old winning the Cup this year.
How often do the youngsters have the better of
the veterans in the two-mile handicap ? Last year Moun-
tain King, the Derby winner, finished third for the Cup.
In the previous year, to be sure, Poseidon and Antonious
ran first and second for both events. But we have to go
back to 1904 to find a Derby horse (Blinker) placed in the
Cup, and back to 1902 for the ease of Abundance, who
won the Derby and was third in the Cup. In 1900 the
Derby winner, Malster, ran second for the Cup, beaten by
another three-year-old, Clean Sweep. In 1899 the
double was won by Merriwee ; in ’97 Auruna was
placed in both events; in ’96 Newhaven won the
double; in ’95 Auraria won the Cup after being
placed third in the Derby, and in ’93 Carnage ran second
for the Cup after winning the blue ribbon. It seems,
then, that one of the Derby horses gets placed for the
Cup, as a rule. That one is usually the winner of the
classic piece of haberdasher}'. Sometimes two of the
Derby horses get placed again on the following Tuesday,
but these times are rare. And sometimes the best
juveniles are not good enough to get a look in at the
finish of the Cup.
The writer presumes that Mooltan, all going
well with him meanwhile, will win the Melbourne Stakes
next Saturday, and finish first or second for the Cup. The
others to be considered are Peru, Tulkeroo, Destinist,
Dyed Garments, Post Town, Welcome Trist, Maranui, Sir
Aymer, Delaware, and the two that run foremost in the
Derby. Dyed Garments is bound to run respectably in
any two-mile race, but he has never before been handi-
capped as high as Bst. 91b. in first-class company, nor has
he ever been as elderly as now. These things may tend
to make him Dead Garments. The Sydney Cup winner
may be considered somewhat out of it this time. He is
overweighted, like Destinist. Sir Aymer is fancied, firstly
because he represents Earnshaw’s stable, and secondly
because he split Mooltan and Maranui in a vv.f.a. race
over 12 furlongs. The “ form ” is impressive only on
paper, for Mooltan won as he liked, and some of the
others were not galloping for their lives. Sir Aymer ran
badly in the Caulfield Stakes, his staying power is
doubtful, and it is only his light weight that
recommends him. Should he make a bold bid in
the Melbourne Stakes next Saturday he will be
a strong fancy for the Cup, but in the 'meantime there
are a number of “ ifs ” in his case. Post Town and
Delaware are possibilities rather than probabilities.
Welcome Trist has run one good race this season, when
he won the Coongy Handicap from Wooroona, who
afterwards won the Moonee Valley Cup. Were the Trist
an approved stayer, the writer would fancy him a bit,
but his stamina is very doubtful, and Maranui should
beat him at the weights. Between Maranui, Peru and Tul-
keroo there is positively nothing to choose, assuming
that the Maorilander can go two miles. The other two
are unquestionably equal to the distance, for they fin-
ished first and second for last Australian Cup, with
only a length between them. The four-year-old Peru,
having improved considerably meanwhile on the w.f.a.
scale, has to allow the six-year-old Tulkeroo 91b.
for the beating he gave him last March. Lately
we have seen Peru run a moderate second to Mooltan in
the Metropolitan, and Tulkeroo run a closer second to
Maranui in the Caulfield Cup race. Each of them mcits
his recent conqueror on improved terms in the Melbourne
Cup. Youpay your money and you take your pick.
Seeing that Peru happened to meet and beat
Maranui in the Metropolitan on worse terms than he will
meet him on next Tuesday, the writer prefers Peru to
Tulkeroo, who was beaten by Maranui at Caulfield. And
he prefers Mooltan to Peru, notwithstanding the
burden of 9st. 121 b., which will lie heavy on the con-
science of that consistent performer when he comes to
the last furlong of the Cup journey. Perhaps he is not
“ a glutton for a distance,” as the hard-faced gentry would
put it. For that matter, Carbine w T as not a glutton, in
the full sense of the term. Mooltan, on paper, comes
nearer to the all-round excellence of Carbine than Poseidon
did. Last year, with a light weight up, he ran Melodrama
to a head for the Epsom mile in lmin. 38$sec. Then, with
a lighter weight, he beat Apologue half a head for the
Metropolitan—Apologue giving him 221 b. In the Mel-
bourne Cup, a month later, it was Mooltan who gave
Apologue weight—2lb.—and ran him pretty close. The
extra weight and extra distance made little or no differ-
ence to him last year. His performances at Eandwick
this month were brilliant, his heavy weight for the Cup
will be only 101 b. more than he carried when he
beat Peru and Maranui (who will also have to
put up some extra pounds), and all the time
he is said to be improving with every gallop.
Among the horses from four years old and upwards,
this honest son of Positano and Dilisk stands in a class by
himself. He looks equal to giving all the required
poundage to Peru and Maranui. He is seriously
threatened by nothing save the possibility that Sir
Aymer is a good stayer, in which cage a difference of 2J
stone in his favor should enable the English horse to beat
the top weight. There is, of course, the other possibility
of a clinking three-year-old .or two coming forth to
battle. But The Bulletin, going to press a week before
the Cup is run, is all in the dark re the question of clink
or no clink in the Derby field, therefore this prediction
reserves the right of changing the name of Parsee for
something else, if something else happens to win the
Derby. Here followeth the prediction
1. Mooltan.
2. Peru.
3. Parsee.
4. Maranui,
with Baw Bee as the best of the 50 to one chances.
If the youngsters that ran behind Zilka in the
Breeders’ Plate at Randwiek were a fairly good lot, the
Maribyrnong Plate also should go to that speedy filly.
* * * *
William Day, probably the oldest of English
turf identities, died last month. The son of a horse-
trainer, he started life as a jockey, riding the winner of
the Ascot Gold Cup some 70 years ago. As a trainer he
must have had more than 40 years’active experience, and
in the “plunging” times, when his father and brother
trained a long string of gees at Danebury, wily William
carried on a friendly rivalry with them at Woodgates. It
sometimes happened that the two stables provided all the
entrants for a race at a small, tony meeting in their
neighborhood. Whilst the guileless public made a market
on the event by backing all the nags “ right out ” with
the “list” bookmakers in London (who at that time
plied their trade openly in Farringdon-street), the Days
W’ould choose a winner out of, say, two intended
starters, and having backed the dead sure thing
through their commissioner, would scratch all the others.
But these family arrangements were only minor considera-
tions in William Day’s business. He had a special talent for
bringing off big handicap coups with “dark” horses.
Mystery was a passion with him. Still he managed after
a time to lose ground in the training business, despite his
cleverness. For some years he did nothing of conse-
quence, but in 1881 he scored a great double event, the
Caesarewitch and Cambridgeshire, with Foxhall, for the
American ovyner, J. R. Keene; and the trainer won
enough money to confirm him in his resolve to retire
from business. William Day at that time was a very old-
looking little man of 59, but he lived to be 85. Soon
after his retirement he published his first book—“ The
Racehorse in Training,” and in 1886 he brought out his
“ Reminiscences of the Turf,” a book of anecdote and
gossip which promises to live as a classic in sporting
literature, and whereof another edition will probably be
published on the strength of the old man’s death.
With idyllic weather and firm wickets, Mel-
bourne Pennant cricket furnished some almost interest-
ing displays on Saturday last. Melbourne’s struggle with
Northcote was less than heroic, the former winning
easily by an innings and 39. Although the wicket was a
piece of perfection, Armstrong and Hazlitt, bowling un-
changed throughout the afternoon, took all the ginger
out of Northcote’s defence, that team’s total compilation
being only 96. P’ran, which defeated the almost invincible
East on the first innings, might have very easily made it a
straight-out win. East, which closed i ts second in nings with
five for 143, left P’ran with 110 to win, but, mainly owing
to the languid batting of Healy, the P’ran total stopped
at 6 p.m. with 103 for five wickets—eight short of victorjL
The end of the discussion between South and Richmond
supplied frantic excitement for an audience of about four
men and a dog. South, requiring 214 to win, looked as
if it had victory assured with three wickets to
spare, but Richmond bowler Kelly confiscated the
last three sticks practically for nothing, and
Skinner’s suburb lost by three runs, Carlton’s
victory over Fitzroy was notable for the first-
named’s unpromising commencement. Requiring 179 to
win, it at one stage of the game stood at only 36 for five
wickets, but some fine, vigorous batting by Warne (65),
Delves (31) and Fontaine (44) pulled the match out of the
conflagration. Carlton won by nine runs on the first
innings. University’s eclipse of Essendon by an innings
and 28 was scarcely in the nature of a surprise. The
students, who had one down for 95 on the previous Satur-
day, increased their score to 219 for four wickets,
declared their innings closed, and dismissed the ’Dons on
their second attempt for 116. As for the duel between
St. Kilda and North Melbourne, it was settled in favor of
the latter by a margin of two wickets and 16 runs on the
first innings.
Either there are some amazing batsmen
operating- in Sydney second-grade cricket, or the bowling
in the lower division is unusually defective. Consider
some of the scores on Saturday : Chapman, of Mosman,
101 ; Carroll and Langley, of Newtown, 104 and 135, both
not out ; Beveridge, of Randwick, 158 ; Smith, of Glebe,
200 ; Janes, of Balmain, 106. Six century-makers on the
job on one afternoon.
* * * *
The first-graders, the same afternoon, failed to
produce one century among them, although one of them
got aggravatingly close when A. Loudon, of Redfern,
smacked up 97 before Sullivan got him out l.b.w. After
Loudon the next best was the 87 not out of Pite, of
Sydney, and 78 by Richardson, of the same club. Satur-
day was the second consecutive occasion when Pite, the
chronic not-out, carried his bat. The previous Saturday
he shouldered his timber for 118 against Middle Harbor.
Evidently this is the season of the colts.
* * * *
S.A. and Victoria have been playing a big
“ correspondence ” chess match, which is a weary affair
stretching out over long months ; indeed, the closure had
to be applied at last, and fully a quarter of the games
arbitrated on. The chief interest of the match was its
great size—32 a side—and the fact that S.A. won by 36£
to 27£. McArthur, for S.A., won both games from Grant
at the top board, Belcher tying with Gundersen at the
second.
* * * *
“ Smell ”.- I rise to say a word on behalf of a
downtrodden and much-abused cult. I refer to the paid
motor-driver. Is it our fault that silly old ladies crossing
the road will sprint wildly back when within two feet of
the pavement and get into the centre of the road right
in front of the car as soon as they hear the horn tooting?
Can it be honestly said that the driver is to blame when
young ladies stop in the middle of the street until they
see on which side he is going to pass them, and then
trustingly run in the same direction. Yet for these
things we are blamed. And how we are reviled ! I, my-
self, the most careful of drivers, have been hailed as
“ road hog,” “ body snatcher,” and when I was addressed
as “ dog-squash ” I thought I had struck the bed-rock of
opprobrium. But I hadn’t, for in Brisbane, when the near
presence of the Law compelled me to crawl along at six
miles an hour, a dirty-faced, misbegotten brat, who ought
to have been at school, in gaol, or out of the world, drove
a dilapidated goat, attached to a box on wheels,
alongside, and addressed me. “ Hulloa, travellin’
stinker!” he yelled, coarsely; “I’ll race yer to the
Valley fora tray.” Really,. I am surprised so few children
get hurt—l mean murdered.
* * * *
Owing to a smash-up at Bathurst (N.S.W.)
sports, the crack wheeler, George Horder, also F. Hagney
and Maorilander J. Summers are on the shelf for repairs.
Horder has accumulated much gravel rash, Hagney has a
broken collarbone, and Summers carries three fractured
ribs. The crack cyclists are a dwindling band.
* « * *
The Melbourne Saucer Track crowd has opened
negotiations for the importation of European and Yankee
cyclists with a view to working up another boom.
* * * *
At Hawthorn (Vic.), last Friday, ex-cyclist C.
St. Vincent, who is apparently possessed of more leisure
than “ savvy,” attacked the world’s skipping record for
1000 skips, and having got the beast by the wool or the
ear or something, knocked 4secs. off it—smin. 13secs.
The previous best, of smin. 17secs., was held by F. Con-
nor, of America, which address this paper admits is
vague but is sufficient for the importance of the subject.
Now that Australia holds the records for club-swinging,
piano-punching and skipping, it is about up to some
weak-minded but strong-limbed person to assault those
for thumb-twiddling', standing on one leg, and so forth.
And when the records for these strenuous exercises have
been caught, some still more deliberate individual can
have a shot at the record for leaning against a post.
* * * *
Things will get exciting when the road hog of
this generation takes to air-motoring. It was cabled, the
other day, that the occupants of the collapsing balloon,
when the Gordon Bennett race started in Berlin, “cut
away 32 bags of ballast from a height of 4000 ft.” “ The
incident,” it was added, “shows how safe ballooning
really is.” Safe? Rather ! A mere bag of ballast,
dropped from a paltry 4000 ft., wouldn’t hurt anybody—
so long as anybody didn’t happen to be underneath it.”
* * * «
For the first time since the season opened
Sydney sailing men, on Saturday, got the breeze they
love. It blew delightfully from the N.E. and the
amphibians revelled in it. It was the first yacht race of
the season and the craft flew the Sydney Yacht Squadron’s
burgee. The Thelma, a yacht designed by Sydney naval
architect iWalter Reeks far back in 1889, suddenly re-
appeared under control of G. Lloyd-Jones, and downed
all the later comers, including the fast-running Awanui
and the cloud-raking Rawhiti. The Thelma started on
six minutes’ handicap, Rawhiti on three, and Awanui
was, of course, on scratch. Awanui, rushing through the
water, finished first, leading Rawhiti by 3min. 22sec.,
which was ahead of Thelma by lmin. 41sec. But the
adjustment of the handicap made Thelma winner by 57sec.
In the open-boat section, things were exciting,
and Sydney Flying Squadron’s two heats and a final were
full of incident. Ben-my-cree went under at Shark
Island, andMaritana turned over at Point Piper. In the
first heat, Arthur was streaking along in great style when
a sudden earry-away brought it to a standstill, and Scot
rushed to the front and stayed there till the finish, with
Eileen and Oweenee in pursuit. In the second heat,
Pheasant got to the front from the jump, and continued,
with Donnelly and Evelyn behind at the winning line.
The final provided a great finish. Pheasant, which was
leading, crowded on extras to get away from Scot, which
was coming up very fast. But the effort was futile. Scot
again scooted to the van, and relegated Pheasant to
second place. Evelyn was third. Six other clubs held
races that were more or less exciting.
* * * *
Apparently swimmer Beaurepaire knows how
to look after himself, even if it entails being unpleasant.
Which is a good thing. It seems that the Glasgow gentle-
man who was looking after Bogey’s Scottish engagements
had arranged to match him with Taylor, the English and
Olympic champion paddler, over 440yds. at the annual
gala of St. Bernard’s Swimming Club, Edinburgh. But
when Bogey arrived at the city of the Tolbooth he
declined to enter the water with Taylor. He offered to
streak over the distance against Taylor’s time, but swim
with Taylor he would not. Beaurepaire’s reason was
that, in a previous event,'; a half-mile struggle which he
won and in which Taylor was runner-up, the latter so
persistently bumped him that Beaurepaire vowed he
would never compete with him again, and, very properly,
he kept his word. If more athletes would take the same
stand it would do a lot to check the spread of Yankee
sporting tactics, which are becoming more or less general.
* * * *
S.A. Football League has an annual medal for
the most brilliant and fairest player (the daily papers
added “and most gentlemanly,” but this is officially
denied). What would happen if the fairest player was
not brilliant, and the most brilliant player wasn’t fair,
seems doubtful. And then neither of them might be'
gentlemanly; they might both drink out of the finger
bowl. Anyway the medal has just been awarded—on the
vote of the umpires, who should be good judges—to
Tierney, who admittedly has all the good qualities, and
whose bald head has dominated the ruck of West
Adelaide (and incidentally of all the teams that “ West ”
has bumped up against).
The defeat of Joe Grim, iof Philadelphia, at
Sydney’ Gaiety last week by Arthur Cripps, the Australian
middleweight champion, confirmed the suspicion that the
“ Iron Man from Philadelphia ” is a pugilistic ignoramus.
His ignorance of fistic science is almost as.great as his
capacity to endure punishment, and that, is bey’ond all
precedent. If he only knew anything about even the first
principles of his trade he would be a world-smasher, con-
tinually’ confronted with the problem of where to bury
his dead. He swings blows that would make an ox totter,
but as they never by any chance get home, no one is any
the worse. One of them, delivered with judgment and
precision, would not only have ended the fight with Cripps
instanter, but haye sent his head whizzing'among: the audi-
ence. The defeat is not likely to injure Grim professionally.
Win or lose, he will be a profitable draw, for as a mounte-
bank and a clown he can give even George Reid many laps
start and beat him easily. He is a joke and a farce, but a
dangerous one. He rushes to the attack like a Free-
trade manufacturer rushing a Protective tariff, and if
only one of the cyclone of blows he delivers reaches a
vital spot, it is going to be a long good-night to the
other fellow. Curiously enough, if the other fellow
can anticipate the attack, Grim becomes moment-
arily helpless, and a straight left on the nose com-
pletely breaks up any idea of “ going mad.”
In the fight both men started cautiously enough.
Even Grim tried to be serious. Was he not up against a
champion? He received smashes and drives on the
mouth, the point, the neck, and bowed with courtly
foreign grace in acknowledgment; and then suddenly
clinching, walloped Cripps behind the ear right round his
neck. He showered blows down the back, and whacked
and beat the Australian all over the hinder part, from Han
to Beersheba. Cripps in his agony clawed wildly for a
grip of that destructive right, and when he got it he held
it tight. Towards the middle of the scrap Cripps dis-
covered that his best defence was to get in the first blow,
and he landed some beauties, which knocked the Yankee-
Italian’s head right back. But Grim never heeded them
—they seemed to produce no effect. Once Cripps smote
him a right hander on the mouth, crashed a straight left
on to the chin, and then bashed him heavily on the
top of the nose with the right—three knock-out
blows in quick succession. And still Grim was 6n his
feet, apparently unhurt. In the 17th round Grim began
to whirl his arms like flails ; in the 19th he went mad;
and in the 20th he was absolutely baresark. Cripps slung
boxing to the winds and unashamedly hung on to him,
embracing the Italian round the waist while a delighted
audience howled to him to kiss the Roman. But Cripps
hung on, and waited breathlessly for the gong to sound,
and all the while Grim jerked and bashed and struggled
to get free. With a desperate wrench he got loose, and
aimed three blows that would have burst open the gate
of a city, but they didn’t come within inches of Cripps.
Then the gong sounded. The referee pointed to Cripps.
Grim vaulted lightly over the ropes down among the
audience, and vanished without giving the hoped-for
speech.
* * * *
When he arrived at Adelaide Johnson promptly
denied having “ opened his head ” on the subject of
Burns, and complained that the newspaper men were
trying to foster ill feeling between himself and his
opponent. If all the boasting and killing of Burns with
his mouth that has been attributed to Johnson is merely
newspaper invention, it is time some newspaper men
were in gaol; for the big black has been held up to scorn,
hatred, ridicule and contempt as a loud-mouthed
braggart. Writer met Johnson when he was in Australia
before, and certainly saw nothing of the boaster about
him. Quite the opposite, in fact, and when the reports
of the mouth butchery began to come through, most
people who had met Johnson wore a pained expression.
They had not expected it of him.
* ♦ * *
“J.”: A consideration of the Cripps-Grim
battle last week convinced me that those papers wrong
Grim who treat him as a pugilistic joke. Cripps is the
cleverest man at or near his weight that Australia owns.
Good judges opine that he would give T. Burns himself
something of a battle. Yet at no stage of the whirling
contest the other night was Cripps altogether at his ease.
There is danger in the Philadelphian’s very unconven-
tionality of attack. His oppone l i never knows what
sort of hit Grim is going to v"'gh in with next;
neither does Grim. If one of Grim’s wild lashes
connected unchecked with an adversary’s head it
would probably knock that portion of him into
the gallery and leave the rest to be counted out in
the ring. Cripps knew too much to undergo that experi-
ence in all its awfulness. But he endured some nasty
jars, nevertheless. Grim is extraordinarily clumsy in
all his movements, and in his battle with Cripps he was
made to appear even clumsier than he is, by contrast
with the other’s beautifully correct pugilism. But there
is method in Grim’s clumsiness ; a certain amount of it
is, not improbably, assumed. His footwork, which is by
no means bad, quite belies the weird arm-waving in which
he indulges. There is no question about Grim’s right to
the title of Iron Man. After about half-a-dozen
rounds, Cripps got quite tired of shooting wicked
body-blows into the Dago’s stomach and ribs,
(since the said blows produced no effect whatever),
and opened out on his opponent’s face and head. Grim
seemed to positively like it. After pokes in the jaw and
eye that would have acted like chloroform on a normal
human being, he smiled affectionately on Cripps and
clamored for more. For all the 20 rounds which the
contest went he skipped about, and thrashed the air and
Cripps’ head, neck, back and shoulders with flail-like
blows unceasingly, and yet when it was finished he
vaulted gaily over the ropes and cantered to his dressing-
room like a released two-year-old. Of course, he wasn’t
knocked out, or anywhere near it. No mortal fist will
achieve that feat in our time. It will require a six-
chambered revolver at close range.
* * * *
Another foreign reputation that was uncomfort-
ably buried in an Australian grave on Monday night last was
that of Patrick O’Keefe, who drew a six-round scrap with
W. Papke, the man who broke up Stanley Ketchell, who
was to have killed Burns when he returned to the U.S.A.
E. Williams acted as sexton, and had a weary and painful
time of it at Melbourne Cyclorama, where the interment
took place. A feature of the contest was the rushing of
the building by the push, which declined to pay, and
swept in like a wave, carrying doors, ticket-takers and
police before it. O’Keefe received a lot of depressing
circumstances, which lasted 15 rounds. Then the police
interfered, and the referee gave Williams the verdict on
points. Williams’ ribs and midriff were much deteriorated
in the in-fighting, and he was about the most uncomfort-
able victor who has appeared in an Australian ring, but
O’Keefe’s walloping was none the less decisive.
A ORAY-FISH FANTASY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Great Mooltan was one of my fancies,
I also respected Peru,
And was fond—very fond—of the chances
Of Trist and the stout Tulkeroo.
But last night put an end to my doubting;
Past asleep on the sofa I fell,
And was wakened by somebody shouting
Monobel! Monobel! Monobel 1
Up to then I had talked of Sir Aymer,
Dyed Garments, Post Town and Parsee,
And had backed Alawa (he is gainer
Than old Delaware or Baw Bee);
By a man who is wise in his scheming
I’d been told Maranui looks well,
But now I am all the time dreaming
Monobel 1 Alonobel 1 Monobel!
Yes, it sounds like the name of a hallad,
But it stands for the name of an ’orse.
I had supped upon crayfish and salad,
And my mind, as a matter of course,
Was concerned with the Cup, when a babel
Of voices was rent by a yell
Which appeared to come “ straight from the stable ”
Monobel ! Monobel! Monobel!
Lord knows I am not an ascetic
In touch with the spirits of grace,
And never a vision prophetic
Has guided my pound on a race ;
Yet I, who have e’er been a scoffer
At Cup dreams, am bound by a spell
To accept what the bookies may offer,
Say, thirty-to-one Monobel 1

SYDNEY ADDRESS 255 CLARENCE STREET.
Jim Currie, Queensland mail contractor,
horse-owner, bookmaker, and anything else that
happened along, has gone to his last settling.
Few men were better known in Western Queens-
land in the days of big cheques. He used to run
the mail between Longreach and Winton, but he
went for higher game afterwards. He owned the
horses Arthur, Delaware, Sailor Boy, Frolic, and
Residue. In his younger days he was a ped who
made money out of his heels. He and Billy Clarke
used to set traps for the tugs in the West. Currie
once struck a fencer’s camp on the Bulloo, where
there was a man who talked loudly about his
sprinting abilities. Currie gave him syds. in 100
for £5O a-side and annexed the stakes. On
another occasion Joe Julian, smarting at a defeat,
followed Currie out to Welford with a runner!
Julian was £2OO poorer when the race came off.
Currie entered for the first Sheffield at Longreach,
and was surprised to hear that he was placed on
the 15yds. mark. He bought himself in the Cal-
cutta, worth £lOO, and took all the money going
at 10 to 1. It was a great win for him. But when
he went to Melbourne he found there were others
there, so he went down slump by slump, and
finally an abscess on the brain finished a fight that
for a long while had been going seriously against
him.
The Sydney University chemistry profes-
sorship, vacated by Dr. Liversidge, has been
given by the Senate to Dr. Fawsitt, a lecturer in
Glasgow University. It was expected that the
choice billet would go, if not to an Australian
graduate, at any rate to a man from one of the
Australian Universities. But Sydney ’Varsity
Senate is keen on importations. It imports the
lesser lights for lectureships, and, no matter how
hard they may work, when the professorships are
vacant it imports afresh. This policy drives away
the ambitious younger men. Thus E. Kilburn
Scott, the electrical engineering expert imported
a little while back, is said to be getting ready to
quit. The Sydney ’Varsity Senate is too much
for him.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Private Enterprise can be a cheerful bri-
gand even when it doesn’t try. A while ago a man at
Etheridge (N.Q.) wanted a truck of maize. He bought it
at Atherton, and had it sent down on the Queensland
State line to Mareeba at 4s. per ton. There it had to be
put on to the Chillagoe Co.’s line. It went to Almaden
(the section over which the rates are fixed by Govern-
ment) for £2 ; total so far for 97 miles, £2 16s. But Pri-
vate Enterprise is still building the line on from Alma-
den, and as Private Enterprise affirmed that it doesn’t
have to adhere to the fixed rate (50 per cent, over Govern-
ment rates) until the line is completed, Private Enter-
prise calmly charged £l2 for the next 83 miles—£9 4s.
more for 14 miles less railage. Fortunately the man who
wanted the maize wears a limber leg with a large-sized
boot at the end of it, and he kicked so hard that Private
Enterprise was quite surprised, and hauled down the
rate from £l2 to £B, which is still over three times more
per mile than the State charges.
The Mari on the Land has a lot of time to think.
Last year Maoriland passed a law prohibiting the import-
ation of fruit which was not accompanied by a certificate
that fruit fly did not exist within a mile of the orchard
where the fruit was grown. Some of the Parramatta
orchardists succeeded in getting on the list of “ clean ”
orchards, and were able to ship fruit to M.L. at high
prices. But the N.S.W. Government Inspectors got
suspicious when they found that one grower was
exporting twice as much fruit as the inspector had seen
hanging on his trees. Then things were probed, and it
was found that the grower had been buying cheap fruit
from “ dirty ” orchards and selling it at high prices as
his own, on the strength of his Government certificate.
Not only was he plainly guilty of obtaining money under
false pretences, but he was exposing every orchardist in
Maoriland to fruit fly, and every one in N.S.W. to the risk
of having his produce wholly shut out by that angry
State. And the only punishment was the cancelling of
his certificate. People have “ done ” a long spell in a
carefully-enclosed place for much less.
* * * *
The Australian —that useless fellow who can’t
make a clothes-peg—has been inventing again. Manager
Paddle, of the Ple.vstowe Sugar Mill, near Mackay (Q.),
has devised a sand-filter for dealing with sugar-cane
juice after it leaves the subsiders, the result of which is
said to be a reduced volume of molasses, less invert sugar,
and an improvement in vacuum pan work. The sugar
business, up to a certain point, has been shamefully abo-
riginal—the inevitable result of employing' cheap colored
labor—and the more improvements that come, the more
firmly will the industry be established as a white man’s
concern.
* • * *
The “Old Soldier ” talks about wool in China :
—J. B. Suttor, the N.S.W. Commercial Agent, has got
hold of a yarn that the Chinese are going to clothe their
troops in woollen, and are putting up a mill between
Pekin and Kalgan, whence people start to cross the Gobi
Desert. “The necessary machinery has been ordered
from England, and may be expected to arrive in about a
month’s time.” When Younghusband was in Kalgan in
the late ’eighties, on his way to Yarkand, he found there
an active white agent collecting the wool of the two-
humped Bactrian camel for treatment in a mill in the
neighborhood. Welby, who crossed Tibet from Kashmir
to Pekin, was carried down the Hoang'-Ho in one of a
great fleet of wool-laden boats so numerous as sometimes
to cause serious blocks in the great river. Welby re-
peatedly remarks in hisaccount of his journey on the num-
ber of sheep always in sight on the “ Chang,” or table-
land of Northern Tibet. The expression, “sheep always in
sight” occurs constantly in his book. My own experience
on the N.W. frontier of India, 20 years before Welby’s
time, is that there was then a very large trade done by
the Tibetans across the border in coarse blankets and
woollen cloth of their own make. Make no mistake, John
Chinaman is not so simple as not to know all about wool
and woollen clothes when it suits him to savee, whatever
the guileless Commercial Agent may say.
* * * »
The Socialist Co-operative Society of Victoria,
which runs a general store in Melbourne next door to where
Torn Mann used to clank the chains, seems to have gone
sailing in unexplored waters, and to have landed it doesn’t
quite know where. Its secretary being recently charged
with annexing 6s. 6d. and 25., the chairman of the special
committee appointed to overhaul the books explained
that the Society hadn’t launched the proceedings, but
was fully persuaded the discrepancies were merely clerical
errors. The Court was impressed, but reckoned it ought
to have a little more information. “ Well, sir,” said
the co-operator, “when we started the Society we
decided not to follow the usual lines. Consequently,
money would be handed over in the street, and
no receipts would be given. We thought we’d follow out
the Socialistic principles ; but the committee found it
impossible to trace many amounts through the books ; in
fact, it couldn’t feel absolutely sure about many small
amounts.” The P.M. agreed there was a good deal of
probability about the absence of sureness under such cir-
cumstances, and said that, as there was manifestly no
case against the secretary, he had better go and have
another try at straightening up his books. It says whole
volumes for the honesty of these Melbourne Communists
—not Socialists—that their store has been running about
two years and hasn’t “ gone bung ” yet. There are evi-
dently no importers in that society.
* * it *
South Aus. has dredged up a new industry.
Recently a Parliamentary party went to Port Broughton,
on Spencer’s Gulf, and one of the speakers at the in-
evitable banquet produced some fine samples of fibre, and
spoke hopefully of turning it into an industry of consider-
able dimensions. The fibre is got from the sea near the
Port, and takes dye well. Experts have reported that
mercerised material of great value can be manufactured
from it; and another said he was getting ready to
use it for binder twine. Several well-gilded men of Ade-
laide are pushing the business along.
Suttor, N.S.W. Commercial Agent in “the
East,” is throwing up his hat because the Straits Settle-
ments imported £45,546 worth of Australian frozen meat
in 1907, an increase of £23,000 over 1906 ; and he is cheer-
ing on Australia to do better next year. But Australia is
beginning to have its doubts, and starts wondering
whether it wouldn’t pay it better to have chops at a
ha’penny or so cheaper for its own breakfast.

THE FOOD REFORMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To abstain from meat is not sufficient. In order to
secure the perfect diet which promotes great physical and
mental endurance it is necessary to abstain from alcoholic
liquors, tea, coffee, cocoa, tobacco, meat, eggs, milk,
peas and beans. —Cranky hut pious American paper.
Now trembles Bung upon his beery throne,
Likewise the slayer of the ram and bull ;
The men of Cow in all the vales make moan,
And e’en the baker’s heavy heart is full.
The rooster mourns the sad, forsaken egg ;
The fishes sorrow for the frying-pan ;
The beans ungathered in the garden lag ;
No more are these fit sustenance for man.
I cannot work my best with these for food,
They fail alike to nourish brawn and brain ;
The only things that really do me good
Are hard-boiled lettuces and husks of grain.
These shall supply the place of beef and rum—
Behold, my dinner fields are waving green ;
These shall sustain my heart till I become
Old as Methuselah and twice as mean.
MAORILAND.
D.M.W.


The London Musical ft Courier.
London , July 18, 1908.
Australian Pianos.
ONE of the many results of the Franco-British
Exhibition is to further impress us with the
enormous agricultural and mineral resources of
Australia, and incidentally with the present attainment
of her manufacturing industries. Although the pro-
ducts of the last-named are.not graphically represented
by exhibits, with the commendable exception of pianos
by Messrs. Beale & Co. and harvesting implements of
the Meadowbank Co., they total an annual output of
=£80,000,000 and give employment to 240,000 of the
still sparse population of the Commonwealth.
Prom official publications it will be seen that there
has been an enormous increase in the quantity and
variety of manufactured articles since the federation of
the States and the imposing of a Protective tariff. It
has been the aim of the Government to foster the manu-
facture in Australia of all that her people require, both
in necessities and luxuries, and doubtless the recent
increase of duties will prove a further stimulus to the
present manufacturers, and attract others.
The practicability of Englishmen is proverbial when
circumstances call out their best, and this could hardly
find a more striking exemplification than in the achieve-
ments of Messrs. Beale & Co., who have established a
successful piano manufactory in Sydney. They have
already produced upwards of 10,000 instruments, and
sold them through their own retail organisations.
Anyone wh.o knows the manifold difficulties to be
overcome in the making of a high-class instrument, will
readily appreciate the exceptional attainment of this
pioneer firm in what seems destined to become a great
industry at the Antipodes.
The fact that Messrs. Beale & Co. made and sold to
the public 1700 pianos last year proves that they have
found a large demand. This, as a natural sequence, is
largely the result of the satisfaction engendered by the
use of the thousands of Beale pianos previously sold
throughout the Commonwealth. Under new-world con-
ditions they have in fourteen years acquired a pro-
ficiency in construction which is usually considered
possible only after the accretion of decades of experi-
ence, tradition, and practical experimental development.
Previous to their becoming manufacturers to any
great extent they had sold at retail in Australia over
12,000 pianos of nearly all makes, and had become
thoroughly conversant with the characteristic qualities
of each. As none of the manufacturers of the world
had thought it worth while to patent their individual
improvements in Australia, despite the fact that there
was a large trade from there, this firm had an unre-
stricted field in adapting whatever there was of practi-
cal value to them. It is significant, however, that the
very heart of their instruments is their own original
creation. Indeed, they have been fortunate in invent-
ing and perfecting an iron and steel tuning system, of
which the iron wrest-plank forms an integral part of the
frame, which resembles an iron harp. By this means
they secure absolute rigidity, a greater length of string,
and higher tension—results impossible of attainment
under the prevailing system of employing a separate
wooden wrest-plank. This new' method affords an
opportunity for a slightly different disposition of the
stringing in laying out the scale, and secures a full,
round, brilliant tone, enriched by perceptibly increasing
the volume. Another invention is the patent hitch-
pins, which, by an original and ingenious method, are
cast in the frame itself, and are immovably attached
thereto.
In speaking of this Beale-Vader patent, the firm
says : “ Our invention is simplicity itself. Instead of
introducing a wrest-pin from the front, driven into the
wood like a nail, we introduce a steel pin from the
back, fitted nicely into a hole drilled in a solid metal
plate, which is part of our solid metal frame. The pin
has a conical butt fitting a conical shoulder in the
drilled hole. A tension screw (or stud) presses the pin
against the shoulder, thus controlling the pin and im-
parting any desired tension. The strain of the pin is
taken up securely by the iron frame, and all the vibra-
tion of the string is conserved. This system facilitates
the work of the tuner, and the string will remain per-
manently at any desired pitch.”
It is the aim of the firm to make every detail in their
own factory, thus controlling the quality of the materials
and workmanship, and ensuring that each instrument
will sustain the high standard achieved by them. The
large demand for their pianos has fostered a spirit of
enterprise, which has enabled them to overcome what
have been almost insurmountable difficulties and taught
them the important lesson of self-reliance. Being far
removed from, and not always satisfied with, the supply
of “parts” of the piano, they found it expedient to
devise machinery for making their sconces, action stan-
dards, pressure-bars, castors, pedals and other parts.
They found that by making them they were sure of
their quality and consequent durability.
Messrs. Beale & Co. have published a pamphlet which
gives many illustrations of the processes of manufacture.
This and a splendid catalogue of their instruments,
which is printed in three colors, will be sent to
anyone interested. Several of the illustrations are
given herewith.
Those who have seen Messrs. Beale & Co’s manu-
factory—and they are many, from the Prime Minister of
Australia down to the commonef— say that it is a model
one, and that the various machines and appliances for
the processes of construction are superior in every
respect. Numerous musicians have also given like
endorsement, and two of the directors of Messrs. J.
Lyons and Co. were much impressed during their recent
visit. These gentlemen personally inspected the fac-
tories.
Every department of their large factory at Annan-
dale, Sydney, is fully equipped, and in many instances
machinery is employed that has been invented and
made by the firm in their own shops. Among these are
the facilities for casting their patent combined metal
frame and wrest-plate, and their original automatic
machines, one of which, for example, makes ornamental
thumb-screws from metal blanks cast in the foundry.
It grasps the blank, turns it, faces it, drills a hole in it,
makes the thread, and cuts it the right length.
There are thirty departments embraced in the process
of preparing the materials and manufacturing. These
include the quay and yards at Rozelle Bay, for receiving,
stacking and open-air seasoning of timber; shelter
sheds for dry timber and the factory ; modern kilns for
seasoning by heat and moisture ; iron foundries electri-
cally fitted ; brass foundry, for brass and bronze
fittings ; blacksmiths’ shop ; iron shops, for drilling,
turning, fitting, grinding, screw working, and for pro-
ducing the plant; shop for enamelling, brass finishing,
and metal polishing, veneer sawing and slicing, drying
by patent system, matching and preparing and laying
by a patent method from three to nine plies ; timber
machining shops for the manufacture of all wood parts,
joiners’ shop, scale-drawing department, cabinetmakers’
shop, French polishers, carvers, keyboards, action
making, frame making, action fitting, case fitting,
regulating, polish finishers, tuning and intoning,
packing and shipping.
The firm acknowledge the hearty co-operation of their
workmen, whom they claim are second to no other
craftsmen in the world. They have been quick to learn
and to adapt to new conditions their former experience,
and their distinct individuality is reflected, much to
their credit, in their work. Their employees number
over 300 in the factories, and their annual disburse-
ments for wages in the making and selling of the
instruments are over They have branches in
every city of the Commonwealth, and do the whole of
their selling through these. Their pianos have been
unequivocally endorsed by the most eminent men of
Australia.
AWARDS to BEALE AUSTRALIAN PIANOS at Franco-British Exhibition.

GOLD MEDAL for inventions (patent wrest=plank, patent cabinet=work, etc.), and factory organisation

SYDNEY.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Herbert Flemming, who had been broken in health for
two or three years, died last week at Menzies’ Hotel, Mel-
bourne. His age was perhaps a little more than 53, but,
anyhow, he passed out long before his time. A mighty
fine athletic-looking fellow was Herbert Flemming in his
young Australian days when he played ingenuous lovers,
lurid villains, or elderly character parts for “ the Firm.”
The writer remembers him as the “juvenile lead” —a
quite godlike figure—in “ The Parvenu,” at the original
Princess’s Theatre, Melbourne, nearly a quarter of a
century ago. His association with the Broughs and
Boucicault dated back to the first Australian production
of “ The Magistrate,” after which he appeared with
Charles Warner at the Royal and the new Princess’s.
Never a conspicuously clever actor, he was always effect-
ive, and when his personal appearance got a fair chance
the girls thought him lovely indeed. Sometime in the
early ’9o’s he went to England, and was next heard of
hereabouts as a manager in S’Africa. He had been some
14 years away from Australia when he returned—a stout,
weather-beaten Flemming—bringing Mel. B. Spurr with
him. Spurr’s entertainment was a remarkable success,
and manager Flemming made so much money by it that
he was presently able to go back to England and organise
the Brough-Flemming co. In the meantime, Spurr had
died in Australia. In “ The Walls of Jericho ” Flemming
made his first and best appearance with his co-manager,
but even then he was beginning to be a sick man. It was
an ill-fated firm. Brough died, and for a while Flemming
carried on the business under the old names, acting,
when his health permitted, some of the parts that his
late partner would have filled. Then, at the expiration
of the partnership period, Flemming went again to Eng-
land, and came back to make a fresh start with a com-
pany of his own, which was playing at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s the night before he died. He was to have appeared
in “ Simple Simon ” two nights later. With him, as it
had been with Brough, the worries of the actor hastened
the manager’s end, and, like Brough and Spurr before
him, Herbert Flemming went very near to dying on the
boards.
“ Sceneshifter ” : I am glad to see the Beatty-Mclntosh
co. Is going to revive that fine old-timer, “ She Stoops to
Conquer.” I have kindly memories of previous revivals
of the comedy, which Oliver Goldsmith originally called
“ The Mistakes of a Night.” Remember Louise Pomeroy,
Marie de Grey, Mrs. Bernard Beere, Mrs. Robert Brough,
and Mrs. Brown-Potter as Kate Hardcastle. Of the five,
I give the palm to Mrs. Brough. Mrs. Bernard Beere,
who staged the comedy twice at Sydney Criterion in 1892,
after the Royal fire, was too tall and stately for the
character, and it was very difficult to account for young
Marlow’s folly in mistaking so elegant a creature for the bar-
maid of a wayside inn. However, the ‘‘English Bernhardt,”
as I have heard her called, played with spirit in this scene,
and I well recall the demure tones of her reply to Marlow,
“ Nectar? nectar? That’s a liquor there’s no call for in
these parts. French, I suppose. We keep no French
wines here, sir.” Generally speaking, Mrs. Beere’s man-
ner was too polished and town-bred. Her Lady Teazle
was more convincing. Louise Pomeroy, a ’Murkan star,
who first visited this country in the ’eighties, and who
was a ravishing Cleopatra at Sydney Royal in, 1882, in-
cluded Goldsmith’s comedy in her repertoire. I saw her
in it at the now defunct Queen’s Theatre in Princes-st.,
Dunedin, where she had the support of her hubby,
Arthur Elliott, also Charles Hall, as Hastings, J. G.
Joyce as old Hardcastle, and Blanche Lewis (now the
wife of Albert Norman, Bland Holt’s erstwhile stage
villain) as Mrs. Hardcastle. A fine comedian, J. J.
Kennedy, who figured with Billy Hoskins in a memorable
revival of “ The Comedy of Errors,” was Tony Lumpkin.
What a treat was the Brough-Boucicault version in four
acts ! Percy Lyndal was the lover, Bob Inman his pal,
Leopold a comical Diggory, William Holman (still with
us) was Sir Charles ; Bob Brough made a glorious Tony,
and his mother, Miss Rorner, a perfect Mrs. Hardcastle.
It was in 1896 that the Bellew-Potter crowd played the
piece, but La Gown Trotter was rotten. Charles Berkeley
made a fine Tony Lumpkin, when he got a show at the
character.
