LITTLE BOY IN CHARGE : “ Conic along , gents.
Better luck next time. ’AVE another shy!”
Harry Rickards has left his English estate, where for
the past few months he has lived a life of rural ease amid
cows, milkmaids, lambs, goats, and other phenomena
of the sort which he had till recently only encountered in
the form of stage props, and embarked for Sydney. Harry
is now only a showman by spasms, as it were. He has
prospered so exceedingly in the past few years that he has
been enabled to leave his various “ houses” in the charge
of managers, and be that for which Nature, in all likeli-
hood, originally designed him—a grand old British squire.
Henry is to be entertained at a concert and supper by the
local citizens on his arrival, in recognition of his services
to Art, his geniality, and other alleged achievements and
qualities.
“ Sceneshifter ”: The revival of the seldom-acted
“Twelfth Night” by Margaret Anglin recalls a few
memories. It was superbly done by Musgrove’s company
at the Sydney Royal in 1903, with W. H. Denny as Mal-
volio, Dora Rignold as Viola, Maud Milton as Maria, Nora
Kerin as Olivia, Athol Ford as Sir Toby Belch, Oroker
King as Sir Andrew, and Charles Kenningham as a sweet-
voiced clown. Boiled down to three acts, and graced
with lovely scenery and costumes, this revival seemed to
promise a long run, which it didn’t have. Two or three
weeks was all Mussy could get out of his edition de luxe
of the Bard’s comedy. In 1882, at the same theatre,
eventuated Louise Pomeroy’s notable production, in
which she proved a splendid Viola. Her support included
Herbert Flemming as Sebastian, Theo Hamilton as Sir
Toby, J. B. Steele as Malvolio, Harry Leston as Sir
Andrew, J. Musgrave as the clown, Lena Ellis as Maria,
and Jessie Gray as Olivia. Alfred Clint made a big
feature of the scenery, and a very pretty dance closed the
comedy. In Maoriland, Louise Pomeroy doubled the
roles of Sebastian and Viola. Another ’Murkan star,
Janet Warldorf, played “Twelfth Night” in Melbourne.
Augustin Daly, the one time big Yankee manager, was a
great and original producer of Shakspeare. He did the
comedy in three scenes—Olivia’s House, the Duke’s
Palace and Olivia’s Garden, and repeated each. Daly did
not adopt the usual Elizabethan setting, but laid the
scene in a classical period. The court of the Duke of
Illyria was a picture of magnificence which might
have suggested the palace of Antiochus the Great.
Irving played it at the London Lyceum in 1884, but it
was a bitter frost. The Thames almost froze over because
of it. Much of this was owing to his original misconcep-
of Malvolio. During the brief run he entirely re-con-
structed the character, and in the mad scene no longer
grovelled on the stage and caught straws. He, as the
situation really seenjs to demand, stood up, erect and in-
dignant, as a man unjustly deprived of his liberty would.
Irving overloaded the five acts of his version with
gorgeous scenery, there being no less than 16 changes,
most of them heavy “ sets.”
“ Lyre ” : Re the National Opera Company. “ Bohemian
Girl” follows “ Maritana.” Balfe (1808-1870) and Wallace
(1814-1865)were contemporaries and rivals. “The Bohemian
Girl” came in 1843, “ Maritana ”in 1845—a glorious pair
indeed. Wallace’s delicious ballads show thought and
polish. Balfe seems to have composed more freely, in
spontaneous rills from the fountain of melody, and indeed
he gave himself no time to think. How different the
troublous vagabond life of Wallace from the steady pros-
perity of Balfe, taken by the hand by all, from
Rossini and Cherubini onwards 1 His multifarious
operas were Italian, French and English. What
mountains of fine music evaporate ! As for Wallace,
I cannot help thinking he was a bit of a Munchausen. I
knew a man who knew him in Sydney. He seemed to be
only a humble music teacher. He was little more than a
lad when he arrived there, aged 21, in 1835. How did he
go sheep farming? Then it seems to me his life was
saved by the Maori chief’s daughter because he composed
an opera, in London, in the “Pocahontas” vein, called
“The Desert Flower.” Why did he go whaling? His
musical genius was enough to carry him along even in
such remote places and times. His travels all over India,
before the railways, must be exaggerated. Also his
tobacco and piano speculations, etc., in South America.
Who vouches for him ?
“Melb. “She Stoops to Conquer” is underlined at
Melbourne Royal. Goldsmith’s masterpiece and Sheridan’s
“ Rivals ” are the finest of racy English comedies. “ The
School for Scandal ” ranks higher, but is comparatively
French and artificial, with its crystal brilliancy and impos-
sible epigrams. When “ She Stoops to Conquer ” was first
placed in rehearsal, the two best actors in the company
threw up their parts as Tony Lumpkin and Marlow.
They said it was unmitigated rot. Everybody
prophesied failure, as with Sothern in “Our
American Cousin ”at the London Haymarket. Mathews
(not acting there at the time) saw a notice in the green-
room, “ Rehearse ‘ She Stoops to Conquer’ To-morrow.”
He persuaded manager Buckstone to force “ Our Ameri-
can Cousin ’’ahead. Buckstone and Sothern each made
£30,000 out of it. When Barry Sullivan managed the
Melbourne Royal he posted a notice, “ ‘She Stoops to
Conquer’ Next Saturday Night.” Charley Young, cast
for Lumpkin, protested he had not time for study,, and
left the establishment. Richard Stewart, Nellie’s
father, was immediately engaged, and found no diffi-
culty with Tony. It was one of Ooppin’s crack parts.
Rogers and Lambert both excelled as Hardcastle, and
Mrs. Phillips, mother of Hans of that ilk, was an ideal
Mrs. Hardcastle.
“C.S.G.”: Over the exuberance of the peanut-laden
youth who sits in Sydney Tivoli gallery these times, a dark
cloud, or smudge of misgiving, lies heavy, like a damper.
The cause, alas, is not far to seek. Irving Sayles is fast
losing the deafening blast of rag-time voice that he once
exploded nightly. His old ear-splitting howl is a
mere ghost of its former self. The very orchestra, which
at one time, when Irving’s “ turn ” was next, stood on its
hind legs and pawed the air with an exulting medley of
tumultuous sound, is now hushed and grave. It heralds
him with th e piano, not to say the pianissimo , stop on.
The spectacle of an outrageous voice slowly fading to
normal proportions makes the gallery feel sad.
“ S.S.” : One finds the reason of Miss Anglin’s liking for
“Zira”in Act 111., where she gets her great chance to
“pile it on,” and she piles it as if it were a pyramid
instead of a mere drama. The agony of Zira’s encounter
with Ruth Wilding is long drawn, but Miss Anglin saves
it from tediousness by her tremendous energy and fine
play of emotion. In the hands of 99 actresses out of 100
that lingering recrimination would fall flat, but the Anglin
saves the situation with great vigor, and the mere con-
sumption of physical energy probably excuses the heaving
bosom so much in-evidence. But, all the same, I had the
comment of a gallery neighbor on another big American
ringing in my ears : “ Lurnrne, she’s er great actress 1
Look et the play of her chest!” Large chest expansion
is as mucfi a feature in an actress as in an athlete.
“Hiphen”: I have a suggestion to make to producers
of emotional plays. It is that each emotional-looking
female who enters the theatre should be presented with
a small packet of cough drops. T’other evening I went
to see “The Thief.” After one act I began to think I
had made a mistake and had wandered into a consump-
tive sanatorium. Finally I came to the
conclusion that I had struck a theatre-
night of the One Lung Club or the Weak
Chested Association. But it was just
ordinary audience, for another night at
“The Truth” it was exactly the same.
Every tearful situation aroused a verit-
able avalanche of coughing. It was quite
a study. I gave up trying to listen to the
dialogue and started classifying the
coughs. There was the small, shy scrape
of the timid maiden lady. There was
the dry, husky rasp of she with the
doubtful past, the certain present and
the most uncertain future. There was
the pathetic little wheeze of the girl who
had that day quarrelled with her lover
in just the same (or nearly the same)
way as Margaret Anglin had fallen out
with Kolker. Loudest of all was the
strenuous bark of the stout lady who
hung ecstatically over the gallery-rails,
with the lemonade bottle slipping un-
heeded from her nerveless grasp. It was
all very interesting indeed ; but I had
not gone there (and paid) to collect and
classify coughs and keep them bottled
afterwards. Nor do I preserve dried
coughs pinned on cards as some people
do butterflies. lam not a collector. I only wanted to
hear Miss Anglin, and I couldn’t.
“Snell” : A young- Australian singer was suddenlj’’ ele-
vated from the ruck during the run of “ The Lily of Killar-
ney,” at Melbourne Royal, and put in some excellent
work without getting any newspaper credit. The im-
ported principal fell ill, and Rufus Ferguson was called
upon at shortest notice to essay Danny Mann. He had
not understudied, and had no preparation beyond what had
come through an observant eye and an attentive ear, but
he stepped into the pare gaily enough, and made good.
His Danny went brightly for two or three nights. The
press wasn’t looking, but the gallery got on to it.
Philip Wirth, of the Circus, had a cruel disappointment
the very day he returned to Melbourne from his latest
trip to Europe. In Berlin, or somewhere, he had fixed
up a star engagement with a man who dived 40ft. through
the air (all diving begins in the air by the way) on to an
inclined platform, striking it so exactly “flush ” with his
chest and outstretched arms that he slid down the soaped
surface without hurting himself—as a rule. The plane
was about 30ft. long, and by the time the rapid traveller
reached the bottom the audience had enjoyed a lovely
thrill. The feat was everything that Australia could wish
for. It would have revived memories of the intrepid
Peart (of Fitzgeralds’ Circus) who used to dive from a
giddy height into a canvas tank of water, until he struck
the edge of the tank with his chest and died of shock to
the system. On his way out to Australia Phil Wirth
dreamed of letters in the newspapers protesting against
the unwholesome excitement appertaining to the
dangerous performance of the man who dived upon the
greasy platform. He also prayed that Providence might
spare that hare-brained individual for a long engagement
in this country—for the sake of all parties concerned.
Alas ! On the very morning of his arrival in Melbourne a
cablegram informed Wirth that the diver had met his
death in Berlin the day previously. He had hit the edge
of the inclined plane instead of the flat thereof. By delay-
ing the catastrophe a few weeks the unfortunate daredevil
could have given Widths’ the splendid advt. of arranging
a public funeral for him—with the Circus band, mounted
on the six elephants, playing the “ Dead March” at the
head of the procession, and the giraffe bringing up the
rear.
“ Sceneshifter ”: It is a daring thing to introduce a
re-incarnated Christ as leading male on the London
stage—a stage that 10 years or so ago would not permit a
Sydney parson's Biblical play to be produced for fear of
“shocking the religious-minded.” J. K. Jerome’s new
piece, “The Passing of the Third Floor Back,” does it,
however, nightly at the London St. James’, and no one
takes offence. Forbes Robertson, of the beauteous voice
and the impressive presence, plays the Saviour. His role
is to mistake vice and humbug for virtue and truth —to
the confusion of the former qualities. It is a subtle and
original stage idea that one would not have looked for
from the cockney Mr. Jerome.
“Sideshow”: One of the few interesting baritone warblers
in Melbourne is Claude Hall, the amateur actor and re-
citer. Hearing him for the first time at Glen’s Concert
Hall the other night, I was struck by his artistic render-
ing of several pretty little “drawing-room” songs. He
knew exactly what he could do, and he did it admirably,
which is more than can be said for the young lady who
was giving the concert. Another pleasing item was a
violin solo by one Du Boulay, a tall, swarthy, young Aus-
tralian, who went abroad for his training, and was now
making a first appearance in Melbourne.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) ivill he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. ivill be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.
Comet: “Competition” a very jerky effort —
you’ll lose your tail if you’re not careful... .R.E.L.: Two
or, three things spoil “L’Amour.” Don’t know frogs
intimately, but don’t believe the noise they make after
7 p.m. are “ night alarms.” Also some of the expressions
are clumsy Airynut: “The Safety Bag” has been
jettisoned. Its lack of local application was in part
responsible for its overthrow. ..A.8.: No room for heavy
essays on “Progress” or about revisiting childhood’s
home W. J. Walters : “ The Breaker’s ” poems are out
of print Cavendish : Your satire is miles too long, and
its archaic form is against it. The modern demand is for
something brief in the way of satires.,... .Sprucah: Your
pars are rough, and the writing is so evil and the lines so
close together that medical attention is an impossibility.
Give about three times more space to the next batch
R.D. (South Africa): The Cape Times doesn’t know its
subject. The sugar “bounty” (so-called) was £335,916
for the financial year to 30th June, 1907—not £573,000.
That is to say, the Government collected £546,653 in
sugar excise, and remitted £335,916 as “ bounty,” so that
it was £210,000 odd in pocket instead of being £573,000
out of pocket. As the cane grown increased from
I, tons in 1902 to 3,950,340 tons in 1907, Australia
is feeling quite satisfied with its anti-Kanaka policy.
And as each State is credited with the excise on' the
sugar it uses, and debited with its share of the “ bounty,”
the tale about other States having to pay “hundreds of
thousands” to support the sugar-growing States is sheer
rubbish Shrimp: Sorry it isn’t good enough
J. : You are sparring at a shadow-grievance. In all
capital cases the Crown offers accused legal aid. For
other cases there is an Act which provides that any
prisoner who can’t afford counsel himself may at any
time before the jury is sworn make application to the
presiding. Judge or committing magistrate for legal aid.

that he had previously worked for an Irishman

Kermeth D.: You state truths that are almost
painfully obvious. Take this re Death :
’Tis Death that quenches man’s all-flick’ring light,
That visits every peaceful smiling glade
To cast, not beauty, nay ! but doleful shade —
’Tis Death which saddens all, leaves nothing bright.
The Wasp: Your poem on the Gotch-Hackenschmidt
match is a shade belated : the kick-up took place some
months ago. But it is too interesting to be lost:
Wrestler Gotch gouged Hackenschmidt
Like a wild beast of prey,
Prepared to defeat the world’s champion
By treacherous foul play.
The Russian appealed to the Referee,
While cheers and applause
Rang out for the American
Who gouged with his claws.
Bruised, bleeding, and half-blind,
The Russian sank to the mat,
And declined to resume wrestling
With the wild human cat.
The match was a grim farce,
A bloodthirsty display.
Gotch won the contest
By loathsome foul play.

as to their looks.
Some deadly results have been got at the
Port Melbourne rifle ranges from a new explosive
called Pomeroid. Martini-Henry bullets weigh-
ing 372 grains were charged with 13 grains of the
explosive and fired at oil drums, empty or filled
with water, at heavy piebes of timber, and at
boulders, and the cheerful substance blew them
all to pieces. Also a bullet fired through a canvas
target exploded on that slight resistance. John
Pomeroy, the inventor, considers he has arrived
at something good, and if Australia had only its
arsenal and ammunition factories in going order it
would be in a position to keep that good thing for
itself, and so cause possible enemies to think twice
before landing once.

KUGELNANNS
PURE /lERBAL SKIN SOAP
IMPORTANT ANALYSIS. I
ARTHUR B. CHATER, Fellow of the Chemical Society; Examiner in Chemistry
to Queensland Pharmacy Board ; Public Analyst for City of Brisbane.
Analytical Laboratory, South Brisbane, July 12th, 1905.
Messrs. H. E. KUGELMANN & CO.
D ear Sirs, —Having made an analysis of “ H. E. Kugelmann’s Pure Herbal Skin
Soap,” I find it to be an absolutely pure Soap, containing no free Alkali, which has an
injurious action upon the human skin. It should therefore be suitable for application to
any human skin, or for use as a dentifrice. It also contains extracts of medicinal herbs
which the proprietors claim as a trade secret, and to have a curative effect upon the skin.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) ARTHUR B. CHATER.
[Copy.]
Central Analytical and Assay Office,
Vickery’s Chambers, 82 Pitt Street, Sydney,
21 st September, 1908.
Messrs. H. E. KUGELMANN & CO.,
199 Clarence Street, Sydney.
” • .j.
Gentlemen, —I have made a careful analysis of a sample of “ Kugelmann’s Pure
Herbal Skin Soap," which I find to be composed of vegetable oils and fats saponified
with pure alkali.
It contains no free alkali nor any substance deleterious to the most delicate skin.
The perfume and color are both of natural and harmless plant origin, neither having any
trace of animal or poisonous materials.
Having powerful but non-poisonous antiseptics thoroughly incorporated in the process
of manufacture, this Soap possesses healing properties which will remove diseases of the
skin and restore it to a healthy condition.
It is a beautifully-finished article, comparing favorably with similar imported Soaps,
and it used regularly as an adjunct of the toilet, it will assist in keeping the body in
health, and will have a pleasing, soothing effect on the skin.
Yours faithfully,
F. H. MOLESWORTH, F.C.S., F.G.S., M.5.C.1., London,
Public Analyst and Assayer.
Obtainable at all Chemists, Drapers, and Storekeepers throughout Australasia at 2s. per box of three tablets.
If cannot purchase locally, send postal note or stamps of any State to the value of 2s. to the Proprietors,
when it will be posted per return mail.

ROM the stand-
point of the In-
terstate tourist,
Queensland is
full of interest.
It is more—it is
full of wonders!
From Brisbane to Thursday Island
it has enough scenery of the imposing
with ;? rd<l, ' 1 t ° l m:lke Ule tripper drunk
th ltl T ! ; '- Lin - the truest
' ° ' tC!UI Jesa,d aho "t it is that it doesn’t
;; und-,t inebriates ! The mind reels with the
beauty of it, while the sight is, in a loose way of
speaking staggered with its opulence. With the
chest of soils and a tropical climate, with
• 16 P ar t‘eular forms of tropical growth,
. tre ®. and creeper and flower, growing
>n prodigious luxuriance, its effect upon the
Stranger is easily understandable. To-day there
are men going to Queensland to take up land,
■ ,• t the same time, the steamers of the Five
ompanies are taking an increasing number of
tourists. But the tourist traffic is not as great as
it might be-perhaps because the inhabitants of
the bouth have yet to learn a lot about their
northern countrymen and the land they live in
Victoria and New South Wales are muffii better
known to the bulk of Australians, just so much as
most are more familiar with Sydney and Mel-
bourne than they are with Brisbane
Brisbane is a small town to the southern eve
which is used to largeness in city growths, but it
is a bright one, and nicely situated on a bunch of
bids, through which the Brisbane River crawls
■in the shape of a tortured snake. There
are views to be had there which are
worth stealing in a pocket kodak; and
t lere are air and good sunshine to keep the
spirits out of cold storage. Also, there are
plenty of life and heaps of amusements,
ranging from high drama to sea-pad-
dhng; so the visitor is in no danger
of expiring from utter nothing-
ness. Moreover, the deep sea
steamers of the Five Com-
panies can wander up the
stream right into the centre
of the city’s digestion, so to
speak, and lean up against
the buildings in Queen-street.
An improved river has led
to that improved navigation. By dredging
a-nd by the construction of training walls,°the
river is now damp to the depth of 24ft. at
lo w tide—thus allowing big boats to paddle up from
More ton Bly in about two hours. The convenience
to the passenger is obvious. He steps ashore and
boards a tram with as much facility as he would in
bydney or Melbourne. And, talking about the
trams, Brisbane has a service that will surprise
the dictionary into its best efforts. The whole
city is linked up in a mesh of rails, and the trams,
which will only suspend motion at street corners
or white posts, fly along the roadways like scoot-
ing comets. Threepence will take the passenger
right through the city from one side to the other
while penny sections save those people who only
want to travel short distances from com-
plaining about extortion. By the trams, one
goes anywhere m Brisbane. Such largely charm-
ing spots as Breakfast Creek, Dutton Park
Mt. Coot-tha, Toowong and the Western Suburbs
are all reached by those swift public vehicles;
whde, even within the city itself, they’re as
handy as may be. Public buildings, leaning to
compactness and dignity rather than to grandeur,
Brisbane has like any other city of importance.
The Parliament Buildings in George-street, and
the Treasury Buildings in Queen-street are fine •
while others, such as the G.P.0., are at least
worth a tram-ride. Gardens and parks, wherein
the tired stranger may get an idea of the luxuri-
ance of the local foliage, are plentiful. The
Botanical Gardens, on the wedge of earth that
steals out into the river, so to say, are valuable
for their big collection of tropical and sub-tropical
plants and, on. warm days, are as enticing
as cool bars. Other gardens are Albert Park
with an observatory tower, providing a spacious
bird s eye view of Brisbane; Musgrave Park a
gay resort, with cricket pitches and lawns for
respectable games such as bowls; and the Acclima-
tisation Society’s Gardens, wherein may be found,
despite its battlemented name, a great show oF
Queensland orchids. Outside fhe city itself, one
can easily knock a large cavity in a week in ’visit-
mg and recording. One can go out to the Enog-
gera Reservoir, picnic if need be. (it’s a favorite
picnic spot), or just lounge around and praise the
view, which is idyllic—if it’s nothing else!
-Nudgee, with its pineapple gardens and sea-
jeach ; bandgate, a seaside resort, similar in
popularity to St.. Kilda or Manly; Red-
cliffe, another attractive resort on Moreton
ii ■ , old ?™ ek ' »'ie of the sources of
the. .Brisbane Water Supply, and set in a fistful
of forest-grown hills and gullies; Brookfield,
notable for its strawberry and orange cultivation-
all of those are within a few miles of the city, and
may be visited at the price of two or three
shillings per head. And that doesn’t exhaust the
list. X here are others, but, by keeping to the
them 010 "’ ' vds ’ tor won t trouble any about
After Brisbane there is still a whole State to be
considered, but unless the stranger has about a
century-and-a-half to spare—and he can’t do that
without neglectinghis business or pursuits a trifle—
he won t have time to study it in detail. But, given
two or three weeks, he can see quite enough to im-
press him, and can then take the balance foreran ted.
if he s wise, he’ll board the boat again,“and go
upward to Rockhampton, which is a wee bit back
from the coast at Keppel Bay. Rockhampton is
interesting, in that it is adjacent, or rather not
tar distant from the great Mount Morgan gold
anc \, c ?Pf! er . mines, in themselves worth the trip
north if their is nothing else to look at. The city
is itself up-to-date, comfortable, and inviting:
the principal city in a large church diocese ; and,
like other Queensland places, full of luxuriant
gardens. If Rockhampton is enticing, Mackay
a few hours journey away, is equally so.
Une of the principal centres of the cane in-
dustry, it may now be seen in the middle of its
season which extends from July over the latter
half of the year. Those who take interest in
great Australian industries will feel impelled to
have a look over the cane mills and watch the
manufacture of sugar. Even Bowen and Towns-
ville may be visited, time and weather permitting,
as they won’t fail to surprise. Bowen is noted for
its many extensive orchards and its pastoral
pursmtSr-whilstTownsville, the commercial capital

" INSECTICIDE “
THE- GREAT INSECT DESTROYER


When the piano duty was before the Aus-
tralian Parliament the lying yell of the Freetrade
gang was that such a duty would be nothing more
or less than a subsidy to Beale and Co. There
wasn’t enough piano business in Australia to sup-
port more than one factory, even if we could make
pianos in Australia, which, of course, we couldn’t,
though we were doing it all the time. Also, if we
made pianos, it would not be a genuine manufac-
tory, but merely the fitting together of imported
parts. And so on and so forth ad, lib., regardless
of the fact that in the only existing Australian
factory almost everything was made on the pre-
mises, down to the screws and pins. On
Friday last, at Richmond (Vic.), the foun-
dation stone of Mr. Wertheim’s piano fac-
tory was laid down, in the presence of
a distinguished company which included the
great Tommy Bent. So within the year Australia
will have two big piano factories in full blast, and
the chances are that more will follow. The duty
on pianos is 30 per cent, general and 25 per cent,
preferential. So far the duty has not had the
effect elf closing up the fountains of Australian
music, jas foretold, and the weary night-hawk is
still soothed on his homeward way by the strains
of “ The Merry Widow ” waltz and other classic
pieces. But the d.uty has made it more profitable to
manufacture pianos in Australia than to import
them, and they are being manufactured accord-
ingly.* '
1 he person who fills up the bottle of the good
brand with inferior liquor struck a cold time in
Victoria recently. The Court fell tooth and nail
on two liquid vendors who filled up with an
inferior article bottles bearing the trade mark of
Dixon and Co.’s largely-demanded O.T. punch.
“ O.T. i” as everybody knows, is one of the drinks
of the gods, and is frequently used by Zeus him-
self as a nightcap, and the caitiffs who use its
bottles to dispose of an inferior article are liable
to a minimum fine of £lOO. Caitiffs had better
make a note of this.*

CAT ARRH is inflammation of the lining- membrane
of the nose and passages which lead to the ears
throat, and lungs; it injures sight and hearing,
destroys taste and smell; the discharge causes dys-
pepsia, and consumption.

According to recent estimates, the British
unemployed number 15,000 in Manchester and
Salford, 12,350 at Liverpool, 12,000 in the Tyneside
towns, 10,000 at Sunderland, 11,000 at Sheffield,
and about 20,000 at Glasgow. The local Labor
party proposes as a remedy a reduction of working
hours, so that more ants can get on to one bit of
meat or more men live on one bit of work. But
if the bit of Work which now supports 10 men is
made to keep 12, either their wages (which are
low enough already) must be reduced, or else the
cost of their product must go up. And if the cost
of the product goes up, it will be still less able
than it is now to compete, under Ereetrade con-
ditions, against the imported article, and increased
foreign competition will tend to take the bit of
work away altogether. Then, instead of support-
ing 12 men or 10 men, it will support nobody.
On page 11 appears the advertisement of
a well-meaning but soon to be disillusioned man
who proposes to raise an Australian navy by
voluntary contribution. He hopes to get at least
one pound per annum from each Australian adult
during the year, with which to provide the Aus-
tralian Government with the means of construct-
ing a battle fleet. Australia has tried the volun-
tary business in many ways, and in every one of
them it has failed to justify itself. It has tried
voluntary defence, with disastrous results. It
has experimented with voluntary municipal
cleanliness, and the undertakers have been
kept busy. It has tested voluntary education,
and the victims of it are hewers of wood and
drawers of water even unto this day. And now
this patriotic individual proposes to try voluntary
battle-fleet building. It is up to the advocates of
the voluntary business to dig deep into the depths
of their pockets and send the money along in
shoals, for if ever a country needed a battle fleet
it is Australia, and if ever the voluntary or
Private Enterprise system had a chance to justify
itself it is this one. Supporters of the idea are
requested to send no money at present, but to
communicate with “Rangitira,” Box 247, G.P.0..
Sydney.

“I have to thank Cuticura Reme-
dies for completely curing me of one
of the worst attacks of boils it is pos-
sible to imagine. I suffered from
boils incessantly for a year, and I was
So unsuccessful in trying remedy after
remedy that I began to despair of ever
getting my system clear of this dis-
tressing ailment. My shoulder and
back were principally affected. Any
one who has had a single boil knows
how dreadfully painful boils are, so it
will be evident how much I suffered
when I tell you that I had four large
ones. They would be a long time com-
ing up and so very painful all the time
that I could hardly bear my clothes
on. They were very large, one on my
back extending to the size of a five-
shilling piece, while the smallest was
as big as a sixpence. My health suffered
much as the result of the attack and I
got info a very run-down condition.
After I had tried other means without
any benefit, I read of a cure by Cuticura,
and though I had not much faith, I
thought I could not do any harm in
trying Cuticura Soap. So I bought
a tablet and after I had washed the
affected parts a few times I noticed
such a great improvement that I de-
cided I could not do better than try
the whole Cuticura Treatment. I got
the Cuticura. Soap, Ointment, and Pills,
and my progress from that time was
truly wonderful. A great change for
the better took place almost immedi-
ately and now, well within three months
from when I started with the Soap, I
can pronounce myself absolutely cured.
Cuticura Remedies have got the disease
right out of my system. I am ex-
tremely grateful for the great benefit
I have derived, and I wish most ear-
nestly to recommend Cuticura Remedies
to every one troubled with boils. I
shall never be without Cuticura Soap..
Mrs. E. J. Chapman, 9, Union Square,

Britain's Alleged Horse-Sense.
A Lord Somebody, who seems to have sounded
Parliamentary opinion on the matter, declares
that the British Government would get the sup-
port of /5 per cent, of the House of Commons if
it were to table a Bill for the encouragement
of cavalry horse-breeding in Britain and the
prohibition of 'horse exports from the country. The
policy of Protection in certain small matters
appeals to the dullest Freetraders. They can
understand how unwise it is to let the local manu-
facture of horses decline for want of Government
support, even as they understand the absurdity of
permitting cheap American hops to imperil the
respectable old industry of hop-growing in a coun-
try famous for its beer. But, whilst they would
willingly exchange “freedom” of trade for “restric-
tion” of trade as regards hops and horses, they shut
their eyes and ears to the fact that a little clique
of mine-owners is robbing the country of its coal
possessions and selling the fuel to foreign navies
and manufacturers. An embargo on the exporta-
tion of gee-gees.is desirable, but it should be pre-
ceded by a full stop to Britain’s foreign trade in
coals. And that is something not to be thought
of by the House of Commons. The millionaire
mine-owners would bring their wide range of in-
terest to prevent any such un-Hinglish interference
with the liberty of the subject. Likewise the
mighty body of ship-owners would rise in their
fat wrath to oppose it. The miners who pick and
truck millions of tons of coal for annual export
would also be horribly indignant. Government
restriction of Britain’s madness in that respect is
out of the question thus far. Horse-breeding is
quite another story. Many of the noble persons
who own the earth would gladly take a personal
interest in the production of big-boned steeds if
the State gave them a bonus on the output and
the War Office [laid £4O a head for the animals.
And that’s how it happens that 75 per cent,
of the House of Commons members are pre-
pared to rob British trade of its .freedom in one
particular line. They are willing to apply the
principles of wicked Socialism to one industry for
the sake of the common weal, 'out they couldn’t
think of abating their antagonism to Tariff Pro-
tection and Socialism in general!

“D.M.W.” writes from Maoriland :
The political atmosphere of Maoriland is a great dent 1
more electric than it has been for years past. In a feu
months there will be a general election, the first sinci
one-half of the present electors became qualified to vote
in which the large personality of Richard John Seddon
will no longer count. No one can really gauge popular
feeling. The Ward Government is much spoken against
in many electorates that were intensely, blindly attached
to Seddon. As a reactionary force, the Farmers’ Union,
will probably make itself felt. The Labor party is pro-
foundly dissatisfied, although far from being united in
opinion and aim. There are large new organised Labor
forces in rural districts, whose vote is practically an un-
known quantity. The Cold-Tea party, with its‘demand
for National Option and shutting of pubs by
the vote of a bare majority, is now fairly
in politics, and thousands of electors will sink
all their other political opinions in order to slay Mr.
Publichouse. The Ministry itself rouses no enthusiasm
and inspires little confidence. There is a steadily-grow-
ing uneasiness with regard to its finance. The opinions
of its members are so much at variance as to create a
large distrust of their policy. The whole constitution of
the Cabinet is felt to be based on a series of compromises,
and the holding of office is to each Minister of far
greater importance than adherence to any political
principle. So much is this the case that the Leader
of the Opposition might comfortably accept a portfolio
without more sacrifice of anything in the way of con-
viction than the rest of those who form the Ministry.
Whether the electors will tolerate a continuance of this
state of affairs or not remains to be seen, but not very
much in the way of choice is left to them. Massey stands
for a good deal that is reactionary, but he professes a
wholesome hatred of borrowing, and that may bring him
votes.
Ward, to meet this danger, throws out an immense
programme of roads and bridges. But they are mostly
loan money roads and borrowed bridges, and the country
begins to wear a really alarmed expression on that subject.
“ Kamarooka ” writes
The Victorian Government makes a good thing out of
deluded selectors who take up bits of the 2,000,000 acres
of State lands Bent says he has on hand, and then when
the deluded settlers find that the land is no good and
leave it in disgust and with curses, the Government is
the richer by the survey fees paid on the abandoned land,
and then baits are held out to catch other deluded
settlers to take up the same land and again pay survey fees
on it. And if the second lot of settlers also find that
they have a piece of sterile desert and throw it up, they
lose the money they paid for survey fees, and so on. A
case in point is what is knowm as the Whipstick scrub, a
big area of State lands a few miles to the north of
Bendigo. About 40,000 acres of this were thrown open for
selection some four years ago in 200 and 300 acre blocks.
There was a terrific scramble on the part of cockies’ sons
and others to get a block, and the successful applicants
gleefully pulled out of their pockets cheques for £8 and
£lO apiece, which were handed over to the Government
in liquidation of the survey fees. The settlers, having
paid their tenners, bogged in like a mob of hogs on a
jamboree, and tore up the scrub, and tore in crops of
w’heat and oats, and then started to bog in and fence
their blocks, befoi the crops got too high. But after
they had shoved in a lot of posts and did various odd jobs
about the place, they went and had a look at the crops.
And when they looked they saw' no crops. Then they
waited a few months before they looked again, but still
there were no crops in sight, although the scrub
which was rooted out was growing again like the
Lfevil. So the first year passed, and it was a
dead failure for the settlers. A few', wiser than the rest,
said the land was no good to man or beast, and cleared off
it. The others said that they would have another go, so
they stopped, and rooted up the new crop of scrub,
and rooted in a new crop of wheat and oats,
finding time in the meanwhile to hand out a few
morepoundsto the Government for rent. The second
season was a bigger failure, if possible, than the first for
the wheat and oat crops, but the new crop of scrub was
taller and strong-er than ever. This was enough for most
of the remaining settlers, and with a collective curse they
packed up their belongings, and left the land, and their
visions of an earthly paradise upon it. Now', the same
land is again being advertised for selection, and the
survey fees are set down at from £8 to £lO for each block,
but nary a word about the fees having been paid onc»
before
“F.R.”: One of the most persistent cries of the
Tories who oppose compulsory cutting up of large
estates is that it will have a ruinous effect upon
our wool production. Even if it were true that
sheep would decrease in numbers, there is not
much in the argument, as cultivation is far more
remunerative than jumbucks. But, as happens
usually with Tory statements, this argument has
neither top nor bottom left when the facts are
ascertained. Victoria has cut up sundry big estates
during the past five or six years. Yet the Govern-
ment Statist now announces that the sheep have
increased from 11,455,115 in 190 G to 14,140,734 in
1908 (March). And the Statist unkindly adds that
“almost the whole of thisincrease (over 2,500,000)
is accounted for by the man who owns less than 2000
sheep.” And it is reasonable that this should be
so. In pastoral matters, as in agricultural ones,
the man with the gigantic holding seldom gets the
best out of his land. There is “ intense cultiva-
tion ”in sheep as well as in vegetables. Many a
big station might multiply its jumbucks by three
if it were cut into small areas—and multiply its
men by twenty or fifty.

Amid the uproarious demand for more
money made by Melbourne Hospital minions of alj
grades, the honorary doctors are beginning to
think hard. The Think makes them ponder the
desirability of asking for a rise in their own non-
existent salaries. They work for nothing except
glory and experience. As boys they left school
to become medicos. That meant five years at
the University (if they were lucky enough or
brilliant enough to pass successfully each year).
The majority of them were toiling at Pa’s
expense. Following this came the impera-
tive walking of the hospitals for necessary
experience at no pay a year. Only an extra-
ordinary person could earn more than tuppence
outside and alongside this work. No wonder
medicos want to charge when they grow feathers
on their wings ! They have waited long enough
without any real affluence coming to them.
How charming is the seriousness with which
the Briton takes the Society convention ! In the
London Daily Mail; one Lady Grove writes a
thrilling “special column ” on Visiting Cards and
what to do with them. She knows “a colonel
of exalted rank” who has a wife. The two were
doomed for a space to live in a provincial town.
The neighbors called. Now occurred what Lady
Grove cites as
an admirable example of delicate consideration for the
feelings of others, where the convention of their own
particular world was set at naught by a generally inac-
cessible couple in the tactful effort to diminish the dis-
tance between themselves and those they were brought
into daily contact with.
The degraded provincialists
almost invariably had their own and their husbands’
names jointly inscribed on one piece of pasteboard 1
Not so, of course, the exalted ones.
The colonel and his wife naturally had their names on
separate cards.
It was a ghastly situation. The Colonel and his
wife were faced with the grim alternatives
of (1) hurting the provincialists’ feelings by return-
ing their calls with cards which were correctly
inscribed, or (2) having fresh cards printed in imi-
tation of the provincialists’ cards, and jettisoning
social self-respect in the cause of kindly tact. A
brief struggle, and kindliness won. With jaws
doggedly set, the devoted pair went forth one
afternoon with cards on which, despite the rank of
the owners , their names were jointly inscribed !
It was not the proper thing, but it was magnifi-
cent. It was also humorless ; but that is a bulls-
eye which Lady Grove misses by a mile or so.


MICK. SIMMONS

Retired.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE owns a little villa
Beside the Sydney shore,
This grizzled, grey, old skipper,
Retired, and sixty-four.
I often see him sitting
On his verandah chair,
The sea-wind gently blowing
About his foam-white hair.
Before him spreads the Harbor ;
And, telescope to eye,
He watches, late and early,
The ship-procession ,by. <
And spar and mast and funnel,
And engine, sail and screw,
They speak to him the language
Of that great world he knew.
They tell him of the countries
That lie so far away ;
The messmates and the cronies
Of his departed day.
His wife is sleeping yonder,
He laid her down at sea ;
A long-gone captain’s daughter,
A captain’s darling she.
And oft, I know, his glasses
Grow filmy with the dim,
Sad memories and longings
The brave ships bring to him.
He knows them all by number,
And most he knows by name ;
The whither of their going,
The whence their cargoes came.
And oft I see him pass ng
His hand across his eyes,
When hoot the outward sirens
And high Blue Peter flies.
The ports that they are bound for—
Ah, these he knows right well,
The light and buoy and beacon,
The foghorn and the bell.
He sees, in mind, the coastline,
He sees the storm-swept bay,
And down the foggy channel
He feels a cautious way.
He sees the docks of London,
Where once his lading he
To cheer his merchant owners
Delivered faithfully.
In dreams he sees the Indies,
In dreams he doth behold
The coasts of California,
The fevered Coasts of Gold.
The Cape and Horn, in fancy,
He beats around again,
In sunshine and in starshine,
In clear and calm and rain.
The clipper roads, he knew them
The infant days of Steam—
And ships, long dead, forgotten,
Go drifting through his dream.
Along that wide verandah
He paces to and fro ;
And with him walk the shipmates
Of years and years ago.
He hears the homing chanteys,
And on the poop he stands,
With ready crew beneath him
To follow his commands.
He dresses and he strips her,
He lays her course once more
From Boston to the Foreland,
From ’Frisco to the Nore.
I doubt not he is ready
His final Port to win ;
I doubt not that St. Peter
Will let the old man in.
His papers all in order ;
His manifest clear writ,
With many goodly items
Set down, in gold, on it,
One day he’ll take his passage
To that Uncharted Shore
Whence neither crew nor captain
Re-voyage evermore.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.
Dodgewick’s Divorce.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DODGEWICK never approaches me
from the front. He leaps on me from
the innermost recesses of a private bar,
or drags me backwards out of a crowd and says,
“Look here, old man, I’m in an awful hole!
Have you got any money on you ? ”
Dodgewick never specifies any amount. He
waits until he sees how much I’ve got, and then
borrows it until salary day. I don’t think Dodge-
wick has been paid any salary for at least 18
months, because up to now he owes me about £23. I
don’t mind that so much, because I earn an enorm-
ous living by being a clerk and can afford to be
generous to Dodgewick. It is the load of family
trouble he heaps on me that I object to.
Dodgewick seems to think that I take an in-
terest in him. He told me one night that the
only souls who ever gave him any sympathy were
barmaids and myself. Dodgewick seems to re-
quire more sympathy than any man who has
lived since the barmaid was invented.
Last night, at seven o’clock, as I was going into
the Royal, I was seized from behind and lifted
into a dark corner. Any ordinary man would
have concluded he was being garroted or ar-
rested. I just said, “ How much do you want,
Dodgewick?”
He sobbed on my collar and said that he didn’t
want money —he wanted sympathy.
I said, “Well, come in and see Lottie.”
Dodgewick choked down a sob and said that
Lottie was a painted woman.
I said that I’d always thought the painted
ones contained more sympathy than the other
sort.
Dodgewick said that he wanted my sympathy—
also my moral support. He said that his wife
wanted to get a divorce, and that I was to come
along and stop her. He seemed to take it for
granted that I could stop her.
Up to that moment I didn’t know that Dodge-
wick had a wife. I didn’t even know he had a
home. I never met him either going to or com-
ing from it, and he never alluded to anything of
the kind in his conversation. Therefore I was
astonished.
I tried to ask some questions, to get some de-
tails, but Dodgewick caught me by the arm and
hurried me along towards Chancery Lane, where,
I was given to understand, Dodgewick’s wife and
another lady were waiting with the family lawyer.
I asked Dodgewick what they were waiting for.
Dodgewick said they were waiting for me 1
“But,” I gasped, as we stumbled and rushed
together up Little Collins-street, “ where do 1
come into the story ? ”
Oh, don't talk,” he wailed, “don’t talk, for
God’s sake! I told them you’d explain every-
thing.”
“ But what have I got to explain ? ”
“Oh, for God’s sake don’t ask me! They’ve
been going into my past life ever since four this
afternoon, and I’m all kite high unless you back
me up and explain how things are ! ”
“ What things ? ”
“Everything! Smooth ’em over—tell ’em it
will be all right. Eix ’em up any way you like, so
long as you break up the meeting ! ”
“Dodgewick,” I said sternly, for I saw there
was no escaping it, “why didn’t you come
to me before ? It isn’t fair to call me in at this
late stage of the proceedings. Why didn’t you
come to me before ? ”
“I did!” he answered. “I’ve been round
town five times since four o’clock this afternoon.
I stop in that office and listen to those women
having hysterics until I can’t stand it any longer.
Then I tell them I’in going out to look for you.”
“Dodgewick,” I said, “have you told those
women that I’ll”
“ Here we are ! ” he said, and dragged me up a
dark hall-way. Before I had time to adjust what
thoughts I had on hand, Dodgewick rushed me
along to the door, flung it open, and pushed mein
in front of him.
I stood for a moment contemplating the scene
before me, and I must admit I was never in an
atmosphere more heavily charged with emotion
than that was.
There was a tear-sodden woman in the last stage
of exhaustion sitting limply by a desk, behind
which sat the most wretched man I have ever seen
alive. These two I took to be Dodgewick’s wife
and her solicitor, and I subsequently found my
supposition to be correct. The second lady—who
looked as if she had been having uninterrupted
hysterics for a fortnight—l put down as the Diffi-
culty.
The lawyer stood up as I entered. “Mr.
Dodgewick,” he said, “I presume this is your
friend ?”
“Yes,” muttered Dodgewick. “This is my
friend Mr. Johnson. Mr. Johnson—Mr. Gadge-
snap.”
“ Gadgestack,” corrected the lawyer, angrily.
Dodgewick didn’t apologise. He just sat down
in a chair and mopped his forehead like a man
who has dropped a heavy burthen of trouble.
Both the women looked appealingly towards
me, and the distraught lawyer motioned me to a
chair and resumed his seat.
“ Now, Mr. Jolinson, ” he said, “if you will be
so good as to tell us what you know, we may be
able to get this unfortunate misunderstanding ad-
justed and then all will be well. Mr. Dodgewick
has assured us that you can explain everything.”
Mrs. Dodgewick here burst into tears. “Oh !
do explain everything,” she sobbed. “ Tellers nit
about it! Tell us everything! ”
“ Tell them ! ” said Dodgewick, in adiollow voice
behind. . /
“Be perfectly frank with us/’ pleaded the
lawyer. f
The Difficulty sniffed damply?—almost appre-
hensively—and said something about “hating to
be suspected, ” or “ selected, ’ ’ or something to that
effect.
There was a slight pause.
“Now, Mr. Johnson ! ”
It was the gong. I cleared my throat and
started to spar for an opening. Speaking in my
most deliberate tone, I said : “Before I make any
statements I would like you to tell me what it is
you wish cleared up. It is very difficult for me,
arriving as I do in the midst of your delibera-
tions ” ——
“ Quite so,” interrupted the lawyer ; “ I under-
stand ! You mean to say you would rather reply
to specific instances ? ”
“ Exactly! ”
He turned to his client. “ Are you agreeable
to that, Mrs. Dodgewick ? ”
“ Oh, yes ! Yes !”
Then to the Difficulty: “Are you agreeable,
madam ? ”
The strange lady sniffed again—defiantly this
time —and asserted that “nobody could say any-
thing against her.”
“Very good,” said the lawyer, turning to his
desk and examining his notes. He laid his finger
on one and looked keenly at me.
“Well, Mr. Johnson, we will take the evening
of June 25th last. Mr. Dodgewick says you were
with him that evening, about ten o’clock, in the
private bar of The Old Bull and Bush, where
this lady was then employed. Will you kindly
give us your version of what occurred tlhere? ”
I looked again at the somewhat crumpled lady
in the chair, hesitated for one brief second, and
cried in joyful amazement, “ Lottie ! ”
She looked up at me with tear-stained eyes and
gave me a pathetic, friendly little nod. “ Hello,
Kid,” she faltered.
Mrs. Dodgewick stiffened audibly. (The snaky
swish of her garments was the hiss of Virtue at
Vice.)
“Well, Mr. Johnson?”
I dragged myself together. “So far as I can
remember, I met Mr. Dodgewick that night some-
where about 10 o’clock. I generally used to go in
there for a drink on my way home. I think Mr.
Dodgewick was there when I came in.”
“ What was he doing?”
“My recollection is not very clear, but I think
he was talking to Miss Latitude here.”
“Yes, yes. Was his manner affectionate to-
wards her ? ”
(Dodgewick shuffled nervously behind me.)
“ His manner was friendly.”
(Dodgewick sighed with relief.)
“Was his manner only friendly all the time?”
“Well ”
“Yes, Mr. Johnson?”
“ Well, I would rather not ”
Dodgewick leaped to his feet and cried “Stop!”
The solicitor leaped to his feet and demanded,
“ Why should we stop, sir ? ”
(Continued on next par/e.)

PEARS
Matchless for the Complexion.
“Because, Mr. Gadgesnip, I want to have a
word or two in private with Mr. Johnson before
he goes any further."
The maddened solicitor banged the table with
his fist, and shouted, “It’s—it’s highly improper,
and—and I won’t allow it, and my name isn't
Gadgesnip or Gadgesnap—or Gadge —anything
else ! It’s Gadgestack, sir ! G-a-d-g-e-s-t-a-c-k !
Remember that!”
Dodgewick strode over to the table and banged
it in exactly the same place as the solicitor had.
“ I’m going to have a word with my friend John-
son, and I don’t care a d —n —no, nor half a d —n !
—what you or any other pettifogging fool says !
As for your name, Mr. Gadgepot, I’ll call you any-
thing I like or I’ll call you nothing at all. While
we’re on the subject, I may as well tell you that I
think your name is an absurd one. I think that a
man with a name like yours ”
The solicitor, white with fury, put up an ad-
monishing hand. “That is sufficient, Mr. Dodge-
wick ! No vulgar abuse if you please !”
“Can I have a word with my friend, then !”
“You may,” sneered the lawyer, sitting down.
Dodgewick made toward the door and beckoned
me to follow. We walked up to the end of the
dimly-lit passage, and there in the dusty shadows
Dodgewick seized my arm and whispered hoarsely
that I’d have to see him through.
I said, “Of course! Ain’t I seeing you
through now?”
“No,” hissed Dodgewick, passionately, “I mean
you must see me right through ! Johnson, this is
no time for any paltry fiddling help ! This calls
for a sacrifice, Johnson! It calls for a big sacri-
fice ! ”
I answered him in a cool, collected way. I said,
“Well, if you can’t get out of it any other way, I
suppose you’ll have to make some sort of a sacri-
fice.”
There was a pause, and then he reeled, and
gasped, and clutched me in an excess of horror.
When at last he spoke, his voice sounded like a
man soliloquising in a cave. “My God! John-
son, surely you don’t expect me to make the sacri-
fice ! .. , My God ! .. . Johnson, don’t trifle with
me ! This is no time for trifling ! ”
“ But ”
“Don’t attempt to joke about it, Johnson !
Remember what I’ve done for you ! You must help
me, man ! You owe it to mts ! ”
Owed it to him! My brain stopped at the very
thought. Good lord ! if there was one man on
earth of whose debt I was free that man was
Dodgewick. How on earth could I owe things to
Dodgewick ? How could anyone owe Dodgewick
anything? Hadn’t I given him half my income,
and three-quarters of my time, and all my
thought ? What more did Dodgewick want ?
And intuition whispered, “ Your reputation ! ”
I broke from his grasp on the instant, crying,
“ No, I won’t !”
But he seized me as I turned to fly, and implored
me to save him. He held me firmly by the arm,
in the dark corridor, and begged and prayed me to
do my best for him, until at last he hypnotised me
into a weary acquiescence.
He made as if to drag me back with him, but I
cried in anguish, “Hold on! What am I going
to tell them now l Tell me what I’ve got to say ! ”
“Oh, say that Lottie’s your friend, and that
whatever spying sneak it was that told the tales
has been mixing us up ! ”
“No ! ” I yelled, “ I won’t do it ! I want my
reputation. I haven’t got much, but”
“ Your reputation !” he hissed. “Yourrepu-
tation ! D—n your reputation ! What the Devil’s
the good of a reputation to you ? What use have
you got for it ? What use has any single man got
for a reputation ? I’m married, and I’ve got to
look out for this sort of thing, but you’re free !
Good Heavens, Johnson ! I thought you at least
were above talking tripe about reputations. A
single man has no more use for a reputation than
ne has for a parasol. Leave the reputations to
the women, my boy—they’ve got some use for the
d—n things. If ever you see a woman drop her
brolly or her reputation, help the poor girl to
pick it up—but for Heaven’s sake don’t try and
sport one yourself! They’re women’s toys,
Johnson, and they suit a man just about as well
as a picture hat would ! ”
“Nevertheless,” I answered.
“Nevertheless be d—d ! Now come along in
and square this thing up! . . . No, it’s no use
talking rot —you’ve simply got to do this forme,
Johnson! Don’t funk it, man. Pull yourself
together ! Remember you owe it to me! ”
He dragged me, protesting, to the door, and
pushed me into the room, saying in a loud voice
as he did so, “No, Johnson, no ! I cannot screen
you any longer ! ”
“Screen me!” I shouted, turning on him.
“ I don’t want you to screen me! ”
I never realised until that moment what a great
actor had been lost to the stage in Dodgewick.
He fairly blazed with virtuous indignation.
Pointing to a chair, he said in his sternest tones,
“ Well, if you don’t want me to screen you any
longer, sit down and tell these people what you’ve
just been telling me ! ”
But I revolted. I wasn’t going to sit there and
put a firestick to my reputation to save twenty
Dodgewicks. I folded my arms and said in a loud
voice that I’d be d—d if I would.
“Then Til tell them!” he shouted, indig-
nantly, and he commenced forthwith.
I had always known that Dodgewick was a liar,
but as soon as he was fairly started, I recognised
that he was an inspired one. With burning elo-
quence and passionate reproaches he explained
how it was he’d been mistaken for me. (I can’t
remember how he did it, but it sounded quite
feasible while he was talking.) He told, with
sobs, how I had borrowed large sums of money
from him, and how he had invoked the aid of Miss
Latitude —for whom I had a very great regard—
to save me from ruin.
At this stage I turned and begged Lottie
to tell the lawyer and Mrs. Dodgewick that
Dodgewick was telling lies. I nearly fell
off my chair with astonishment and rage
when, instead of supporting me, she gazed
reproachfully at me and faltered, “Oh, Kid, do
let us be honest with them ! We have nothing to
be ashamed of, dear ! Let Mr. Dodgewick tell
Mr. Gadgestack the whole story, and then per-
haps Mrs. Dodgewick will be satisfied that there
is nothing in these horrid tales ! ”
Before I could get my breath, the lawyer cast a
contemptuous look in my direction and said, “I
think Miss Latitude’s suggestion is a very wise
one, and that you had better let Mr. Dodgewick
tell’us the whole truth.”
I opened my mouth to reply, but Mrs. Dodge-
wick burst into tears and wailed that “ she knew
all the time I was keeping something back.”
I would have given all the wealth I pos-
sessed in the world and whatever prospects I
had in the next to have been allowed to shout
out the true facts of the case. But they wouldn’t
have listened to me. At that stage of the pro-
ceedings, and in that atmosphere of outraged
virtue, the true facts would have appeared merely
as a tissue of fantastic lies. Some such thought
struck me even as I reeled under the blow. I
gave one despairing look at Dodgewick, swollen
with wrath and indignation, and resigned myself
to the inevitable. From that on I let him say
what he liked, without interruption of any kind ;
in fact, I fell into a sort of waking trance, in
which Dodgewick was merely a remote noise. I
knew that he was tearing my character to shreds
and scattering it to the four winds, but was it not
a useless encumbrance? Hadn’t he told me so
himself ? Even supposing the thing was of any
use, what right had I to object if Dodgewick de-
cided its sacrifice was necessary ? What was my
mission in life? Wasn’t it to serve Dodgewick?
What use was it fighting against Fate ? None, of
course ! Then, of course, for me Dodgewick was
Fate ; so it was no use fighting against Dodgewick.
He annihilated opposition ; he defied the laws of
financial gravity, and borrowed money from all
sorts of impossible people. Virtue shrank to one
side whenever he passed, and Truth fled before
him. What use was it resisting ? None what-
ever ! I decided not to struggle any more, and
sank like a stone in a sea of thought.
* * * *
When I awoke the lawyer was clearing his
throat and Mrs. Dodgewick was alternately sob-
bing and exclaiming: “Oh, I never could have
believed it ! I never— never could have believed
it! ”
I jumped to my feet and shouted, desperately,
“ Well, don’t believe it ! It’s all lies ! ”
She dabbed her sodden wisp of handkerchief to
her eyes and wailed, “Oh, how dare you address
me ! ”
Dodgewick, from behind, commanded me to
“Sit down !” and the lawyer remarked furiously
that I was “ going on like a madman.”
Lottie looked at me with beseeching eyes, and
said. “Oh, Kid, do try and control yourself.”
“ Control myself !” I yelled. “Why should I
control myself?”
“ Because,” shouted the lawyer, pounding the
desk, “ because you’re supposed to be a civilised
gentleman, and not a savage. Bad as your con-
duct has been in the past, you are making things
a thousand times worse by your insulting conduct.
Remember where you are, sir ! Remember where
you are ! ”
Dodgewick said, in a voice like a bull, “Sit
down, Johnson ! ”
I sat down.
Then they resumed, the lawyer leading off.
“Mrs. Dodgewick, am I correct in assuming
that you regard Mr. Dodgewick’s explanation as
satisfactory ? ”
“ Oh, yes—yes ! I was a wicked, foolish woman
ever to doubt him ! ”
“Ah-h!” the solicitor sighed with evident
relief. “Then I take it that the matter is to be
allowed to drop here ? ”
“Yes, indeed !. . . Oh ! William !” (Sobs.)
Dodgewick shuffled his feet and made no reply.
Mrs. Dodgewick rose slowly, still sobbing, and
requested to be taken home at once. Dodgewick
—without a word or a look—brushed past me and
offered her his arm. Distraught as I was, I
noticed that he did it awkwardly. His pose was
angular and uncertain, and had no suggestion of
ease or grace about it. Probably he had not given
her his arm in many years. They trailed out
together, looking miserable enough, and the lawyer
held the door and stared after them with an odd
expression.
Then there was silence for a minute while he
came back to his desk and fiddled with piles of
papers. I was staring moodily at his white
nervous hands moving restlessly amongst the
tape-bound documents, and wondering what poor
devils were paying for them, when a miserable
damp sniff in the corner reminded me of Lottie.
I roused myself and remarked in a lifeless sort
of way, “ May I get a cab for you ? ”
She stood up, sniffed again, and pulled down
her veil over her face. Then I offered her my
arm and we went out, the lawyer again holding
the door open. Half-way down the passage a
sudden thought struck me, and I looked back over
my shoulder. He was regarding us with the same
inscrutable look. It had a shade of malignancy
in it.
Outside we found a cab. “Found” is the
proper expression, because we didn’t “ call” it—it
wasn’t the sort of cab anyone would call. The
horse was an utter ruin, and the vehicle itself,
under ordinary circumstances, would have been
preposterous. Just then it suited our mood, and
we climbed in and told the driver—who was an
evil-looking man with red hair and a crooked eye
—to drive to Angelotanko’s oyster saloon. He
unwound the reins and jabbed the ruin with his
whip, and the thing woke up and staggered away
in front of us.
Then I asked Lottie what she meant by her
conduct, and she laid her hand on my arm and
said, “Oh ! Kid, we had to save him, you know !
We couldn’t sit there and let that jealous wretch
of a wife of his ruin the poor boy’s life, could we?
Think of all he's done for us !” .
I was too limp to get in a rage, or curse, or be
astonished. I just sighed, “ What has he done
for us?”
Her answer was an essentially feminine one.
She replied, “Well Kid, I’m sure he always took
the greatest interest in you , and he used to give
me —oh, such lots of good advice ! Then, too, he
was always so kind and sympathetic ! I never met
such a sympathetic man in all my life —and I’ve
been in some of the best hotels in Australia.”
I let it go at that. Outside, the decayed horse
fell about in the shafts in an aimless, senile
fashion, and wagged his ears at the traffic. The
mannerism irritated me and I was on the point
of complaining to the cabman about it when an
enormous constable seized the absurd thing by the
head, and made it stop rocking itself to sleep, or
whatever it was doing. The evil-looking man
with the red hair got in a most fearful passion,
and he and the policeman engaged in a long,
stupid argument. I stood it as long as I could,
and then I helped Lottie to get out, and we made
through the crowd and walked down the street to
Angelotanko’s.
We went upstairs, and I ordered oysters for two.
When they came, we set-to and ate them in per-
fect silence —at least, I ate in silence. Every now
and then Lottie sobbed, and told me that I was
“a dear, good Kid” for making such a sacrifice,
but that I must remember all that Willie (she
called him Willie) had done for me, and that I
owed it to him !
I said nothing, but, when my oysters were
eaten, and the twelve shells lay empty and
desolate on my plate, I threw my serviette on the
dirty floor and stared up at the fly-spotted ceiling,
that hadn’t been painted since God-knows-when.
In the stillness, I imagined I heard a faint echo
of some distant commotion, and the whim took me
to go to the window and look out.
Far down the street, under the big are lamps, was
a small, black crowd. In the centre was a cab and
a horse, and close by a red-headed man was dancing
with a policeman, and pointing excitedly at an
oyster-shop I never go to on the opposite side of
the street.
I thought there must be a fire, or that they were
both mad, until I recognised the red-headed man
as the driver.
Then I remembered I hadn’t paid him.

EXTRACT FROM “SYDNEY MORNING HERALD,’’
OCTOBER 1, 1908:
“ The Minister for Lands stated in the Legislative
Assembly last evening- that experiments for the
eradication of prickly pear had been recently carried
out on the Scone temporary common under the
supervision of the Government Botanist. The ex-
periments included spraying by means of a Tyree
machine with arsenical solution of varying strengths
on different plots of land in the same locality. The
solutions were also injected into the top parts of the
plants and into the bulbs below ground. Where the
bulbs were treated it was found that the plants died.”
We supply Sprays of all sizes and Tyree Eradi-
cator for destroying all kinds of vegetation.
